The Logical Fallacy in Identifying Existence with the Necessary
Logical critique of an argument for identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a second figure syllogism with two affirmative premises cannot prove that existence is identical to the Necessary.

Core Idea
Shared attributes do not entail identity without logical equivalence.

This passage addresses a common but flawed argument in kalam that tries to identify existence with the Necessary Being by pointing out they share certain attributes. The author, presenting a logical critique, exposes both a premise weakness and a formal logical error. Before diving into the source, consider: can two things be proven identical just because they have the same descriptions?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms like 'شكل ثاني' and 'موجبتين'.
These logical terms are central to understanding the critique.
2
Identify the two critiques
The author first notes weakness in some premises, then points out a formal logical flaw: two affirmative premises in the second figure yield no conclusion. Find both in the text.
The second critique is the core of the passage.
3
Understand the verification (tahqiq)
The author explains that even if attributes are necessary concomitants of existence, this does not prove existence is the Necessary unless those attributes are equivalent to the entailed thing. Grasp this deeper point.
This is a subtle point about the direction of entailment.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this logical distinction matters for arguments about God's attributes. How does it prevent hasty identifications?
This connects to broader debates in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي بعض المقدمات ضعف لا يخفى، ولو سلم فغاية الأمر اتصاف كل من الوجود الواجب بهذه المعاني، ولا إنتاج عن الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني . وتحقيقه أن لزوم هذه الأمور للوجود لا يوجب كونه الواجب ما لم تتبين مساواتها للملزوم.
And in some of the premises there is an obvious weakness. Even if it were granted, the utmost result is that each of existence and the Necessary is described by these meanings, and there is no conclusion from two affirmative premises in the second figure. Its verification is that the entailment of these matters for existence does not necessitate that it is the Necessary unless their equivalence with the entailed thing is made clear.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is a logical critique. The author points out that the premises of the argument that identify existence with the Necessary are weak (da'f). Even if conceded, the argument only shows that both existence and the Necessary share certain attributes (like being pure existence, pure good, simple, etc.). This is a second figure (al-shakl al-thani) syllogism with two affirmative premises (mu'jibatayn), which yields no valid conclusion (la intaj). The author then provides the verification (tahqiq): the fact that these attributes are necessary concomitants (lawazim) of existence does not prove that existence is the Necessary unless it is shown that these attributes are equivalent (musawat) to the entailed thing (i.e., the Necessary's essence). In other words, the argument lacks the converse: even if the Necessary has these attributes, other things might also share them. This is a standard critique of such identification.

Key Terms
مقدمات
إنتاج
شكل ثاني
موجبتين
لزوم
مساواة
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence has all the attributes of the Necessary, then existence must be the Necessary.
Correction: Shared attributes do not prove identity; one must also show that those attributes are unique to the Necessary (i.e., equivalent to its essence).

Checkpoint
What logical flaw does the author identify in the argument?
A) It uses an invalid mood in the first figure
B) It has two affirmative premises in the second figure, which yields no conclusion
C) It confuses the particular with the universal
D) It assumes what it needs to prove

Key Takeaway
The argument fails because even if existence and the Necessary share attributes, without proving those attributes are unique to the Necessary, no identity conclusion follows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been quick to conclude that two things are identical because they share some attributes? How might this logical error affect your understanding of Allah?
?
This passage shows that even well-intentioned theological arguments can fail. Does your trust in Allah depend on the strength of human arguments? If so, what happens when those arguments are shown to be weak?
?
The author demands equivalence, not just entailment. In your own beliefs, are there areas where you have assumed one thing necessarily leads to another without establishing a two-way relationship? How can you correct that?

The Term 'Wujud': Shared, Univocal, or Graduated?
Sanusi presents three views on the term 'wujud' and endorses one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three competing views on the term 'wujud' and why the graduated view is considered correct in the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
The word 'existence' is one in meaning but not applied equally to all things.

In Islamic theology, the term 'wujud' (existence) is central. But is it used in the same way for God and creatures? Scholars disagreed, with three main positions. This passage from Sanusi explains each and declares the correct view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully along with its literal translation. Notice the flow: the author first references a previous disagreement, then introduces the three views about the word 'wujud'.
The passage is short but dense—take your time.
2
Identify the three positions
Look for the three terms: mushtarak (shared), mutawati' (univocal), and mushakkik (graduated). Understand the basic claim of each: does 'wujud' have multiple meanings, one uniform meaning, or one meaning with variation?
3
Understand the nuance of mushakkik
The graduated view says 'wujud' has a single concept (being) but applies not equally. This allows for differences in intensity or priority, e.g., God's existence is more necessary than a creature's.
This is the author's chosen view.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Think about the theological stakes: if 'wujud' were merely shared (different meanings), then saying 'God exists' and 'a stone exists' would be unrelated. If univocal, they'd be exactly the same. The graduated view preserves both unity and distinction.
This connects to how we speak about God and creation.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to see if you can identify the correct view and explain why the others are rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها اختلافهم في أن الوجودات الخاصة نفس الماهيات أو زائدة عليها كما سبق . ومنها اختلافهم في أن لفظ الوجود مشترك بين مفهومات مختلفة على ما نقل عن الأشعري، أو متواطىء يقع على الوجودات بمعنى واحد لا تفاوت فيه أصلاً ، أو مشكك يقع عليها بمعنى واحد هو مفهوم الكون لكن لا على السواء. وهو الحق . وهي متباينة.
And among them is their disagreement over whether particular existences are the essences themselves or additional to them, as earlier. And among them is their disagreement over whether the word 'existence' is shared (mushtarak) among different concepts as transmitted from al-Ash'ari, or univocal (mutawati') applying to existences in one sense with no variation at all, or graduated (mushakkik) applying to them in one sense — the concept of 'being' — but not equally. And this is the truth. And they are distinct.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage lists further disagreements regarding existence. First, it mentions the disagreement about whether particular existences are identical to the essences or additional — a topic already covered. Then it focuses on the linguistic disagreement about the word 'wujud'. Three positions are given: (1) 'wujud' is mushtarak (shared), meaning it has different meanings for different things, as attributed to al-Ash'ari; (2) it is mutawati' (univocal), applying to all existences in exactly the same sense; (3) it is mushakkik (graduated), applying in one sense (the concept of being) but with varying degrees of intensity or priority. The author declares the third view to be the truth. The final phrase 'وهي متباينة' (and they are distinct) may refer to the existences themselves being distinct despite the single concept.

Key Terms
لفظ الوجود
مشترك
متواطىء
مشكك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'wujud' is used in exactly the same way for God and creatures (univocal).
Correction: The graduated view allows for difference in intensity: God's existence is more necessary and perfect than a creature's.

Checkpoint
What is the correct view about the term 'wujud' according to the author?
A) It is shared (mushtarak).
B) It is univocal (mutawati').
C) It is graduated (mushakkik).
D) It is equivocal.

Key Takeaway
The graduated view (mushakkik) is the correct position: 'wujud' means one concept but applies differently to different existences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God exists' and 'this pen exists,' do you truly sense the monumental difference in the meaning of existence? If not, what does that reveal about your awareness of God's reality?
?
The graduated view teaches that existence is one concept but with different intensities. How does your daily life reflect the fact that God's existence is infinitely more real and present than your own? What would change if you lived with that certainty?
?
If you affirm that God's existence is necessary while yours is contingent, does your prayer life and reliance on Him demonstrate that asymmetry? In what specific area do you most need to realign your heart with this truth?

Objection and Answer on Mental Images
Refutes an objection that understanding mental images is tautological.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that mental images are tautological fails and how they function as the basis for judgment about external realities.

Core Idea
Mental images are not passive copies; they enable active judgment about the external world.

This passage tackles a subtle philosophical objection: if understanding something means having its image in the mind, then saying 'I understand' seems to be a meaningless repetition. The author refutes this by showing that mental images are not the end goal but the medium through which we make judgments about external entities. Let's examine the objection and its precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection equates 'understanding' with 'obtaining an image,' leading to a tautology.
The objector thinks that if understanding is just obtaining an image, then the statement is circular.
2
Identify the author's answer
Find where the author responds ('قلنا'). He redefines the role of mental images: they are the means through which we form judgments about external things.
The answer clarifies that obtaining the image is not the end; it's the starting point for judgment.
3
Reflect on the function of mental images
Consider how this answer preserves the usefulness of mental images. They are not just mental copies but tools for interacting with reality.
This connects to broader themes in epistemology and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين الصور الذهنية والأعيان الخارجية، لا معنى لفهمها والعلم بها سوى حصول صورها، كان بمنزلة أن يقال : يحصل من حصول الصور حصول الصور. قلنا : المراد أنه إذا حكم على الأشياء كان الحاصل في الذهن هو الصور، ويحصل منها الحكم على الأعيان الخارجية.
Between mental images and external entities, there is no meaning to understanding them and knowing them except the obtaining of their images. This is tantamount to saying: from the obtaining of images, the obtaining of images occurs. We said: The meaning is that when one judges about things, what is obtained in the mind is the images, and from them the judgment about external entities is obtained.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding mental images (al-ṣuwar al-dhihniyya). The objector argues that if understanding something is simply the obtaining of its image in the mind, then saying 'I understand' is equivalent to saying 'the image is obtained from the obtaining of the image' — a tautology. The answer clarifies that the purpose is not mere obtaining of images; rather, when one makes a judgment (ḥukm) about things, the mental images are the means by which the judgment applies to external entities. Thus, understanding involves not just passive reception but active judgment about external reality via those images.

Key Terms
الصور الذهنية
الأعيان الخارجية
حصول الصور
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental images are merely copies with no role in judgment; understanding is just having the image.
Correction: Mental images are the basis for making judgments about external entities, so they play an active role in knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the author's reply, what is the purpose of mental images when making a judgment?
A) They are the final object of knowledge.
B) They enable the judgment to be applied to external entities.
C) They are identical to external entities.
D) They replace external entities entirely.

Key Takeaway
Mental images are not the endpoint of understanding but the medium through which we form judgments about external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you form an opinion about someone, do you ever pause to consider that your judgment is based on a mental image, not the person's full reality? How can this awareness prevent you from being unjust in your judgments?
?
How often do you mistake your mental pictures for absolute truth? What will you do today to remind yourself that your images are only a medium, and that the real truth lies beyond them?
?
If understanding is not just passively receiving images but actively judging, are you passive or active in your understanding of your faith? What concrete step will you take to move from passive reception to active judgment about the truths of revelation?

Words and Mental Images: The Second Objection and Answer
Objection and answer defending mental images as word meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that mental images serve as necessary intermediaries between words and external objects, and how the author defends this using self-evident reason.

Core Idea
Words point to mental images, which in turn point to external things.

This passage presents a second objection against the theory that words denote mental images. The objector claims words directly denote external objects. The author responds by showing that mental images are self-evident to reason and serve as efficient intermediaries. You will see how the argument unfolds and why the author considers mental images essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the initial objection that words are placed for external entities.
Notice the objector's certainty: 'we are certain that words are only placed in correspondence with what we conceive of external entities.'
2
Identify the author's reply
The author bases his answer on the self-evidence of mental images (بديهة العقل). Observe how he counters the objection.
The reply begins with 'قلنا: مبني هذا الكلام على إثبات الصور الذهنية'.
3
Understand the chain of signification
The passage describes a three-level chain: word → mental image → external object. Grasp how each level signifies the next.
The external is the signified of the image, and the image is the signified of the word.
4
Appreciate the wisdom of efficiency
The author explains the practical benefit: preserving multiple images through letter combinations saves effort.
This is the 'قلة المؤونة' (ease of effort).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: نحن قاطعون بأن المواضع إنما عين الألفاظ بإزاء ما نعقله من الأعيان، وللدلالة عليها ولهذا يقول بالوضع والدلالة من لا يقول بالصور الذهنية في الخارج. فإن قيل: نحن نعم إذا لم يكن للمعقول وجود في الخارج، كان المدلول هو نفس الصورة عند من يقول بها كالمعدوم والمستحيل. قلنا: مبني هذا الكلام على إثبات الصور الذهنية، فإنه مما يكاد يقضي به بديهة العقل. ولما كان عند سماع اللفظ ترتسم الصورة في النفس، فيعلم ثبوت الحكم لما في الخارج، جعلوا الخارج مدلول الصورة والصورة مدلول اللفظ، وأما كون مدلول الخط هو اللفظ فظاهر، والحكمة فيه قلة المؤونة، حيث اكتفى بحفظ صور متعددة تترتب بترتب الحروف في الألفاظ، من غير احتياج إلى أن يحفظ لكل معنى صورة مخصوصة.
If it is said: We are certain that words are only placed in correspondence with what we conceive of external entities, and for denoting them, and for this reason one who does not affirm mental images in the external speaks of convention and signification. If it is said: We accept; if the conceived has no existence in the external, then the signified is the very image according to those who affirm it, like the non-existent and the impossible. We said: This statement is based on the affirmation of mental images, for it is something that the natural intellect almost necessarily judges. And since upon hearing the word, the image is impressed in the soul, and thus the establishment of the judgment for what is in the external is known, they made the external the signified of the image, and the image the signified of the word. As for the fact that the signified of writing is the word, that is apparent. The wisdom in this is the ease of effort, since one suffices with preserving multiple images that are arranged according to the arrangement of letters in words, without needing to preserve a specific image for each meaning.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage contains a second objection and its answer. The objector argues that words are directly placed to denote external things (aʿyān), and even those who deny mental images still accept conventional signification. Then a further objection: if the conceived has no external existence, its meaning is just the mental image (like non-existent or impossible things). The author replies that this whole discussion presupposes the existence of mental images (al-ṣuwar al-dhihniyya), which is self-evident to reason. When a word is heard, a mental image is formed in the soul, and through that image we know the judgment applies to external things. Thus, the external entity is the signified of the image, and the image is the signified of the word. Writing directly signifies the word. The advantage is efficiency: we can store many image-concepts by combining letters, rather than a unique image for each meaning.

Key Terms
الوضع والدلالة
المدلول
الصورة الذهنية
بديهة العقل
قلة المؤونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think words directly denote external objects without any mental intermediary.
Correction: The author argues that words first denote mental images, which then denote external objects. The mental image is a necessary intermediary, affirmed by self-evident reason.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the role of the mental image when a word is heard?
A) It is the same as the external object.
B) It is impressed in the soul and enables knowledge of the judgment about the external.
C) It is the word itself.
D) It is unnecessary if the object exists externally.

Key Takeaway
Mental images are necessary intermediaries between words and external objects, affirmed by self-evident reason and enabling efficient communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's names, do you consciously let the words form a mental image in your soul that then points to the divine reality, or do you rush through the sounds without that inner picture? What might you be missing by skipping the image?
?
The author says the existence of mental images is 'self-evident to reason.' Yet many of us live as if our words directly grab the external world. In a conflict, have you ever mistaken your mental image of someone's intentions for the truth? How can remembering the chain protect you from false assumptions?
?
You rely on mental images every time you think about the Unseen—Paradise, Hell, the Day of Judgment. Are your images of these realities shaped more by the Qur'an and Sunnah, or by whispers and worldly distractions? What is one image you can correct today to align with revelation?

Refuting the Forms Objection to Mental Existence
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that concepts exist as separate forms or in Active Intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the objection that concepts could exist as separate forms or in the Active Intellect, thereby establishing mental existence for all intelligibles.

Core Idea
Even if concepts exist elsewhere, intellection requires mental representation.

Al-Taftazani has argued that mental existence is necessary for non-existent things. But an objector counters: why not say concepts exist as Platonic forms or in the Active Intellect? In this passage, al-Taftazani shows that even if that were the case, the act of intellection still requires the form to be present in the mind. This lesson walks through that crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the section starting with 'فإن قيل' to understand what the objector claims. The objector says that non-existent things could be forms subsisting by themselves, like Platonic forms, or in the Active Intellect.
Note the three possibilities: separate forms, suspended forms, and forms in the Active Intellect.
2
Analyze the response
Now read al-Taftazani's reply starting with 'قلنا'. Notice how he argues that even if forms are in the Active Intellect, intellection requires the form to be obtained in the mind, which is exactly mental existence.
Pay attention to the phrase 'وهو يستلزم المطلوب' – this is the key point.
3
Compare with al-Mawaqif's view
Al-Taftazani mentions the author of al-Mawaqif's argument that even if forms are impressed in the Active Intellect, that proves mental existence because it shows a distinction in the mind. Consider why al-Taftazani finds this view not fully satisfying.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
The final point is that the very act of intellection involves obtaining the form in the intellect, so mental existence is unavoidable. Think about how this strengthens the overall argument for mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو العاقل والمعقول، سواء كان العلم عبارة عن حصول صورة الشيء في العقل عن إضافة مخصوصة بين العاقل والمعقول أو عن صفة ذات إضافة (١) ، والتعلق بين العاقل وبين العدم الصرف محال بالضرورة. فلا بد للمعقول من ثبوت في الجملة ولما امتنع ثبوت الكليات بل سائر المعدومات سيما الممتنعات في الخارج تعين كونه في الذهن .

فإن قيل : في رد هذا الجواب أن المعقولات التي لا وجود لها في الخارج لا يلزم أن تكون موجودة في الذهن لجواز أن تكون صوراً (۲) قائمة بأنفسها، كالمثل المجردة الأفلاطونية (۳) على ما سيأتي. في بحث الماهية، وكالمثل المعلقة التي يقول بها بعض الحكماء، زعما منهم . لكل موجود شبحاً في عالم المثال. ليس بمعقول ولا محسوس على ما سيأتي في آخر المقصد . الرابع : أو قائمة ببعض المجردات كما تدعيه الفلاسفة من ارتسام صور الكائنات في العقل الفعال، وينبغي أن يكون هذا مراد الإمام بالأجرام الغائبة عنا، وإلا فقيام المعدومات بالأجسام مما لا يعقل .

قلنا : الكلام في المعدومات سيما الممتنعات ولا خفاء في امتناع قيامها بأنفسها بحسب الخارج، ولا بالعقل الفعال بهوياتها، إذ لا هوية للممتنع بل غاية الأمر أن يقوم به تصورها بمعنى تعقله إياها، وهو يستلزم المطلوب من جهة استلزامه كون التعقل بحصول الصورة في العاقل فترتسم الصورة في القوة العاقلة. وهو المعنى الموجود الذهني، ثم إذا كان طريق التعقل واحداً كان تعقل الموجودات أيضاً بحصول صورها في العقل . وذكر صاحب المواقف : أن المرتسم في العقل الفعال إن كان الصور والماهيات الكلية يثبت الوجود الذهني، إذ غرضنا إثبات نوع من التميز للمعقولات غير التميز بالهوية الذي نسميه بالوجود الخارجي، سواء اخترع العقل تلك الصور أو لاحظها من موضوع آخر، كالعقل الفعال وغيره، وفيه نظر لأن غاية ذلك أن يكون للمعقولات تميز عند العقل بالصورة والماهية ، لكن كون ذلك بحصول الصورة في العقل هو أول المسألة .
Or (the relationship between) the intellect and the intelligible, whether knowledge is defined as the obtaining of the form of the thing in the intellect due to a specific relation between the intellect and the intelligible, or as an attribute with a relation (1), and the connection between the intellect and pure non-existence is necessarily impossible. So the intelligible must have some sort of subsistence. And since it is impossible for universals—indeed all non-existent things, especially the impossibles—to subsist externally, it is determined that they exist in the mind.

If it is said: In refutation of this answer, the intelligibles that have no existence externally do not necessarily have to exist in the mind, because it is possible that they are forms (2) subsisting by themselves, like the Platonic separate forms (3) as will come later in the discussion of quiddity, and like the suspended forms which some philosophers affirm, claiming that every existent has a specter in the world of example, which is neither intelligible nor sensible, as will come at the end of the fourth section; or they may subsist by some abstract entities as claimed by the philosophers regarding the impression of the forms of generated things in the Active Intellect, and this should be what the Imam meant by bodies absent from us; otherwise, the subsistence of non-existents in bodies is inconceivable.

We say: The discussion concerns non-existents, especially impossibles, and it is clear that it is impossible for them to subsist by themselves externally, nor by the Active Intellect in their identities, because the impossible has no identity. Rather, the utmost is that it (the impossible) can be represented in it (the Active Intellect) in the sense of its (the Active Intellect's) intellection of them, and this entails the desired point in that it entails that intellection occurs through the obtaining of the form in the intellect, so the form is impressed in the rational faculty. And that is the meaning of mental existence. Then, if the mode of intellection is one, then the intellection of existent things is also through the obtaining of their forms in the intellect. And the author of al-Mawaqif mentioned that what is impressed in the Active Intellect, if it is the forms and universal quiddities, it establishes mental existence, because our purpose is to prove a kind of distinction for intelligibles other than the distinction by identity which we call external existence, whether the intellect invents those forms or observes them from another subject like the Active Intellect and others. But there is a consideration: the utmost of that is that intelligibles have a distinction in the intellect by form and quiddity, but that this occurs through the obtaining of the form in the intellect is the very first point of the issue.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial objection and answer in the debate on mental existence. First, the author establishes that mental existence is necessary because non-existent things cannot exist externally. Then an objector raises that concepts might exist as Platonic forms or in the Active Intellect. The answer clarifies that even if they did, their representation in the intellect is still required. The author explains that the impossible has no identity, so it cannot subsist by itself; rather, intellection involves obtaining the form in the mind. Thus mental existence is established. The author also discusses the view of al-Mawaqif that even if forms are in the Active Intellect, the purpose of mental existence is proven. The answer concludes that the very point of the matter is that the form is obtained in the mind.

Key Terms

المثل الأفلاطونية
العقل الفعال
الصورة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Concepts can have real existence outside the mind as separate Platonic forms or in the Active Intellect.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that such subsistence is impossible for non-existents, and even if representation occurs, it still involves the form being in the mind, which is mental existence.

Key Takeaway
Mental existence is established because intellection always involves obtaining the form in the mind, regardless of any external subsistence.

?
If even impossibles must have mental representation, then your mind is constantly engaged with things that have no external reality. What are the 'impossibles' you worry about or imagine? How can the truth of this lesson free you from unnecessary mental burdens?

Refuting the Heat and Cold Objection: Form and Identity
Al-Taftazani refutes the heat and cold objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that mental existence would entail the mind being hot or cold by distinguishing between essential existence (al-wujūd al-muta’aṣṣil) and form (ṣūra).

Core Idea
The mind contains forms, not physical identities.

One common objection to the theory of mental existence is that if the mind contained the form of heat, it would literally become hot. Al-Taftazani rejects this by clarifying a crucial distinction: what exists in the mind is the form of a thing, not its essential identity. This passage explains that distinction with a clear analogy, showing that mental existence does not entail physical change.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the structure: first the opponents' argument, then the refutation.
The footnote markers (١), (٢), (٣) indicate key parts.
2
Identify the distinction between form and identity
Focus on the sentence: 'So what is hot is that in which the identity of heat subsists, not its form.' This is the core of the refutation.
Understand that 'identity' (huwiyya) means the essence or reality of a thing, while 'form' (ṣūra) is its mental representation.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider the analogy of water in a pitcher and the pitcher in a house. How does it illustrate the difference between essential existence and mental existence?
Think about how the water exists essentially in the pitcher, but the pitcher exists in the house only as an object located there.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question and complete the activity to solidify your grasp.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتصاف الذهن بالحرارة والبرودة بديهي الاستحالة

قال : تمسك المانعون (۱) بأن اتصاف الذهن بالحرارة والبرودة وحصول السموات فيه بديهي الاستحالة وبأنه لو وجد في الذهن ما لا تحقق له في الخارج لوجد فيه، لأن الموجود في الوجود في الشيء موجود فيه ورد (۲) بأن ذلك في الوجود المتأصل، فالحار ما يقوم به هوية الحرارة لا صورتها والمحال حصول هويات السموات في الذهن لا صورها (۳). والحاصل في الذهن صورة المعدوم، وفي الخارج هوية الذهن، بخلاف وجود الماء في الكوز والكوز في الدار.
The description of the mind with heat and cold is self-evidently impossible.

He said: The opponents (1) adhere to the argument that the description of the mind with heat and cold and the obtaining of the heavens in it are self-evidently impossible, and that if something existed in the mind that has no reality externally, then it would exist in it, because what exists in existence exists in the thing. And it is refuted (2) that this is in (the case of) essential existence. So what is hot is that in which the identity of heat subsists, not its form; and what is impossible is the obtaining of the identities of the heavens in the mind, not their forms (3). What is obtained in the mind is the form of the non-existing thing, while externally it is the identity of the mind, unlike the existence of water in the pitcher and the pitcher in the house.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage addresses another objection against mental existence. The opponents argue that if the mind contained the form of heat, it would become hot, which is absurd. The author responds by distinguishing between essential existence (الوجود المتأصل) and form. When we say something is hot, it means the identity of heat subsists in it. In the mind, only the form of heat is present, not the identity. Similarly, the heavens do not literally exist in the mind; only their forms do. The author illustrates with the analogy of water in a pitcher and the pitcher in a house: water exists essentially in the pitcher, but the pitcher exists in the house in a different way.

الصورة
الهوية

Misconception: Mental existence implies the mind takes on physical properties of the objects it thinks about.
Correction: The mind only contains the form (ṣūra) of an object, not its physical identity (huwiyya), so no physical change occurs.

Checkpoint

B) Because the mind only has the identity of heat.
C) Because the mind has the form of heat, not the identity.
D) Because heat is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Mental existence involves forms (ṣuwar) of things, not their essential identities, so it does not change the physical nature of the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Make a conscious effort today to detach from one such thought and see it as a form, not your identity.
?
If the form of a nonexistent thing can exist in your mind, then your fears and anxieties are often about things that are not real. How can you regularly remind yourself that the form in your mind is not the same as external reality? What specific step will you take the next time you are overwhelmed by a worst-case scenario?

Three Objections to Mental Existence and Their Refutation
Author enumerates three objections to mental existence and provides a unified response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main objections raised against the doctrine of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) and how the author refutes them by distinguishing between essential existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) and mental form (al-sura al-'aqliyya).

Core Idea
Mental existence is about forms, not identities—a key distinction that defuses all objections.

Before we can grasp the doctrine of mental existence, we must confront the strongest objections against it. Opponents argue that if mental existence were true, absurdities would follow: the mind would become hot, contain the heavens, and give external reality to non-existents. In this passage, the author presents these objections and then delivers a unified refutation based on a single crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponents' initial argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author summarizes the opponents’ reasoning: that if mental existence were admitted, then anything conceived would have to exist in the mind, leading to contradictions. Note the example of heat and cold.
The opponents assume that mental existence requires the identity of the thing to be present in the mind.
2
Examine each of the three objections
The author lists three specific objections: (1) conception of heat and cold would make the mind both hot and cold; (2) conceiving the heavens would put them in the mind; (3) conceiving non-existents would make them exist externally. For each, notice the common error the author will later identify.
All three objections share a misunderstanding.
3
Learn the author's unified answer
Read the answer starting from 'The answer: That the basis of all this is the failure to distinguish...'. Understand the key distinction between essential existence (identity) and mental existence (form, picture). See how it resolves each objection.
The answer also explains how mental forms differ from external identities in being universal and abstract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : تمسك المانعون بأن اتصاف الذهن بالحرارة والبرودة وحصول السموات فيه بديهي الاستحالة وبأنه لو وجد في الذهن ما لا تحقق له في الخارج لوجد فيه، لأن الموجود في الوجود في الشيء موجود فيه ورد بأن ذلك في الوجود المتأصل، فالحار ما يقوم به هوية الحرارة لا صورتها والمحال حصول هويات السموات في الذهن لا صورها. والحاصل في الذهن صورة المعدوم، وفي الخارج هوية الذهن، بخلاف وجود الماء في الكوز والكوز في الدار. لما كان مبنى الوجود الذهني على استلزام التعقل إياه. اقتصر المانعون على إبطال ذلك وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : لو كان تصور الشيء مستلزماً لحصوله في العقل، لزم من تصور الحرارة والبرودة أن يكون الذهن حاراً بارداً وهو محال، لما فيه من اجتماع الضدين، واتصاف العقل بما هو من خواص الأجسام . الثاني : أنه يلزم أن تحصل السموات بعضها في العقل عند تعقلها في الكل، وفي الخيال عند تخيلها، وهو باطل بالضرورة. الثالث: أنه يلزم من تعقل المعدومات وجودها في الخارج، لكونها موجودة في العقل الموجود في الخارج ، مع القطع بأن الموجود في الموجود في الشيء موجود في ذلك الشيء، كالماء الموجود في الكوز الموجود في البيت. والجواب : أن مبنى الكل على عدم التفرقة بين الوجود المتأصل الذي به الهوية العينية، وغير المتأصل الذي به الصورة العقلية فإن المتصف بالحرارة ما تقوم به هوية الحرارة لا صورتها، والتضاد إنما هو بين هويتي الحرارة والبرودة لا بين صورتيهما والذي علم بالضرورة استحالة حصوله في العقل والخيال هو هويات السموات لا صورها الكلية أو الجزئية، والموجود في الموجود في الشيء إنما يكون موجوداً في ذلك الشيء، إذا كان الوجودان متأصلين، ويكون الموجودان هويتين كوجود الماء في الكوز والكوز في البيت، بخلاف وجود المعدوم في الذهن الموجود في الخارج. فإن الحاصل في الذهن من المعدوم صورة، والوجود غير متأصل، ومن الذهن في الخارج هوية. والوجود متأصل . وبالجملة فماهية الشيء أعني صورته العقلية مخالفة لهويته العينية في كثير من اللوازم فإن الأولى : كلية ومجردة بخلاف الثانية، والثانية : مبدأ للإمكان بخلاف الأولى ومعنى المطابقة بينهما : أن الماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت تلك الهوية والهوية إذا جردت عن العوارض المشخصة واللواحق الغريبة، كانت تلك الماهية فلا يرد ما يقال إن الصورة العقلية إن ساوت الصورة الخارجية لزمت المحالات وإلا لم تكن صورة لها .
He said: The opponents hold that the mind's being qualified by heat and cold and the heavens' occurrence in it are unquestionably impossible, and that if something existed in the mind that has no realization in the external world, then it would exist in it [the external world] because that which exists in something that exists in a thing exists in that thing. And it was replied that this applies to essential existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil); so the hot is that which is subsistent by the identity (huyya) of heat, not its form, and what is impossible is the occurrence of the identities (huyyat) of the heavens in the mind, not their forms. What is obtained in the mind is the form of the non-existent, while in the external world it is the identity of the mind, unlike the existence of water in the jug and the jug in the house. Since the foundation of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni) is based on the implication of conception (al-ta'aqqul) for it, the opponents confined themselves to refuting that and establishing it by several points: First: If the conception of a thing necessarily involves its occurrence in the mind, then from the conception of heat and cold it would follow that the mind is hot and cold, which is impossible due to the combination of opposites and the mind being qualified by what is proper to bodies. Second: It would follow that the heavens, some of them, occur in the mind when they are conceived as a whole, and in the imagination when imagined, which is obviously false. Third: It would follow from the conception of non-existents that they exist in the external world, because they exist in the mind which exists in the external world, with the certainty that what exists in something that exists in a thing exists in that thing, like water existing in the jug existing in the house. The answer: That the basis of all this is the failure to distinguish between essential existence (al-wujud al-muta'asil) by which is the specific identity (al-huyya al-'ayniyya), and non-essential existence by which is the mental form (al-sura al-'aqliyya). For the qualified by heat is that by which the identity of heat subsists, not its form; opposition is only between the identities of heat and cold, not between their forms. What is known by necessity to be impossible to occur in the mind and imagination is the identities of the heavens, not their universal or particular forms. The existent in something that exists in a thing only exists in that thing if both existences are essential and the two existents are identities, like the existence of water in the jug and the jug in the house, unlike the existence of a non-existent in the mind that exists in the external world. For what is obtained in the mind from the non-existent is a form, and the existence is non-essential, while from the mind in the external world is an identity, and the existence is essential. In summary, the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing, i.e., its mental form, is different from its specific identity in many concomitants: for the first is universal and abstract unlike the second, and the second is the principle of possibility unlike the first. The meaning of correspondence (mutabaqa) between them is that when the quiddity exists externally, it is that identity, and when the identity is stripped of individuating accidents and extraneous attachments, it is that quiddity. Hence, it is not countered by the saying: 'If the mental form equals the external form, absurdities follow; otherwise, it would not be its form.'
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes three objections against the doctrine of mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), which holds that conceived objects exist in the mind as forms or quiddities, not as external realities. The author first summarizes the opponents' initial argument: the mind cannot be qualified by heat or cold, nor can the heavens occur in it, and if something existed in the mind without external realization, it would have to exist externally—a claim refuted by distinguishing between essential existence (wujud muta'asil) and mental existence. The core of the debate is whether conception implies the presence of the conceived object in the mind. The opponents then present three specific arguments. First, if conception required the object's presence in the mind, then conceiving heat and cold would make the mind hot and cold, combining opposites. Second, conceiving the heavens would require them to be present in the mind (and imagination), which is obviously false. Third, conceiving non-existents would force them to exist in the mind, which is part of the external world, so they would exist externally—an absurd conclusion. The author's unified answer is that all three objections confuse essential existence (which constitutes the thing's identity) with non-essential mental existence (which is merely the form or quiddity of the thing). The mind is qualified not by the identity of heat but by its form; opposition applies only to identities; the heavens' identities cannot be in the mind, but their forms can; and a non-existent in the mind exists as a form, not as an external reality. The passage concludes by explaining that the mental form (sura 'aqliyya) differs from the external identity (huyya 'ayniyya) in being universal and abstract, and that correspondence between them means that the same quiddity appears either as a concrete identity (with individuating accidents) or as an abstract form (when stripped of those accidents). This resolves the alleged absurdities about similarity between mental and external forms.

Key Terms
الوجود الذهني
الوجود المتأصل
الهوية العينية
الصورة العقلية
التضاد
الماهية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental existence means the actual identity of the object is in the mind.
Correction: The author clarifies that only the form (sura) of the object exists in the mind, not its identity (huyya).

Checkpoint
What is the main distinction the author uses to refute the three objections against mental existence?
A) Distinction between essential existence and mental existence
B) Distinction between subject and object
C) Distinction between actual and potential
D) Distinction between universal and particular

Key Takeaway
The key to understanding mental existence is distinguishing between the essential identity of a thing and its mental form or picture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Did you treat those thoughts as if they were real external forces battling inside you? How might remembering that they are only forms (not identities) free you from that inner conflict?
?
If mental existence is merely forms without identities, then what you 'know' about yourself—your self-image, your failures, your successes—are all forms in your mind. Are you willing to let go of any self-image that does not correspond to what you actually are in your essential identity before your Creator? What would it take to strip away those forms and see yourself as you truly are?

Existence is Synonymous with Establishedness
Al-Taftazani defines existence and non-existence and discusses their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defines existence, non-existence, and related terms, and the different positions on whether the non-existent can be established and whether there is an intermediate state.

Core Idea
Existence and establishedness are synonymous, but the non-existent can still be established.

This passage from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid lays the terminological groundwork for discussions of ontology. He begins by equating existence with establishedness and non-existence with negation, then presents varying views on whether there is an intermediate state between existence and non-existence. Understanding these definitions is crucial for following later debates about the reality of possibles and states.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the initial definitions
Find how al-Taftazani defines existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam). Note that he says they are synonymous with establishedness (thubut) and negation (nafy) respectively.
3
Map the threefold classification of the known
The passage presents a threefold division of the known (ma'lum) into non-existent, existent, and state (hal). Understand each category and the criterion used.
The state is an attribute of an existent, neither existent nor non-existent.
4
Compare the Mu'tazili view with another group's view
Al-Taftazani reports two additional positions: the majority Mu'tazila view and that of 'some of them'. Compare how they treat the non-existent and whether they allow an intermediate state.
Focus on the terms 'kaun fi al-a'yan' (being in external entities) and 'tahaqquq fi nafsih' (realization in itself).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع: الوجود يرادف الثبوت ويساوق الشيئية، والعدم يرادف النفي، فلا المعدوم ثابت ولا بينه وبين الموجود واسطة، وخوله في الأمرين إفراداً وجمعاً. فقيل: المعلوم إما لا ثبوت له، وهو المعدوم أو له ثبوت باعتبار ذاته وهو الموجود، أو تبعاً لغيره. وهو الحال، فهو صفة الموجود لا موجودة ولا معدومة فتتحقق الواسطة. وقال جمهور المعتزلة: إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم وإن كان له تحقق في نفسه فثابت، وإلا فمنفي. الموجود أخص من الثابت والمنفي من المعدوم، فالمعدوم قد يكون ثابتاً ولا واسطة بينه وبين الموجود. وقال بعضهم: إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإما بالاستقلال وهو الموجود أو بالتبعية وهو الحال وإلا فمعدوم، إما متحقق في نفسه فثابت، أو لا فمنفي، فالمعدوم ثابت وبينه وبين الموجود واسطة.
The fourth investigation: Existence (al-wujud) is synonymous with establishedness (al-thubut) and coextensive with thingness (al-shay'iyya), and non-existence (al-'adam) is synonymous with negation (al-nafy). Thus the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is not established, and there is no intermediate between it and the existent (al-mawjud). And its ruling in the two matters is the same individually and collectively. So it is said: the known (al-ma'lum) either has no establishedness, and it is the non-existent; or it has establishedness by virtue of its essence, and it is the existent; or by dependence on another, and it is the state (al-hal). So it is an attribute of the existent, neither existent nor non-existent, thus an intermediate is realized. And the majority of the Mu'tazila said: If it has a being (kawn) in external entities (a'yan), then it is existent; otherwise it is non-existent. And if it has realization (tahaqquq) in itself, then it is established; otherwise it is negated. The existent is more specific than the established, and the negated is more specific than the non-existent. So the non-existent may be established, and there is no intermediate between it and the existent. And some of them said: If it has a being in external entities, then either independently, which is the existent, or dependently, which is the state; otherwise it is non-existent. Either it is realized in itself, then it is established, or not, then it is negated. So the non-existent is established, and there is an intermediate between it and the existent.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory statement. The author begins by asserting that existence (wujud) is synonymous with establishedness (thubut) and coextensive with thingness (shay'iyya). Non-existence ('adam) is synonymous with negation (nafy). From this, two implications follow: first, the non-existent is not established (meaning it has no reality); second, there is no third state (wasiṭa) between existence and non-existence — something is either existent or not. The author then reports a classification of the known (ma'lum) into three: what has no establishedness (the non-existent), what has establishedness in itself (the existent), and what has establishedness through dependence on another (the state, al-hal). The state is an attribute of an existent, but neither existent nor non-existent in itself, thus an intermediate. Next, he presents the view of the majority of the Mu'tazila. For them, existence is defined by having 'being' (kawn) in extramental entities (a'yan), while establishedness is defined by having realization in itself. Thus the existent is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the established, and the negated is more specific than the non-existent. On this view, the non-existent can be established (if it has realization in itself, like possibles before creation), and there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence. Finally, he reports another group (some of them) who reintroduce the state: if something has being in external entities, it is either existent (independent) or a state (dependent); otherwise it is non-existent. If it has realization in itself, it is established; if not, it is negated. For them, the non-existent is established and there is an intermediate (the state) between existence and non-existence. The author sequentially presents these classifications without yet declaring the truth — that will come in the next passage.

Key Terms
الوجود
الثبوت
العدم
النفي
الشيئية
الحال
المعلوم
المعدوم
الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the non-existent has no reality at all.
Correction: The text shows that even the non-existent can be 'established' (thabit) in the sense of having a quiddity that is mentally conceived, especially in the Mu'tazili view where non-existent possibles are established.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's initial statement, what is the relationship between existence (wujud) and establishedness (thubut)?
A) They are synonymous.
B) Existence is more general.
C) Establishedness is more general.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Existence and establishedness are synonyms, but the non-existent can still be established, and whether there is an intermediate state is debated among theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider something you want to achieve—like a skill or a good deed—do you only act on it once it becomes physically real, or do you give it reality in your intention and planning first? How does the idea that the non-existent can be 'established' in God's knowledge challenge you to treat your future actions as already serious before they happen?
?
The passage reveals a theological tension: some scholars affirm an intermediate state (al-hal) between existence and non-existence, others deny it. In your own faith journey, are there beliefs or doubts you keep in an undefined middle ground because you are afraid to commit? What would it mean for you to resolve that ambiguity, even if the answer is complex?
?
If establishedness (thubut) is broader than existence—meaning many things are real in God's knowledge but not yet in the world—do you live as if the unseen realities (like the Last Day, the angels, your own deeds being recorded) are more 'established' in your heart than the temporary physical things you see? Point to one specific unseen truth you have been ignoring, and decide today how to give it greater weight in your daily decisions.

The Four Schools on the Non-Existent and the Intermediate
Al-Taftazani enumerates four views and declares the truth that there is no intermediate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical positions regarding the non-existent and the intermediate, and why the correct view negates both.

Core Idea
The non-existent has no reality and no intermediate exists between being and non-being.

Al-Taftazani introduces a key debate in Islamic theology: is the non-existent something real? And is there a third category between existence and non-existence? This passage lays out the four possible answers and declares the truth, grounding it in the synonymy of existence with establishedness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure of the passage.
2
Identify the two questions
The disagreement is over two issues: (1) whether the non-existent is established and a thing, and (2) whether there is an intermediate. See how these two binary questions yield four positions.
3
Map the four positions
Work through the four possibilities: affirm both, negate both, affirm first negate second, negate first affirm second. Understand which one al-Taftazani declares as truth.
The truth is negation of both.
4
Understand the proof
The proof relies on the synonymy of existence with establishedness and non-existence with negation. Just as negation is not established, so the non-existent is not established. And just as there is no intermediate between affirmation and negation, so there is none between existence and non-existence.
Also note the discussion on thingness (shay'iyya) and its relationship to existence.
5
Examine the opponents' view
Some Ash'aris and Mu'tazilis affirm an intermediate called 'the state' (al-hal). Understand their reasoning: if something lacks external existence, it is non-existent; if it has establishedness, it is either independent (existent) or dependent (state).
The passage presents this view without refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد اختلفوا في أن المعدوم هل هو ثابت وشيء أم لا؟ وفي أنه هل بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أم لا؟ والمذاهب أربعة، أعني إثبات الأمرين أو نفيهما، أو إثبات الأول ونفي الثاني أو بالعكس. وذلك أنه إما أن يكون المعدوم ثابتاً أو لا. وعلى التقديرين إما أن يكون بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة أو لا. والحق نفيهما بناء على أن الوجود يرادف الثبوت، والعدم يرادف النفي، فكما أن النفي ليس ثابتاً، فكذا المعدوم، وكما أنه لا واسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم، وأما الشيئية فتساوق الوجود بمعنى أن كل موجود شيء، وبالعكس، ولفظ المساوقة يستعمل عندهم فيما يعم الاتحاد في المفهوم فيكون اللفظان مترادفين والمساواة في الصدق فيكونان متباينين ولهم تردد في اتحاد مفهوم الوجود والشيئية. بل ربما يدعي نفيه بناء على أن قولنا: السواد موجود، يفيد فائدة يعتد بها بخلاف قولنا: السواد شيء فصار الحاصل أن كل ما يمكن أن يعلم إن كان له تحقق في الخارج أو الذهن فموجود وثابت وشيء وإلا فمعدوم، ومنفي ولا شيء، وأما المخالفون فمنهم من خالف في نفي الواسطة وإليه ذهب من أصحابنا إمام الحرمين أولاً، والقاضي، ومن المعتزلة أبو هاشم، فقالوا: المعلوم إن لم يكن له ثبوت أي في الخارج لأن مبنى الكلام على نفي الوجود الذهني، وإلا فالمعلوم موجود في الذهن قطعاً فهو المعدوم. وإن كان له فإن كان باستقلاله وباعتبار ذاته فهو الموجود، وإن كان باعتبار التبعية فهو الحال.
They have disagreed concerning whether the non-existent is established and a thing or not, and concerning whether there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. The positions are four: affirmation of both, negation of both, affirmation of the first and negation of the second, or the reverse. That is because either the non-existent is established or not. In both cases, either there is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent or not. The truth is the negation of both, based on the fact that existence is synonymous with establishedness and non-existence is synonymous with negation. Just as negation is not established, so is the non-existent; and just as there is no intermediate between the established and the negated, so there is none between the existent and the non-existent. As for thingness (al-shay'iyya), it is coextensive with existence, meaning that every existent is a thing, and vice versa. The term 'musawaqa' (coextensiveness) is used by them to cover both identity in concept—making the two terms synonymous—and equality in truth-value—making them distinct but coextensive. And they hesitate concerning the identity of the concept of existence and thingness. Indeed, some may claim their difference based on the fact that our saying 'blackness is existent' conveys a worthwhile benefit, unlike our saying 'blackness is a thing'. So the result is that everything that can be known, if it has realization in the external or the mind, is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing. As for the opponents, some of them opposed the negation of the intermediate, and to this view inclined from among our companions (Ash'aris) Imam al-Haramayn (first) and al-Qadi (al-Baqillani), and from the Mu'tazila Abu Hashim. They said: The known, if it has no establishedness, i.e., in the external—because the discussion is based on the denial of mental existence—then it is non-existent (for otherwise the known is existent in the mind definitely). And if it has establishedness, then if it is by independence and by virtue of its essence, it is the existent; and if it is by dependence, it is the state (al-hal).
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a classification of scholarly positions followed by an argument for the correct view. The author first identifies the two points of disagreement: (1) whether the non-existent (ma'dum) is established (thabit) and a thing (shay'), and (2) whether there is an intermediate (wasita) between existence and non-existence. This yields four logical possibilities: affirm both, negate both, affirm the first and negate the second, or the reverse. The author then states the truth (al-haqq) as the negation of both: the non-existent is not established, and there is no intermediate. The proof relies on the synonymy of existence and establishedness, and of non-existence and negation. Since negation is not established, the non-existent is not established; and since there is no intermediate between the established and the negated, there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence. Next, the author clarifies the relationship of thingness (shay'iyya) to existence, noting that they are coextensive (musawaqa) but may differ in concept. Some deny their identity because saying 'blackness is existent' adds information beyond saying 'blackness is a thing'. The conclusion is that anything with realization in the external or the mind is existent, established, and a thing; otherwise it is non-existent, negated, and nothing. Finally, the author reports the view of the opponents (some Ash'aris like Imam al-Haramayn and al-Qadi al-Baqillani, and the Mu'tazili Abu Hashim) who affirm an intermediate: they distinguish between existence in the external (which requires independence) and establishedness in itself. For them, the known that has no external establishedness is non-existent (since mental existence is not considered here), but if it has establishedness either independently (existent) or dependently (state), the latter serves as an intermediate. The author does not argue against them in this passage; he simply presents their position.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the four positions are arbitrary or merely historical accidents.
Correction: The text shows they are logically exhaustive based on two binary questions: whether the non-existent is established and whether there is an intermediate.

?

?

Abu Hashim's and Opponents' Views on States
Distinguishes which attributes entail states according to Abu Hashim and his opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between Abu Hashim's restricted view of which attributes produce states and the broader view of al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn.

Core Idea
States are not created by all attributes, only specific ones according to Abu Hashim.

This passage presents a key debate in kalam: do all attributes produce states (ahwal), or only some? Abu Hashim, the first to systematically discuss states, distinguishes between accidents conditioned by life and those that are not. Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn later adopted a more inclusive position. Understanding this disagreement is crucial for grasping the theory of states and how different mutakallimun conceptualized the relationship between attributes and their subjects.

Guided Path
1
Identify Abu Hashim's classification
Read the passage and note the two categories of accidents Abu Hashim discusses: those not conditioned by life (like color, odor, and 'being') and those conditioned by life (like knowledge and power).
Pay attention to the exception of 'being' (al-kawn).
2
Understand the exception: al-kawn
Abu Hashim says that among accidents not conditioned by life, only 'being' produces a state (ka'iniyya). Ask yourself why 'being' might be special.
3
Contrast with the opposing view
Al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn claim every attribute produces a state, giving examples like blackness producing 'blackness' (sawadiyya). Compare this with Abu Hashim's restrictions.
Notice they extend states to non-life-conditioned attributes as well.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these different views affect the understanding of divine attributes (since the state theory is often applied to Allah). Does restricting states limit what we can say about Allah's attributes?
This is a deeper application, but it shows why the debate matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : وأول من قال بالحال أبو هاشم وفصل القول فيه بأن الأعراض التي لا تكون مشروطة بالحياة كاللون والرائحة لا توجب لمن قامت به حالاً ولا صفة، إلا الكون فإنه يوجب لمحنه[؟] الكائنية وهي من الأحوال، وأما الأعراض المشروطة بالحياة، فإنها توجب لمحلها أحوالاً للمحل كالعلم للعالمية والقدرة للقادرية. وزعم القاضي وإمام الحرمين أن كل صفة فهي توجب للمحل حالاً، كالكون للكائنية والسواد للأسودية والعلم للعالمية
Then he said: 'The first to speak of the state was Abu Hashim, and he detailed his doctrine therein: that accidents which are not conditioned by life, like color and odor, do not necessitate for their subject a state nor an attribute, except for 'being' (al-kawn), for it necessitates for its subject [؟] 'be-ing-ness' (al-ka'iniyya), which is among the states. As for accidents conditioned by life, they necessitate for their subject states for the subject, like knowledge necessitates 'knowingness' (al-'alamiyya) and power necessitates 'powerfulness' (al-qadiriyya). And al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn claimed that every attribute necessitates for its subject a state, like 'being' for 'be-ing-ness', blackness for 'blackness' (al-sawadiyya), and knowledge for 'knowingness'.'
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasting views on which attributes produce states (ahwal). First, Abu Hashim's detailed doctrine: accidents not conditioned by life (like color and odor) do not produce states or attributes, except for 'being' (al-kawn), which does produce a state called 'be-ing-ness' (al-ka'iniyya). In contrast, accidents conditioned by life (like knowledge and power) always produce states for their subjects (e.g., knowingness, powerfulness). Second, al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn hold a broader view: every attribute, including those not conditioned by life, produces a state—thus blackness produces 'blackness' (al-sawadiyya), etc. The passage thus highlights a key disagreement in the conception of states.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes produce states for their subjects.
Correction: Abu Hashim restricts states to accidents conditioned by life and the special case of 'being'; al-Qadi and Imam al-Haramayn allow states for all attributes.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this distinction challenge your self-understanding?
?
The opponents of Abu Hashim argued that every attribute produces a state. Reflect on a time when you assumed that a single mistake or failing defined you completely. Did you treat that attribute as if it produced a permanent state? How can the nuanced view of states help you see yourself more accurately?
?
This debate shows that even great scholars differed on the scope of states. When you encounter disagreement among scholars about matters of belief, do you feel unsettled, or do you see it as an invitation to deepen your own understanding? Mu'tazilite Classification of the Known
Al-Taftazani explains and corrects the Mu'tazilite classification of the known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite threefold division of the known into existent, state, and non-existent, and how al-Taftazani corrects the misconception about the relationship between fixed and non-existent.

Core Idea
The classification of the known reveals the logical precision required in kalam to avoid confusion between categories.

In this passage, al-Taftazani engages with the Mu'tazilite classification of the known into existent, state, and non-existent. He points out a common misunderstanding: that the category of 'fixed' is a subset of 'non-existent'. Through careful analysis, he shows the correct generic-special relationships among these terms. This lesson will walk through that classification and its correction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the Mu'tazilite perspective, the known is divided into existent (mawjud), state (hal), and non-existent (ma'dum). Identify each and note their definitions.
The state is intermediate, having being in essences by dependence.
2
Subdivisions of non-existent
The Mu'tazila further divide non-existent into fixed (thabit) and negated (manfi). Notice that some non-existent is considered fixed.
This is a key point of contention.
3
Recognize the confusion
The author notes that the apparent wording suggests fixed is a division of non-existent, but this is incorrect.
4
Grasp the correct relationship
Fixed and non-existent have a general-special relationship (umum min wajh). Fixed includes existent and state, while non-existent includes negated.
This resolves the misunderstanding.
5
Review the corrected classification
The author presents the correct classification: the known is either realized in itself or not; if realized, it may be existent, state, or non-existent depending on being in essences.
This aligns with earlier divisions from Talkhis al-Muhassal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأعيان فله تقرر في نفسه من غير عكس فيكون الموجود أخص من الثابت. وكل ما لا تقرر له في نفسه لا كون له في الأعيان وليس كل ما لا كون له تقرر له، فيكون المنفي أخص من المعدوم، فيكون بعض المعدوم لا منفياً بل ثابتاً، ومنهم من خالف في الأمرين جميعاً وهم بعض المعتزلة قالوا : المعلوم إن كان له كون في الأعيان فإن كان له ذلك بالاستقلال فهو الموجود، وإن كان بتبعية الغير فهو الحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فهو المعدوم، والمعدوم إن كان متحققاً في نفسه فثابت وإلا فمنفي. فقد جعلوا بعض المعدوم ثابتاً . وأثبتوا بين الموجود والمعدوم واسطة هو الحال، وظاهر العبارة يوهم أن الثابت قسم من المعدوم ، وليس كذلك. بل بينهما عموم من وجه لأنه يشمل الموجود والحال بخلاف المعدوم والمعدوم يشمل المنفي بخلاف الثابت وان كان المعدوم مبايناً للمنفي على ما صرح به في تلخيص المحصل من أن القائلين بكون المعدوم شيئاً لا يقولون للممتنع معدوم بل منفي . كان الأول في هذا التقسيم أن يقال : المعلوم إن لم يتحقق في نفسه فمنفي، وإن تحقق فإن كان له كون في الأعيان فإما بالاستقلال فموجود، أو بالتبيعة فحال، وإن لم يكن له كون في الأعيان فمعدوم وفي التقسيم السابق أنه إن لم يتحقق فمنفي، وان تحقق فثابت، وحينئذ إن كان له كون في الأعيان فموجود، وإلا فمعدوم.
For essences, they have self-subsistence without reciprocal, so the existent is more specific than the fixed. And everything that has no self-subsistence has no being in essences, and not everything that has no being has self-subsistence. So the negated is more specific than the non-existent; thus some non-existent is not negated but rather fixed. Among them are those who disagreed on both matters, namely some of the Mu'tazila. They said: The known—if it has being in the essences, then if it has that independently, it is existent; if it is by dependence on another, it is a state; and if it has no being in the essences, it is non-existent. The non-existent, if it is realized in itself, is fixed; otherwise it is negated. So they made some non-existent fixed, and affirmed between the existent and the non-existent an intermediate which is the state. The apparent wording suggests that the fixed is a division of the non-existent, but it is not so. Rather, between them is a general-special relationship in some respect, because [the fixed] includes the existent and the state unlike the non-existent, and the non-existent includes the negated unlike the fixed, even though the non-existent is distinct from the negated according to what was clarified in Talkhis al-Muhassal, that those who say the non-existent is a thing do not call the impossible non-existent but rather negated. The first in this division is that one should say: The known—if it is not realized in itself, it is negated; if it is realized, then if it has being in the essences, either independently so existent, or dependently so state; and if it has no being in the essences, it is non-existent. And in the previous division, it was said: if it is not realized, it is negated; if realized, it is fixed; then if it has being in the essences, it is existent, otherwise non-existent.
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the known (al-ma'lum) into categories. The author begins by clarifying a previous statement about essences (a'yan) having self-subsistence (taqarrur). He then introduces the Mu'tazilite position which posits three categories: existent (mawjud), state (hal), and non-existent (ma'dum). The Mu'tazila further divide the non-existent into fixed (thabit) and negated (manfi). They also affirm the state as an intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author critiques this view, pointing out the confusion between 'fixed' and 'non-existent'. He explains that 'fixed' actually has a general-special relationship (umum min wajh) with 'existent' and 'state', not with 'non-existent'. He then provides the correct classification: the known is either realized (mutahaqqiq) in itself or not; if not realized, it is negated; if realized, then either it has independent being in essences (existent), dependent being (state), or no being in essences (non-existent). The author also references the Talkhis al-Muhassal to clarify that the proponents of the non-existent as a thing do not classify the impossible as non-existent but as negated.

معلوم (known)
موجود (existent)
حال (state)
معدوم (non-existent)
ثابت (fixed)
منفي (negated)
كون (being)
أعيان (essences)
عموم من وجه (general-special in some respect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'fixed' (thabit) is a subdivision of 'non-existent' (ma'dum).
Correction: Fixed and non-existent have a general-special relationship: fixed includes existent and state, while non-existent includes negated; they are not in a simple genus-species relation.

Checkpoint

D) It is negated.

Key Takeaway
The correct classification of the known requires distinguishing between independent existence (mawjud), dependent existence (hal), and non-existence (ma'dum), with the fixed (thabit) being a broader category that includes both existent and state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you sometimes dismiss someone's pain or struggle as 'nothing' (negated) when it actually has a dependent reality (state) that deserves recognition? How will you train yourself to see the 'states' that others carry?
?
The author corrects a subtle classification error with meticulous logic. Is there a belief you hold about your faith that you have never examined with such precision? Will you commit to studying one such belief this week with the same care?
?
