No Intermediate Between Existence and Non-Existence: The Argument from Necessity
Al-Taftazani argues from necessary reason that no intermediate exists between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that necessary reason compels the denial of any intermediate state between existence and non-existence, and that thingness is coextensive with existence.

Core Idea
Necessary reason shows existence and non-existence are exhaustive opposites with no middle ground.

Al-Taftazani presents a sharp logical argument against the Mu'tazilite notion of an intermediate state (hal) and the fixedness of the non-existent. He appeals to necessary reason (darura), which compels us to see that there is no conceptual space between existence and non-existence. Understanding this argument is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on the ontology of nonexistent things.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two denials
Notice that al-Taftazani denies two things: the fixedness (thubut) of the non-existent, and the existence of an intermediate between existence and non-existence. Both are based on necessity.
These two denials are the core of the passage.
2
Understand the meaning of fixedness
The author equates fixedness with existence: 'there is nothing less from fixedness except existence mentally or externally'. That is, what is fixed is either mental or external existence. There is no third kind.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite idea that non-existent things have a kind of fixedness.
3
Grasp the relationship between thingness and existence
Thingness tracks existence: anything that is a thing is existent. So there is no thing that is non-existent.
This is a key Ash'ari position.
4
Consider the opponent's objection
The opponent tries to introduce an intermediate by distinguishing 'fixedness' from 'existence' and 'non-existence' from 'negation', but the author dismisses this as mere sophistry and terminology not worth disputing.
Al-Taftazani points out that if the opponent insists on using terms differently, it's a verbal dispute, not a substantive one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي ثبوت المعدوم ونفي الواسطة بينه وبين الوجود لنا في المقامين الضرورة فإنه لا يقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، ولا يتصور بينهما واسط. أي في نفي ثبوت المعدوم وشيئيته ونفي الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم الضرورة. فإنها قاضية بذلك، إذ لا يعقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً، أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك والشيئية تساوق الوجود فالثابت في الذهن أو الخارج موجود فيه وكما لا تعقل الواسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم والمنازع مكابر وجعل الوجود أخص من الثبوت والعدم من المنفي، وجعل الموجود ذاتاً لها الوجود والمعدوم ذات لها العدم، لتكون الصفة واسطة اصطلاح لا مشاحة فيه .
Denial of the fixedness of the non-existent and denial of the intermediate between it and existence is for us in both positions based on necessity. For there is nothing less from fixedness except existence mentally or externally, and from non-existence except negation of that, and no intermediate between them is conceivable. That is, in denying the fixedness of the non-existent and its thingness, and denying the intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, [the basis is] necessity. For it judges that, since nothing is conceivable from fixedness except existence mentally or externally, and from non-existence except negation of that. And thingness tracks existence, so what is fixed in the mind or externally is existent therein. And just as no intermediate is conceivable between the fixed and the negated, so too between the existent and the non-existent. And the opponent is a sophist who made existence more specific than fixedness and non-existence more specific than negation, and made the existent a substance having existence and the non-existent a substance having non-existence, so that the attribute becomes an intermediate by a convention not worth disputing.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari argument against the Mu'tazilite position that affirms a state (hal) as an intermediate between existence and non-existence and that the non-existent has a fixedness (thubut). The author asserts that necessary reason (darura) compels the denial of both: there is no fixedness except existence (either mental or external), and there is no non-existence except the negation of that. Thus, there is no conceivable intermediate between existence and non-existence. The author explains that 'thingness' (shay'iyya) is coextensive with existence, so anything fixed in mind or externally is existent. Just as there is no intermediate between fixed (thabit) and negated (manfi), there is no intermediate between existent and non-existent. The opponent is considered a stubborn sophist (mukabir) who attempts to distinguish existence from fixedness by making existence more specific, and similarly non-existence from negation, and who posits that the existent is a substance having existence and the non-existent a substance having non-existence, thereby making the attribute (i.e., the state) an intermediate—but this is merely a terminological disagreement not worth disputing.

Key Terms
ضرورة (necessity/necessary reason)
ثبوت (fixedness)
معدوم (non-existent)
واسطة (intermediate)
شيئية (thingness)
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
منفي (negated)
مكابر (stubborn sophist)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existent has a fixedness (thubut) that is a kind of reality intermediate between existence and non-existence.
Correction: The passage argues that fixedness is nothing but existence (mental or external), and non-existence is nothing but the negation of that. No intermediate exists.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from necessity, what is the relationship between thingness and existence?
A) Thingness is more general than existence.
B) Thingness is identical to existence.
C) Thingness is a state between existence and non-existence.
D) Thingness applies only to the non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Necessary reason shows that existence and non-existence are exhaustive opposites with no intermediate, and thingness is identical to existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You treat many 'things' as real in your mind—worries, regrets, fantasies—that have no external existence. If thingness equals existence, are you giving fixedness to non-existent illusions? Will you audit your inner world today and abandon what is not truly existent?
?
The opponent redefines terms to create a middle ground. Where have you done the same—calling a sin ‘not quite disobedience’ or a duty ‘not quite required’? Are you willing to drop the verbal games and face the exhaustive choice between obedience and disobedience?
?
If necessary reason shows that existence and non-existence are exhaustive with no middle, then your very being is either fixed in the sight of God or negated. What anchor of reality do you stand on—mental abstraction or actual communion with the Creator? How will you live today as someone truly existent before Him?

The Ash'ari Position on Non-Existence and the Intermediary
Taftazani states the Ash'ari position on the non-existence of the non-existent and denies an intermediary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that the non-existent has no fixedness (thubut) and that there is no intermediary state between existence and non-existence, as a necessary rational judgment.

Core Idea
The Ash'aris assert that thubut is identical to existence, so the non-existent is simply nothing and no third state exists.

This passage from Taftazani's Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a core Ash'ari doctrine: the non-existent has no reality even as a fixed essence. The author bases this on rational necessity and refutes the idea of an intermediary state between existence and non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and key terms come through before interpreting.
Notice the strong claim: 'لا يعقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود'.
2
Identify the key terms and their relationships
Focus on thubut, wujud, 'adam, and shay'iyya. Understand how the author equates thubut with existence and 'adam with negation.
Thingness (shay'iyya) is said to be concomitant with existence.
3
Understand the argument against an intermediary
The author argues that no intermediary is conceivable between fixed and negated, so also between existent and non-existent. The opponent is called obstinate.
The terminological distinction some make is dismissed as mere convention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفي ثبوت المعدوم ونفي الواسطة بينه وبين الوجود لنا في المقامين الضرورة فإنه لا يقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك، ولا يتصور بينهما واسطة. أي في نفي ثبوت المعدوم وشيئيته ونفي الواسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم الضرورة. فإنها قاضية بذلك، إذ لا يعقل من الثبوت إلا الوجود ذهناً، أو خارجاً من العدم إلا نفي ذلك والشيئية تساوق الوجود فالثابت في الذهن أو الخارج موجود فيه وكما لا تعقل الواسطة بين الثابت والمنفي، فكذا بين الموجود والمعدوم والمنازع مكابر وجعل الوجود أخص من الثبوت والعدم من المنفي، وجعل الموجود ذاتاً لها الوجود والمعدوم ذات لها العدم، لتكون الصفة واسطة اصطلاح لا مشاحة فيه.
The negation of the existence (thubut) of the non-existent and the negation of the intermediary between it and existence—for us (the Ash'aris) in both positions, [the judgment is] necessity (darura). For nothing is understood from thubut except existence, mentally or externally, and from non-existence ('adam) except the negation of that, and no intermediary is conceivable between them. That is, concerning the negation of the existence (thubut) and thingness (shay'iyya) of the non-existent and the negation of the intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, [the judgment is] necessity. For it [necessity] decides that, since nothing is intellectually grasped from thubut except existence, mentally or externally, and from non-existence except the negation of that. And thingness (shay'iyya) is concomitant with existence; so what is fixed (thabit) in the mind or externally is existent therein. Just as no intermediary is conceivable between the fixed and the negated, so too between the existent and the non-existent. And the opponent is being obstinate. And [some] made existence more specific than thubut and non-existence more specific than the negated (manfi), and made the existent an essence having existence and the non-existent an essence having non-existence, so that the attribute becomes an intermediary—a terminological convention (istilah) about which there is no dispute.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Ash'ari position on the non-existence of the non-existent (ma'dum) and the denial of any intermediary between existence and non-existence. The author begins by asserting that the negation of thubut for the non-existent and the negation of an intermediary are based on necessity (darura), meaning they are self-evident rational judgments. He explains that thubut (fixedness) is nothing other than existence (wujud), whether mental or external, and that non-existence ('adam) is simply the negation of that. No third state is conceivable between them. The term 'thingness' (shay'iyya) is equated with existence, so whatever is fixed (thabit) in mind or external world is existent. The opponent who posits a separate fixedness for the non-existent is accused of obstinacy (mukabara). Some scholars attempted to terminologically distinguish existence as more specific than thubut, and non-existence as more specific than the negated, but the author dismisses this as a mere terminological convention (istilah) about which there is no quarrel.

Correction: The author insists it is a necessary rational judgment, not a convention. The terminological distinction is only a later convention that does not affect the core position.

The author calls the opponent obstinate (mukabir) for denying the self-evident. What would it take for you to submit to the necessity (darura) of truth without obstinacy?
?
Thingness (shay'iyya) is equated with existence. What in your life do you treat as 'real' or 'fixed' that does not actually exist in the sight of Allah? How can you align your sense of reality with what He has decreed as existent?

Five Arguments Against the Fixedness of the Non-Existent
Taftazani enumerates five arguments against thubut al-ma'dum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational arguments used by opponents to deny the fixedness (thubut) of the non-existent in kalam.

Core Idea
Affirming fixedness for the non-existent leads to theological and logical contradictions.

In this passage, Taftazani presents five distinct arguments from those who deny that non-existent things have any fixed reality (thubut). These arguments draw on divine omnipotence, the nature of privation, the oneness of the Necessary Existent, infinity, and logical relations. Each argument aims to show that affirming thubut for the non-existent leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the five arguments.
Notice that each argument begins with a number.
2
Examine each argument individually
Go through each of the five arguments. For each, identify the premise and the conclusion that shows a contradiction.
Take notes on how each argument appeals to a different principle.
3
Understand the theological implications
Reflect on why these arguments are important for kalam: they prevent attributing eternal existence to anything other than Allah.
The doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum is central to debates about divine attributes.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to verify you understood the first argument.
The checkpoint focuses on the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فاستدل بوجوه. الأول: أن ثبوت المعدوم ينافي المقدورية، لأن الذات أزلية والوجود حال لا يتعلق به قدرة. الثاني: أن العدم صفة نفي فينتفي الموصوف به. الثالث: أن ثبوت الذوات عندكم ليس من الغير فيلزم تعدد الواجب. الرابع: أنها غير متناهية مع أن الموجود منها متناه. فالكل أكثر من الباقية على العدم بمتناه فتكون متناهية. الخامس: أن المعدوم كان مساوياً للمنفي أو أخص منه لم يكن ثابتاً، وإن كان أعم منه لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، والا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص. بل ثابتاً. وهو صادق على المنفي فيلزم ثبوته وهو محال.
He said: So he argued with proofs. First: That the existence (thubut) of the non-existent contradicts [divine] omnipotence (maqduriyya), because the essence is eternal (azaliyya) and existence is an accident that cannot be an object of power. Second: That non-existence is a privative attribute (sifat nafi), so the described [essence] is negated [by it]. Third: That the existence (thubut) of essences according to you is not from another, so it necessitates multiplicity of the Necessary [Existent]. Fourth: That they are infinite, while what exists among them is finite; so the whole is more than the remainder in non-existence by a finite amount, thus [the whole] becomes finite. Fifth: That the non-existent, if it were equal to the negated (manfi) or more specific, it would not be fixed; but if it were more general, it would not be pure negation, otherwise no difference would remain between the general and the specific. Rather it would be fixed, and that [predicate] is true of the negated, so its being fixed would entail its negation, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents five arguments (wujuh) from opponents who affirm thubut for the non-existent. The first argument states that if the non-existent had fixedness, that fixedness would be eternal (azaliyya) and thus uncreated, contradicting divine omnipotence (maqduriyya) because existence is a contingent accident (hal) that cannot be an object of power. The second argument claims that non-existence is a privative attribute (sifat nafi), so the essence described by it would itself be negated. The third argument contends that if essences have eternal fixedness not from another, they would be necessary beings (wajib), leading to multiplicity of the Necessary Existent. The fourth argument uses infinity: the set of all essences (including non-existent ones) is infinite, but the existent essences are finite; if one subtracts the finite existents from the infinite total, the remainder (non-existent) is still infinite, but the argument claims this leads to a contradiction regarding finitude. The fifth argument is logical: if the non-existent is equivalent to or more specific than the negated, it cannot be fixed; if it is more general, it is not pure negation; but if it is fixed and applies to the negated, then the negated would be fixed, which is absurd.

Key Terms
ثبوت
معدوم
مقدورية
أزلية
عدم
نفي
واجب
متناه
منفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think these arguments are mere sophistry or wordplay.
Correction: These are serious theological arguments rooted in kalam principles, aiming to protect divine omnipotence and oneness.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the existence (thubut) of the non-existent?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It contradicts divine omnipotence because the essence is eternal and existence is an accident
C) It necessitates multiplicity of the Necessary Existent
D) It makes the non-existent more general than the negated

Key Takeaway
The five arguments collectively demonstrate that affirming fixedness for the non-existent leads to contradictions with divine omnipotence, the nature of privation, the oneness of Allah, infinity, and logical consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading the first argument, consider: do you sometimes act as if your own plans and desires have an eternal fixedness that cannot be touched by God's power? How would you submit that fixedness to His omnipotence today?
?
The fifth argument shows that affirming fixedness for the non-existent leads to logical absurdity. In your daily life, where do you hold onto contradictions about Allah's attributes—perhaps affirming His power while denying His control over your outcomes? What is one such contradiction you need to resolve and align with the truth taught here?

Refutation of the First Argument: Temporal Existence
Taftazani refutes the first argument by allowing temporal origination of existence and denying infinite regress in fixed essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutation allows the essence's being qualified by existence to be temporally originated by divine power, denying the impossibility of infinite regress in fixed essences.

Core Idea
Existence can be temporally originated; infinite regress is allowed in the realm of fixed essences.

The first argument against the non-existence of the non-existent claimed that if the essence were non-existent, it could not become existent by divine power because that would lead to infinite regress or the non-existent being qualified. Taftazani responds by permitting that the essence's being qualified by existence is a temporal event caused by power, and he denies the impossibility of infinite regress in fixed essences (thabit). This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: refutation, objection, answer.
The passage is concise; each part has a specific logical function.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection starting with 'فإن قيل'. It claims that infinite regress and qualification of the non-existent would follow if existence is temporally originated.
3
Understand the response
The answer denies both claims: infinite regress is not impossible in fixed essences, and a fixed essence can be qualified by existence.
This distinguishes between thabit (fixed) and wujud (existence).
4
Reflect on the key move
Consider how this refutation preserves the possibility of creation ex nihilo without logical contradiction.
This is a standard Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول: بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل: هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية. والثالث: بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته. والرابع: يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه. بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس: بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي. فإن قيل: المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات.
And the first [argument] is refuted: by the permissibility that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) by [divine] power. If it is said: It [the essence] is negated [when non-existent], otherwise an infinite regress would follow and the non-existent being qualified by existence. The answer: by denying the impossibility of infinite regress in what is fixed (thabit) and the fixed being qualified by existence. And the second [is refuted] by denying the premise if what is meant is an attribute that is a negation, or if what is meant is a negated attribute. And the third: by [saying] the Necessary [Existent] is that which is independent of another in its existence, not in its thubut. And the fourth: it denies that what exceeds the other by a finite amount becomes finite. Rather, if the other is finite, proving that by the method of matching (tatbiq) between it and the whole is weak. And the fifth: by [saying] that its not being pure negation does not entail its being pure affirmation; rather it may be sometimes and sometimes not, so only some of the non-existent is fixed, so the fixedness of the negated does not follow. If it is said: The intended meaning is that if it were more general, it would be distinct from the specific, so it would be fixed—as an entailment—we say: then most of the premises are invalid.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage contains the Ash'ari refutations (rudud) of the five arguments. The first refutation allows that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) and caused by divine power, so the essence itself is not eternally existent. To the objection that this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the non-existent being qualified by existence, the response denies the impossibility of infinite regress in the realm of fixed essences (thabit) and denies that a fixed essence cannot be described by existence. The second refutation denies the premise that non-existence is a privative attribute; if it is a negation, it does not negate the subject, and if it is a negated attribute, the subject remains. The third refutation distinguishes between necessity in existence (wujud) and necessity in thubut; the Necessary Existent is independent only in existence, not in thubut, so fixed essences are not automatically necessary. The fourth refutation challenges the logic of the infinity argument: subtracting a finite amount from an infinite set does not necessarily make the remainder finite, and the method of 'matching' (tatbiq) between infinite sets is weak. The fifth refutation argues that if the non-existent is more general than the negated, its not being pure negation does not force it to be pure affirmation; it could be partially fixed. To the objection that distinctness from the specific implies fixedness, the answer is that this invalidates most of the premises.

Key Terms
اتصاف
حادث
قدرة
تسلسل
ثابت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think infinite regress is always logically impossible.
Correction: Ash'aris argue that infinite regress is impossible only in some cases, not in the realm of fixed essences (thabit).

Checkpoint
What does the refutation deny the impossibility of?
A) Infinite regress in existence
B) Infinite regress in fixed essences
C) The non-existent being qualified
D) The essence being qualified by existence

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation permits that existence can be temporally originated without falling into infinite regress because infinite regress is allowed in fixed essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson shows that your very existence qualifying you is a temporal act of Allah's power. In your daily life, do you truly live as if your existence is sustained by Him every moment, or do you take it for granted? Name one specific situation today where you can consciously acknowledge this dependence.
?
The Ash'ari refutation allows infinite regress in fixed essences. What are some 'fixed' aspects of your character or identity that you treat as self-originated rather than as attributes given by Allah? How would your relationship with Allah change if you saw them as temporally qualified by His power?

Refutation of the Second Argument: Nature of Non-Existence
Taftazani refutes the second argument by denying the premise regarding the nature of non-existence as an attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutation of the second argument denies the premise that non-existence is either a privative attribute or a negated attribute.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not a real attribute; it is either a negation or a negated attribute of a subject.

The second argument claimed that non-existence is an attribute that negates the subject. Taftazani responds by denying the premise and distinguishing between two interpretations: if non-existence is a negation, it does not negate the subject; if it is a negated attribute, the subject remains. This lesson clarifies that subtle distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a very short refutation—just one phrase denying the premise.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The refutation says 'if what is meant is an attribute that is a negation, or if what is meant is a negated attribute.' Understand each case.
3
Understand the denial
In both cases, the premise fails: a negation is not a real attribute, and a negated attribute still implies a subject.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that non-existence cannot be an attribute that negates the subject completely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول: بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل: هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية. والثالث: بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته. والرابع: يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه. بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس: بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي. فإن قيل: المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات.
And the first [argument] is refuted: by the permissibility that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) by [divine] power. If it is said: It [the essence] is negated [when non-existent], otherwise an infinite regress would follow and the non-existent being qualified by existence. The answer: by denying the impossibility of infinite regress in what is fixed (thabit) and the fixed being qualified by existence. And the second [is refuted] by denying the premise if what is meant is an attribute that is a negation, or if what is meant is a negated attribute. And the third: by [saying] the Necessary [Existent] is that which is independent of another in its existence, not in its thubut. And the fourth: it denies that what exceeds the other by a finite amount becomes finite. Rather, if the other is finite, proving that by the method of matching (tatbiq) between it and the whole is weak. And the fifth: by [saying] that its not being pure negation does not entail its being pure affirmation; rather it may be sometimes and sometimes not, so only some of the non-existent is fixed, so the fixedness of the negated does not follow. If it is said: The intended meaning is that if it were more general, it would be distinct from the specific, so it would be fixed—as an entailment—we say: then most of the premises are invalid.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage contains the Ash'ari refutations (rudud) of the five arguments. The first refutation allows that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) and caused by divine power, so the essence itself is not eternally existent. To the objection that this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the non-existent being qualified by existence, the response denies the impossibility of infinite regress in the realm of fixed essences (thabit) and denies that a fixed essence cannot be described by existence. The second refutation denies the premise that non-existence is a privative attribute; if it is a negation, it does not negate the subject, and if it is a negated attribute, the subject remains. The third refutation distinguishes between necessity in existence (wujud) and necessity in thubut; the Necessary Existent is independent only in existence, not in thubut, so fixed essences are not automatically necessary. The fourth refutation challenges the logic of the infinity argument: subtracting a finite amount from an infinite set does not necessarily make the remainder finite, and the method of 'matching' (tatbiq) between infinite sets is weak. The fifth refutation argues that if the non-existent is more general than the negated, its not being pure negation does not force it to be pure affirmation; it could be partially fixed. To the objection that distinctness from the specific implies fixedness, the answer is that this invalidates most of the premises.

Key Terms
نفي
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think non-existence is a real attribute that negates the subject entirely.
Correction: Taftazani shows that non-existence is either a mere negation or a negated attribute, neither of which fully negates the subject.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation of the second argument deny?
A) The conclusion
B) The premise
C) The impossibility
D) The necessity

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the second argument denies that non-existence is an attribute that fully negates the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own existence is not a permanent attribute but a gift sustained moment by moment by the Necessary Being, how does that change the way you live today? What would it mean to consciously depend on Him for your next breath?
?
When you think about death or the state before birth, do you treat non-existence as a 'thing' to be feared or avoided? How does the truth that non-existence is simply the absence of existence—not a real attribute—free you from that fear and redirect your trust to the One who alone truly exists?
?
This lesson shows that a wrong assumption about non-existence can lead to a false argument against the existence of God. What wrong assumptions might you be holding about yourself—your abilities, your identity, or your purpose—that prevent you from submitting fully to the truth? Will you examine them today and let the truth refute them?

Refutation of the Third Argument: Necessity and Thubut
Taftazani refutes the third argument by redefining the Necessary Existent as independent in existence, not in thubut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutation of the third argument distinguishes between necessity in existence and necessity in thubut.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent is independent in existence, not in thubut.

The third argument posited that if the non-existent were fixed (thabit), it would be necessary. Taftazani responds by redefining the Necessary Existent: it is that which is independent of another in its existence (wujud), not in its thubut. Thus, fixed essences can be contingent in existence while being fixed in thubut.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. This is a single sentence defining the Necessary Existent.
2
Identify the key distinction
Note the contrast: 'في وجوده لا ثبوته'—the Necessary is independent in existence, not in thubut.
3
Understand the argument
The opponent assumed that fixedness (thubut) implies necessity, but Taftazani shows necessity is about existence, so fixed essences can be contingent.
4
Reflect on the implication
This refutation preserves the possibility that non-existent essences are fixed but not necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول: بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل: هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية. والثالث: بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته. والرابع: يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه. بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس: بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي. فإن قيل: المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات.

?
Have you ever believed something was necessarily true simply because it was firmly fixed in your mind? How does Taftazani's distinction between thubut and wujud challenge you to examine the actual basis of your certainties?
?

Commit to identifying one such thing today and ask whether it deserves the status of a necessary principle in your life.

Refutation of the Fourth Argument: Infinity and Tatbiq
Taftazani refutes the fourth argument by denying that exceeding by a finite amount implies finiteness and criticizing the method of matching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutation of the fourth argument challenges the logic of finiteness and the method of matching (tatbiq).

Core Idea
Subtracting a finite amount from an infinite set does not necessarily yield a finite remainder.

The fourth argument claimed that if the non-existent were infinite, subtracting a finite quantity would make it finite, leading to contradiction. Taftazani responds by denying that subtracting a finite amount from an infinite set necessarily makes it finite, and he criticizes the method of matching (tatbiq) as weak. This lesson explains that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the denial and the criticism of tatbiq.
2
Understand the denial
The refutation says 'يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه'—denying that exceeding something by a finite amount makes it finite.
3
Understand the criticism of tatbiq
The method of matching (comparing infinite sets) is weak for proving finiteness.
4
Reflect on the implication
This preserves the possibility of an infinite set of non-existent entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول: بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل: هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية. والثالث: بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته. والرابع: يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه. بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس: بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي. فإن قيل: المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات.
And the first [argument] is refuted: by the permissibility that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) by [divine] power. If it is said: It [the essence] is negated [when non-existent], otherwise an infinite regress would follow and the non-existent being qualified by existence. The answer: by denying the impossibility of infinite regress in what is fixed (thabit) and the fixed being qualified by existence. And the second [is refuted] by denying the premise if what is meant is an attribute that is a negation, or if what is meant is a negated attribute. And the third: by [saying] the Necessary [Existent] is that which is independent of another in its existence, not in its thubut. And the fourth: it denies that what exceeds the other by a finite amount becomes finite. Rather, if the other is finite, proving that by the method of matching (tatbiq) between it and the whole is weak. And the fifth: by [saying] that its not being pure negation does not entail its being pure affirmation; rather it may be sometimes and sometimes not, so only some of the non-existent is fixed, so the fixedness of the negated does not follow. If it is said: The intended meaning is that if it were more general, it would be distinct from the specific, so it would be fixed—as an entailment—we say: then most of the premises are invalid.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage contains the Ash'ari refutations (rudud) of the five arguments. The first refutation allows that the essence's being qualified by existence is temporally originated (hadith) and caused by divine power, so the essence itself is not eternally existent. To the objection that this leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) and the non-existent being qualified by existence, the response denies the impossibility of infinite regress in the realm of fixed essences (thabit) and denies that a fixed essence cannot be described by existence. The second refutation denies the premise that non-existence is a privative attribute; if it is a negation, it does not negate the subject, and if it is a negated attribute, the subject remains. The third refutation distinguishes between necessity in existence (wujud) and necessity in thubut; the Necessary Existent is independent only in existence, not in thubut, so fixed essences are not automatically necessary. The fourth refutation challenges the logic of the infinity argument: subtracting a finite amount from an infinite set does not necessarily make the remainder finite, and the method of 'matching' (tatbiq) between infinite sets is weak. The fifth refutation argues that if the non-existent is more general than the negated, its not being pure negation does not force it to be pure affirmation; it could be partially fixed. To the objection that distinctness from the specific implies fixedness, the answer is that this invalidates most of the premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a set exceeds another by a finite amount, it must be finite.
Correction: Taftazani shows that this is not necessarily true; an infinite set can exceed a finite one by a finite amount.

Checkpoint
What does the refutation say about the method of proving finiteness?
A) It is valid
B) It is weak
C) It is impossible
D) It is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The method of matching (tatbiq) is weak for proving finiteness of an infinite set.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a seemingly intuitive method (matching) is weak in proving finiteness. Is there any belief you hold about Allah or the unseen that you assume is proven because it 'feels right'? Will you test it against sound reasoning today?
?
Taftazani rejects an argument many would find plausible. Have you ever insisted on a flawed argument for God's existence just because it was comfortable? Are you willing to let go of weak proofs and seek stronger ones, even if it unsettles you?
?
If someone told you 'If the universe had a beginning, then it must be finite, so God is not needed,' how would you respond using the insight from this lesson? Commit to actually saying it to someone this week.

Refutation of the Fifth Argument: Generality and Fixedness
Taftazani refutes the fifth argument by denying that generality implies pure affirmation and by rebutting the objection of distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari refutation of the fifth argument handles the generality of non-existence and the implication of fixedness.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not pure negation; it can be partially fixed without being fully affirmative.

The fifth argument claimed that if the non-existent is more general than the negated (specific non-existents), then it must be distinct and therefore fixed. Taftazani responds by saying that not being pure negation does not force it to be pure affirmation; it can be somewhere in between. Additionally, the objection that generality implies fixedness is rebutted by noting that this would invalidate most premises. This lesson explores that nuanced refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation. This is the longest refutation with an objection and answer.
2
Understand the main point
Not being pure negation does not entail being pure affirmation; non-existence can be partially fixed.
3
Understand the objection
The objection says that if non-existence is more general, it is distinct from the specific, so it must be fixed. Taftazani counters that this would invalidate most premises.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that conceptual generality does not entail real existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد الأول: بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة فإن قيل: هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة هي نفي أو إن أريد صفة منفية. والثالث: بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته. والرابع: يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه. بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس: بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل قد وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي. فإن قيل: المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات.

Alternative Proof: Divine Power Negates Fixedness of the Non-Existent
Taftazani presents an argument that divine power would be impossible if the non-existent had fixedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some Ash'ari theologians argued that if the non-existent were fixed, divine power could not create anything.

Core Idea
If the non-existent were already fixed, creation would be impossible.

This lesson presents a key argument within Ash'ari theology. Some scholars did not consider the denial of thubut for the non-existent to be self-evident, so they provided rational proofs. The first proof, given here, is based on divine power (qudra) and its relationship to contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If... then...'.
The Arabic is short but dense. Focus on the logical conditional.
2
Understand the logic of the conditional argument
The argument is a conditional: If the non-existent were fixed (thubut), then divine power (qudra) could not affect it. Ask yourself why that would be the case.
The commentary explains that power acts on what is not yet existent; if it is already fixed, power cannot bring it into existence.
3
Reflect on why this proof is important
This proof shows that the denial of thubut is not merely a necessary truth for some Ash'aris but can be demonstrated through reasoning about divine power. Consider how this strengthens the Ash'ari position on creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منا من جعل نفي ثبوت المعدوم غير ضروري فاستدل عليه بوجوده. الأول: لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً لامتنع تأثير القدرة في شيء من الممكنات
Among us are those who made the negation of the fixedness of the non-existent non-necessary and argued for it with [the following] proofs. First: If the non-existent were fixed, then the effect of [divine] power upon any of the contingents would be impossible.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage introduces a different approach within the Ash'ari school. Some Ash'aris did not consider the denial of thubut for the non-existent to be a necessary (daruri) judgment, so they provided independent proofs. The first proof argues that if the non-existent had fixedness (thubut), then divine power (qudra) could not bring it into existence, because power only acts upon what is not already existent. If the thing is already fixed in its essence, it cannot be the object of creation. This would undermine the possibility of creation and thus contradict divine omnipotence. Note that the passage is incomplete, as only the first proof is given in this window.

Key Terms
ضروري
استدلال
معدوم
ثابت
قدرة
ممكنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Ash'aris consider the denial of thubut as necessary (daruri).
Correction: This passage shows that some Ash'aris did not consider it necessary and instead provided demonstrative proofs.

Checkpoint
Why would divine power be unable to affect contingents if the non-existent were fixed?
A) Because power only creates from nothing
B) Because the fixed non-existent would already have reality, making creation redundant
C) Because power cannot act on what is eternal
D) Because power only acts on existents

Key Takeaway
The non-existent cannot be fixed because otherwise divine power could not create contingents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that nothing is fixed in a way that prevents Allah's power, what specific area of your life are you still treating as unchangeable?
?
When you despair over a situation, do you realize that your despair implies you think that situation is 'fixed' beyond Allah's ability to change? How can this argument correct your heart?
?
What would it mean for your daily actions—your prayers, your trust—if you internalized that Allah's power can bring from non-existence anything, even what seems impossible?

Responses to Arguments Against Thubut al-Ma'dum
Al-Taftazani lists three objections and responds to all five arguments against thubut al-ma'dum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani defends the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum against multiple objections by making key distinctions between fixedness and existence, infinity and finitude, and negation and affirmation.

Core Idea
Distinctions preserve doctrine: fixedness ≠ existence, general ≠ pure negation.

This passage presents three specific objections (third, fourth, fifth) against the fixedness of non-existents, along with responses that also cover the first and second objections. The responses rely on precise conceptual distinctions: the Necessary is independent in existence, not fixedness; something exceeding an infinite by a finite amount can still be infinite; and not being a pure negation does not entail being a pure affirmation. These distinctions allow Al-Taftazani to maintain thubut al-ma'dum while avoiding absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get a sense of the argumentative flow. Notice how objections are stated concisely and responses follow with brief counter-arguments.
Pay attention to the numbered sequence: third, fourth, fifth objections then first, second, third, fourth, fifth responses.
2
Identify each objection-response pair
Match each of the third, fourth, and fifth objections with its corresponding response. Also note how responses to the first and second objections are included, even though those objections are not quoted here.
The responses are in order: first, second, third, fourth, fifth.
3
Analyze the key distinction in each response
For each response, extract the central distinction made (e.g., existence vs. fixedness, finite vs. infinite, negation vs. affirmation). Understand how that distinction blocks the objection.
The third response distinguishes between need in existence and need in fixedness.
4
Reflect on overall strategy
Consider how Al-Taftazani uses conceptual analysis to defend a subtle metaphysical doctrine. Why is making such fine distinctions important in kalam?
This guards against oversimplifying the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن ثبوت الذوات عندكم ليس من الغير فيلزم تعدد الواجب . الرابع : أنها غير متناهية مع أن الموجود منها متناه . فالكل أكثر من الباقية على العدم بمنتاه فتكون متناهية . الخامس : أن المعدوم كان مساوياً للمنفي أو أخص منه لم يكن ثابتاً، وإن كان أعم منه لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، والا لما بقي فرق بين العام والخاص. بل ثابتاً . وهو صادق على المنفي فيلزم ثبوته وهو محال . ورد الأول : بجواز أن يكون اتصاف الذات بالوجود حادثاً بالقدرة (١) فإن قيل : هو منفي وإلا لزم التسلسل واتصاف المعدوم بالوجود. : أجيب بمنع استحالة التسلسل في الثابت واتصاف الثابت بالوجود. والثاني بمنع الأول إن أريد صفة (٢) هي نفي. أو إن أريد صفة منفية . والثالث : بأن الواجب ما يستغني عن الغير في وجوده لا ثبوته .(٣) . والرابع : يمنع تناهي ما يزيد على الغير بمتناه . بل إذا كان الغير متناهياً. وإثبات ذلك بالتطبيق بينه وبين الكل ضعيف. والخامس : بأن عدم كونه نفياً صرفاً لا يستلزم كونه اثباتاً صرف بل وقد فلا يصدق إلا بعض المعدوم ثابت فلا يلزم ثبوت المنفي . فإن قيل : المراد أنه لو كان أعم لكان متميزاً عن الخاص، فيكون ثابتاً إلزاماً قلنا فيلغوا أكثر المقدمات .
The third: that the fixedness of essences according to you is not from another, so it necessitates multiplicity of the Necessary. The fourth: that they are infinite while that which exists among them is finite. So the whole is more than the remainder upon non-existence by a finite amount, so they become finite. The fifth: that the non-existent, if it were equal to the negated or more specific than it, it would not be fixed; and if it were more general than it, it would not be a pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific. Rather it is fixed. And it is true of the negated, so its fixedness is necessitated, and that is impossible. And the first was refuted: by the permissibility that the qualification of the essence with existence occurs by power. If it is said: it is negated, otherwise there would be infinite regress and qualification of the non-existent with existence. It was answered: by denying the impossibility of infinite regress in fixed things and the qualification of the fixed with existence. And the second: by denying the first if what is intended is an attribute that is negation, or if what is intended is an attribute that is negated. And the third: by [saying] that the Necessary is that which is independent of another in its existence, not in its fixedness. And the fourth: by denying that what exceeds the other by a finite amount is finite. Rather if the other is infinite, and proving that by the application (al-tatbiq) between it and the whole is weak. And the fifth: by [saying] that its not being a pure negation does not necessitate it being a pure affirmation; it may be such and such, so only some of the non-existent is fixed, so the fixedness of the negated is not necessitated. If it is said: what is meant is that if it were more general it would be distinguished from the specific, so it would be fixed as an entailment, we say: then most of the premises become invalid.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and responses concerning the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum (the fixedness of non-existents). The text presents three specific objections (the third, fourth, and fifth) and then gives the responses to all five objections, including the first and second which are not explicitly stated here. The third objection argues that if non-existents have fixedness from themselves, then they would be independent of another, making them necessary beings and thus implying multiple necessary beings. The response distinguishes necessity in existence from necessity in fixedness: the Necessary (wajib) is independent in its existence, not in its fixedness, so a non-existent may have fixedness from itself but still depend on a cause for existence. The fourth objection claims that non-existents are infinite while the existing things derived from them are finite, leading to a contradiction. The response denies that something that exceeds an infinite by a finite amount is necessarily finite; if the other is infinite, the excess does not entail finitude. The fifth objection argues that if a non-existent is more general than the negated (i.e., has some positive content), it would not be a pure negation, and if it is true of the negated, then the negated would have fixedness, which is absurd. The response explains that not being a pure negation does not make it a pure affirmation; only some non-existents are fixed, so the fixedness of the negated does not follow. The response to the first objection (not given in full) permits the qualification of essence with existence to be caused by power, and denies that infinite regress is impossible in fixed things. The response to the second objection distinguishes between a 'negating attribute' and a 'negated attribute' to avoid the dilemma. The overall purpose is to defend thubut al-ma'dum against common criticisms.

Key Terms

الواجب (al-wajib)
الصفة (al-sifa)
النفي (al-nafy)
الإثبات (al-ithbat)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fixedness of non-existents implies they are necessary beings or that they have full existence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that fixedness does not require independence in existence (for the Necessary it is only in existence) and that it does not entail pure affirmation. Non-existents can be fixed without being existent or necessary.

Checkpoint
In the response to the third objection, how does Al-Taftazani avoid the conclusion of multiple necessary beings?
A) He denies that non-existents have any fixedness at all.
B) He states that the Necessary is independent in existence, not in fixedness.
C) He argues that non-existents are not real essences.
D) He claims that fixedness is itself a kind of existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends thubut al-ma'dum by showing that fixedness of non-existents does not entail existence, necessity, or pure affirmation; it is a distinct ontological status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about things that Allah knows will happen but have not yet occurred, do you ever confuse His knowledge of them with their actual existence? How does distinguishing 'fixedness' from 'existence' help you avoid that confusion and protect your understanding of tawhid? ?
Al-Taftazani does not reject infinite regress outright but restricts it to certain domains. The Basis of the Doctrine of Thubut al-Ma'dum
The author explains the logical deduction leading to thubut al-ma'dum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that existence is additional to essence logically leads to the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum (the establishment of the non-existent).

Core Idea
The quiddity is established before existence because existence is an added attribute.

The doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum (the establishment of the non-existent) is a key concept in Islamic theology. This passage reveals its logical foundation: the belief that existence is a superadded attribute to essence. The author explains how this leads to the conclusion that quiddities are established prior to receiving existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's logical flow come through before you interpret.
Notice the two 'then they claimed' statements.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two claims that form the basis: (1) existence is additional to quiddity, and (2) existence is an attribute that occurs to and subsists in the quiddity.
These are the foundations of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the quiddity is established (thābita) before existence, and the converse is possible. See how this follows from the premises.
This is the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum.
4
Reflect on the logical connection
Ask yourself: Why must the quiddity be established before existence? If existence is an attribute, it must inhere in something already existent? The author argues that if existence were identical to the quiddity, their establishment would coincide, which is not the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بزيادة الوجود بمعنى أنهم زعموا أن وجود السواد زائد على ماهيته ثم زعموا أنه يجوز خلو تلك الماهية عن صفة الوجود وأيضاً لما اعتقدوا أن الوجود صفة تطرأ على الماهية وتقوم بها، ولم يتصور ذلك في النفي الصرف. أنتج لهم كون الماهية ثابتة قبل الوجود ويجوز العكس لأن الماهية إذا كانت ثابتة ذلك قبل الوجود لم يكن الوجود نفسها، وإلا لكان ثبوتها ثبوته وارتفاعها ارتفاعه .
The doctrine of the increase of existence (ziyādat al-wujūd) means that they claimed that the existence of blackness is additional to its quiddity, then they claimed that it is permissible for that quiddity to be free from the attribute of existence. Also, because they believed that existence is an attribute that occurs to the quiddity and subsists in it, and they did not conceive that in pure negation. This produced for them that the quiddity is established before existence, and the converse is possible, because if the quiddity is established before existence, then existence is not identical to it; otherwise, its establishment would be its establishment and its removal its removal.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage explains the origin of the doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum (the establishment of the non-existent). The author states that this doctrine is based on the belief that existence is an additional attribute (ziyādat al-wujūd) over and above the quiddity (māhiyya). For example, the existence of blackness is considered additional to its essence. They further claim that the quiddity can exist without the attribute of existence. Because they conceive of existence as an attribute that inheres in the quiddity, and they cannot conceive of it in pure negation, they conclude that the quiddity is established (thābita) prior to receiving existence. The converse is also possible: if the quiddity is established before existence, then existence is not identical to the quiddity, otherwise their establishment would coincide.

Key Terms
زيادة الوجود
ثبوت المعدوم
الماهية
الوجود
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That thubut al-ma'dum is an obvious or self-evident doctrine without need for proof.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is a philosophical deduction based on specific premises about the relationship between existence and quiddity.

Checkpoint

A) Existence is identical to essence.
B) Existence is a superadded attribute.
C) Non-existence is pure negation.
D) Essence cannot be established.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of thubut al-ma'dum is logically derived from the belief that existence is a superadded attribute to quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a specific quality you possess—like intelligence or patience. Is it merely an added attribute to your quiddity? How does that affect the way you seek to strengthen it or attribute it to yourself?
?
The passage claims existence is an attribute that 'occurs to' quiddity. When you imagine yourself before you were born, do you sense that your quiddity was somehow 'waiting'? What does that reveal about your attitude toward your own contingency and your need for the Creator's constant bestowal of existence?

The False Doctrine of Existence as an Attribute Additional to Essence
Describes the false doctrine that existence is a superadded attribute to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the false doctrine that existence is an attribute additional to essence and why it leads to the problematic implication that essences are fixed prior to existence.

Core Idea
Claiming existence is added to essence makes essences prior to existence, which is false.

This passage introduces a key false doctrine in kalam: the idea that existence is a superadded attribute to essence. Understanding this error is crucial for grasping the correct view that existence is identical to the existent thing. The passage shows how this belief logically forces one to conceive of essences as fixed realities before they receive existence, a problematic notion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the key terms: الوجود, الماهية, الثبوت.
2
Identify the doctrine being described
The passage describes the claim that existence is additional to essence. Identify how the author reports the arguments of those who hold this view.
They claim that existence is an attribute that occurs to an essence and can be separated from it.
3
Trace the logical implication
Follow the argument: if existence is additional, then the essence can exist without existence (i.e., it is fixed before existence). This implies that essences are prior to existence.
The author mentions that this results from not understanding pure negation.
4
Understand why this is problematic
Reflect on why the author presents this view as false. The implication that essences are fixed before existence contradicts the fact that things only exist when they have existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بزيادة الوجود بمعنى أنهم زعموا أن وجود السواد زائد على ماهيته ثم زعموا أنه يجوز خلو تلك الماهية عن صفة الوجود وأيضاً لما اعتقدوا أن الوجود صفة تطرأ على الماهية وتقوم بها، ولم يتصور ذلك في النفي الصرف. أنتج لهم كون الماهية ثابتة [ذلك؟] قبل الوجود ويجوز العكس لأن الماهية إذا كانت ثابتة قبل الوجود لم يكن الوجود نفسها، وإلا لكان ثبوتها ثبوته وارتفاعها ارتفاعه.
The statement of the addition of existence, meaning that they claimed that the existence of blackness is additional to its essence, then they claimed that it is permissible for that essence to be free from the attribute of existence, and also because they believed that existence is an attribute that occurs to the essence and subsists in it, and that was not conceived in pure negation. It produced for them that the essence is fixed [that?] before existence, and the reverse is permissible, because if the essence is fixed before existence, then existence would not be identical to it; otherwise its fixity would be its fixity and its removal would be its removal.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage introduces the doctrine held by some that existence is an attribute additional to essence. The author explains that this leads to the view that essences (mahiyyat) have a fixed reality prior to receiving the attribute of existence. The argument is that if existence is additional, then the essence can be conceived without it, implying that the essence is somehow established before existence. The author mentions that this view results from not understanding pure negation. The passage sets up the subsequent refutation.

Correction: The passage shows that this leads to the problematic view that essences are fixed before existence, which is false. Existence is not a separate attribute; it is identical to the existent thing.

The Objection that Non-Existent is Described by Non-Existence and the Response
Presents the objection that the non-existent is qualified by non-existence and the reply distinguishing types of negative attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that describing non-existents with non-existence implies their negation, and how the answer distinguishes between types of negative attributes to defend the view that non-existents have fixed essences.

Core Idea
Not all negative attributes negate their subject; some merely indicate the absence of a particular quality.

In this passage, the author presents a second objection against the claim that non-existents have fixed essences. The objection argues that if a non-existent is described by non-existence, which is a negative attribute, then it must be negated. The answer clarifies the ambiguity in the term 'negative attribute' and shows that some negative attributes do not entail the negation of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the objection draws an analogy: what is qualified by affirmation is established, so what is qualified by negation must be negated.
The key is the assumption that non-existence is a negative attribute in the sense of pure negation.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'negative attribute' in the answer
The first meaning is a pure negation of the attribute itself; the second is the negation of a specific thing (like non-spatiality).
The answer rejects the first meaning and accepts the second.
3
Reflect on the example of the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being is described by negative attributes like non-spatiality without being negated. This demonstrates that the objection fails.
This shows that privative attributes can apply to existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أن المعدوم متصف بالعدم الذي هو صفة نفي، لكونه رفعاً للوجود الذي هو صفة ثبوت، والمتصف بصفة النفي منفي، كما أن المتصف بصفة الإثبات ثابت . وأجيب بأنه إن أريد بصفة النفي صفة هي نفي في نفسه وسلب حتى يكون معنى المتصف به هو المنفي، فلا نسلم أن كل معدوم متصف بصفة النفي، وإنما يلزم لو كان العدم هو النفي وليس كذلك، بل أعم منه لكونه نقيضاً للوجود الذي هو أخص من الثبوت وإن أريد بها صفة هي نفي شيء وسلبه كاللاتحيز واللاحدوث مثلاً ، فضاهر أن المتصف به لا يلزم أن يكون منفياً، كالواجب يتصف بكثير من الصفات السلبية، إذ ليس يمنع اتصاف الموجود بالصفات العدمية، كما يمتنع اتصاف المعدوم بالصفات الوجودية.
The second: that the non-existent (al-ma'dum) is qualified by non-existence (al-'adam), which is a negative attribute (sifat nafy), because it is a removal of existence which is an affirmative attribute, and whatever is qualified by a negative attribute is negated, just as whatever is qualified by an affirmative attribute is established. And the answer: if by negative attribute is meant an attribute that is a negation in itself and a privation such that the meaning of being qualified by it is the negated, then we do not concede that every non-existent is qualified by a negative attribute; rather it would follow only if non-existence were the negation, but it is not; rather it is more general than it, because it is the contradictory of existence, and existence is more specific than establishment. And if by it is meant an attribute that is the negation of something and its privation, such as non-spatiality and non-temporality, then it is apparent that what is qualified by it does not necessarily have to be negated, like the Necessary (al-wajib) is qualified by many negative attributes; for being qualified by non-existential attributes is not prevented for the existent, just as being qualified by existential attributes is impossible for the non-existent.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This lesson presents the second objection against the view that non-existents have fixed essences. The objection argues that if a non-existent is described by non-existence, which is a negative attribute, then that non-existent must be negated (i.e., not fixed). The answer distinguishes between two meanings of 'negative attribute'. If it means the attribute itself is a pure negation, then it would entail the subject is negated, but non-existence is not pure negation; it is the contradictory of existence, which is broader. If it means a privative attribute like lacking spatiality, then even the Necessary Being (God) can be described by such attributes, and that does not negate His existence. So the objection fails.

Key Terms
المعدوم
العدم
صفة نفي
صفة ثبوت
الواجب
الصفات السلبية
الصفات العدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any description with a negative attribute implies the non-existence of the subject.
Correction: The answer shows that negative attributes of privation (like non-spatiality) can be applied to existents without negating their existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does describing the Necessary Being with negative attributes not negate His existence?
A) Because He is not a non-existent
B) Because negative attributes in that sense do not imply negation of the subject
C) Because He is described only by affirmative attributes
D) Because non-existence is not a real attribute

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it conflates two meanings of 'negative attribute'; only one would entail negation, but non-existence is not that type and privative attributes do not negate the subject.

The Objection of Infinite Fixed Essences and the Reply
Objection that infinite fixed essences lead to contradiction, with response using numbers analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam objection that infinite fixed essences lead to a contradiction and how the response refutes it using the nature of infinities and an analogy from numbers.

Core Idea
Adding a finite part to an infinite does not make it finite.

In this passage, the author presents a fourth objection against the doctrine of fixed essences: if they are infinite, but only a finite portion has come into existence, then the whole must be finite—a contradiction. The response first denies the premise that adding a finite amount to an infinite yields a finite, and then provides a striking analogy from the infinite levels of numbers. This lesson unpacks the logic of both the objection and its clever rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: objection, initial answer, subsidiary objection, final reply.
2
Identify the objection's core argument
The objection claims that because the part of essences that entered existence is finite, the whole must be finite. Identify the steps: (1) essences are infinite (by your claim), (2) the part that came into existence is finite, (3) so the whole is finite, contradicting infinitude.
3
Understand the initial reply
The reply denies that adding a finite amount to an infinite yields a finite. It says that the result is only finite if the other part were finite, but the remaining essences are also infinite.
This is a key insight: infinite plus finite is still infinite.
4
Examine the subsidiary objection and final reply
The opponent then argues that the remaining part is less than the whole, so matching would show finitude. The final reply uses the analogy of numbers (infinite levels) to show that infinites can have parts that are also infinite, and denies increase/decrease among infinites. Even if the infinitude argument fails, fixity remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الذوات الثابتة والعدم غير متناهية عندكم، هذا محال لأن القدر الذي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناه اتفاقاً، فيكون الكل أكثر من القدر الذي بقي على العدم بقدر متناه وهو القدر الذي دخل في الوجود، فيكون الكل متناهياً بكونه زائداً على الغير بقدر متناه . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم أن الزائد على الغير بقدر متنـاه يكون متناهياً، وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان ذلك الغير متناهياً وليس كذلك، لأن الباقية على العدم أيضاً غير متناهية كالكل . فإن قيل : هي أقل من الكل قطعاً، فينقطع عند التطبيق فيتناهى ويلزم تناهي الكل. فالجواب : النقض بمراتب الأعداد ، ومنع الزيادة والنقصان فيما بين غير المتناهين ولو سلم فلا يلزم من بطلان القول بعدم تناهيها بطلان القول بثبوتها .
The fourth: that the fixed essences (al-dhawat al-thabita) and non-existence are infinite according to you; this is impossible because the portion that has come into existence is finite by agreement; thus the whole is greater than the portion that remains in non-existence by a finite amount, which is the portion that entered into existence; thus the whole would be finite by being greater than the other by a finite amount. And the answer: we do not concede that what is greater than another by a finite amount is finite; it would be so only if that other were finite, but it is not the case, because the remaining entities in non-existence are also infinite like the whole. If it is said: they are certainly less than the whole, so they would come to an end when matched (bi-l-tatbiq) and thus become finite, necessitating the finitude of the whole. The reply: a refutation by the levels of numbers, and we deny increase and decrease between infinites; and even if granted, the invalidity of the claim of their infinitude does not necessitate the invalidity of the claim of their fixity.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This fourth objection argues that if fixed essences in non-existence are infinite, but the portion that came into existence is finite, then the total is finite. The answer denies that adding a finite amount to an infinite makes the sum finite; it remains infinite unless the other part is also finite. The subsidiary question applies a matching argument, but the answer counters using an analogy with the infinite hierarchy of numbers, showing that infinites do not become finite by adding a finite part. Moreover, even if the argument against their infinitude were sound, it would not refute their fixity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That adding a finite number to an infinite set always results in a finite or larger infinite.
Correction: In the kalam view, adding a finite amount to an infinite does not change its infinitude; infinite plus finite is still infinite. The example of numbers shows that infinite sets can have proper subsets that are also infinite without being 'less' in a way that leads to finitude.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the matching argument against infinitude?
A) The levels of numbers
B) The sky and earth
C) The attributes of God
D) The division of time

Key Takeaway
Adding a finite part to an infinite does not make the whole finite; the levels of numbers illustrate that infinites can have infinite subsets without contradiction.
