The Fourth Argument: Infinite Essences and Its Refutation
Argument and first refutation about infinite essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against Ash'ari subsistence of non-existents and the first refutation based on the nature of infinite quantities.

Core Idea
Adding finite to infinite does not yield finite; both parts remain infinite.

Al-Taftazani presents a key argument that the Ash'ari claim of infinite subsistent essences leads to contradiction. He argues that since the portion that came into existence is finite, the whole would be finite. The response, however, points out that this reasoning only works if the remainder is finite, but it is also infinite. This lesson explores that initial exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'الرابع' up to 'كالكل'. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument flow.
Notice the argument structure: claim, reason, conclusion, and the refutation.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The passage first states the opponent's claim that essences and non-existence are infinite. Then it presents an impossibility argument based on the finite existent part. Finally, the refutation denies that exceeding a part by a finite amount makes the whole finite.
The refutation pivots on the nature of the 'other' part: it is also infinite.
3
Understand the logic of infinite quantities
Reflect on why adding a finite amount to an infinite does not yield a finite. Consider the example of natural numbers: even numbers are infinite, adding odd numbers still yields infinite.
This counters the intuition that 'whole > part by finite' implies finiteness.
4
Reflect on the theological context
Think about why this argument matters for the Ash'ari position on non-existence. The subsistence of non-existents is a key point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الذوات الثابتة والعدم غير متناهية عندكم، هذا محال لأن القدر الذي خرج منها إلى الوجود متناه اتفاقاً، فيكون الكل أكثر من القدر الذي بقي على العدم بقدر متناه وهو القدر الذي دخل في الوجود، فيكون الكل متناهياً بكونه زائداً على الغير بقدر متناه .
وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم (١) أن الزائد على الغير بقدر متناه يكون متناهياً، وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان ذلك الغير متناهياً وليس كذلك، لأن الباقية على العدم أيضاً غير متناهية كالكل .
فإن قيل : هي أقل من الكل قطعاً، فينقطع عند التطبيق فيتناهى ويلزم تناهي الكل.
فالجواب : النقض بمراتب الأعداد (۳) ، ومنع الزيادة والنقصان فيما بين غير المتناهين ولو سلم فلا يلزم من بطلان القول بعدم تناهيها بطلان القول بثبوتها .
The fourth: That the subsistent essences and non-existence are infinite according to you; this is impossible because the amount that has come out from them into existence is finite by agreement, so the whole is greater than the amount that remained in non-existence by a finite amount, which is the amount that entered existence, so the whole is finite by virtue of being greater than the other by a finite amount. And the answer is: We do not concede that that which is greater than another by a finite amount is finite; rather it would be so only if that other were finite, but it is not, because what remains in non-existence is also infinite like the whole. If it is said: It is less than the whole definitely, so it terminates upon comparison and becomes finite, necessitating the finitude of the whole. The answer: The refutation by the orders of numbers, and the denial of increase and decrease among infinites, and even if admitted, the invalidity of the claim of their non-finiteness does not entail the invalidity of the claim of their subsistence.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth argument against the Ash'ari position that non-existents have some form of subsistence (thubut). The argument is structured as an objection and answer. First, it states the opponent's claim: that subsistent essences and non-existence are both infinite. This is deemed impossible because the portion that has come into existence is finite, so the whole (which includes both the existent and non-existent parts) would be the sum of a finite portion and an infinite remainder, making the whole infinite? Actually the reasoning: the whole is greater than the non-existent part by a finite amount (the existent part), so if the non-existent part is infinite, then the whole would be infinite plus finite, still infinite. But the arguer here concludes that the whole is finite because it exceeds the other by a finite amount. The answer refutes this by noting that the whole is only finite if the other (the non-existent part) is finite, but it is not; it is also infinite. Then an objection is raised: the non-existent part is less than the whole, so when compared (tatbiq), it must be finite, leading to the finitude of the whole. The answer responds by analogizing with the orders of numbers (like infinite series) and denying that increase or decrease applies among infinites. Even if the opponent's reasoning were accepted, the failure to prove the non-finiteness of the non-existent does not disprove its subsistence. The author is showing the limits of such mathematical reasoning about infinite quantities in metaphysical arguments.

Key Terms
الذوات الثابتة
العدم
متناه
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a whole exceeds a part by a finite amount, the whole must be finite.
Correction: This is only true if the part is finite; if the part is infinite, the whole is also infinite despite the finite excess.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in this passage, why does the argument that the whole is finite because it exceeds the non-existent part by a finite amount fail?
A) Because the existent part is not actually finite.
B) Because the non-existent part is also infinite, so the rule does not apply.
C) Because the whole is not a sum of parts.
D) Because the argument only works for infinite quantities.

Key Takeaway
The initial objection conflates finite addition with finitude of the whole; an infinite set remains infinite even after adding a finite amount.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This argument deals with the nature of non-existence. Reflect on a time you thought about something that does not yet exist. Did you consider it real in some sense? How does this affect your understanding of God's knowledge of all things?

Defending Infinite Essences: The Counter-Objection and Final Answer
Counter-objection and final answer on infinite essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-objection using comparison (tatbiq) and the final answer that denies increase/decrease among infinites and invokes the analogy of number orders.

Core Idea
Infinities do not become finite by being 'less' than another infinite; comparisons do not apply.

The opponent raises a strong counter: if the non-existent part is less than the whole, comparing them (tatbiq) should make it finite. Al-Taftazani responds with a powerful analogy from the orders of numbers and denies that increase or decrease applies between infinites. He also notes that even if the infinity argument fails, the subsistence of essences remains. This lesson unpacks that final exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the sentence beginning with 'فإن قيل' which claims that the non-existent part is less than the whole, so comparison makes it finite.
This is a plausible objection: if the part is smaller, shouldn't it be finite?
2
Analyze the final answer
Al-Taftazani gives three points: (1) refutation by the orders of numbers, (2) denial of increase/decrease among infinites, (3) even if the argument fails, subsistence remains.
The number orders example shows that infinite sets can have different sizes without being finite.
3
Understand the number orders analogy
Consider natural numbers and square numbers. Both are infinite, but natural numbers are 'more' in a sense. Yet neither is finite.
This shows that 'less' among infinites does not imply finitude.
4
Reflect on the final point
The last point is important: even if the opponent disproved that non-existents are infinite, it does not disprove that they have subsistence (thubut).
This separates the two issues: infinity and subsistence.

Source Text

The Fifth Argument: Non-Existence and Negation
Al-Taftazani presents the fifth argument on non-existence and negation with its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between non-existence and negation as argued in the fifth proof against the subsistence of non-existents, and the refutation of that proof.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not purely negation nor purely positive—it is a mixed concept.

This passage presents the fifth argument of the Imam (often Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the view that non-existents have some form of subsistence. The argument explores whether the concept of 'non-existent' is identical to, more specific than, or more general than 'negated'. Each possibility is examined, leading to a syllogism that seems to force a contradiction, but the answer shows that non-existence is a mixed concept, not purely positive or negative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage includes a syllogism and an answer.
2
Identify the three relations
The passage considers three logical relations between 'non-existent' and 'negated': equal, more specific, and more general. Find each one and note the conclusion for the first two.
The first two lead to the same conclusion: no non-existent is subsistent.
3
Follow the general case and syllogism
When non-existence is more general, the argument constructs a syllogism that leads to an impossibility. Trace how this syllogism is formed and why it is considered absurd.
The syllogism: every negation is non-existence, every non-existence is subsistent, so every negation is subsistent.
4
Understand the answer
The answer denies the inference that if non-existence is not pure negation, it must be purely positive. Instead, it is a concept with both subsistent and merely negated instances.
This resolves the contradiction without committing to all non-existents being subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أن المعدوم إما مساو للمنفي أو أخص منه أو أعم، إذ لا تباين لظهور التصادق، فإن كان مساوياً له أو أخص صدق كل معدوم منفي، ولا شيء من المنفي بثابت، فلا شيء من المعدوم بثابت. وإن كان أعم لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، وإلا لما بقى فرق بين العام والخاص بل ثابتاً. وقد صدق على المنفي فيلزم كونه ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الأمر الثابت ثابت، وهو باطل ضرورة استحالة صدق أحد (١) النقيضين على الآخر، هذا تقرير الإمام على اختلاف عباراته، وقد اعتبر في بعضها النسب بين العدم والنفي، ثم قال: وإذا لم يكن العدم نفياً صرفاً بل ثابتاً وهو صادق على النفي انتظم قياس هكذا كل نفي عدم وكل عدم ثابت فكل نفي ثابت. وهو محال . وأجيب عنه : بعبارات محصلها أنا لا نسلم (٢) أنه إذا لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، كان ثبوتياً محضاً. لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً بكون بعض أفراده ثابتاً كالمعدومات الممكنة، وبعضها منفياً كالممتنعات، وهذا القدر كاف في الفرق
The fifth: That non-existence is either equal to negation or more specific than it or more general, since there is no disparity due to the manifest agreement. For if it is equal to it or more specific, then every non-existent is a negated thing, and no negated thing is subsistent, so no non-existent is subsistent. And if it is more general, then it is not pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific; rather it is subsistent. And it has been true of the negated, so it entails that it is subsistent, necessarily because that of which a subsistent attribute is true is subsistent, and this is false due to the impossibility of one of the two contradictories being true of the other. This is the exposition of the Imam in his various expressions, and in some of them he considered the relation between non-existence and negation. Then he said: If non-existence is not pure negation but subsistent, and it is true of negation, then a syllogism is formed: every negation is non-existence, and every non-existence is subsistent, so every negation is subsistent. This is impossible. And it was answered with expressions whose gist is: We do not concede that if it is not pure negation, then it is purely positive. For it is possible that it is a concept such that some of its instances are subsistent, like possible non-existents, and some are negated, like impossible things, and this much is sufficient for the distinction.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against the subsistence of non-existents, attributed to the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). The argument explores the logical relationship between the concepts of 'non-existent' (ma'dum) and 'negated' (manfi). It begins by noting that these two concepts either are coextensive, or one is more specific, or one is more general, since they are not completely different. If 'non-existent' is equal to or more specific than 'negated', then everything non-existent is negated, and since no negated thing is subsistent (thabit), no non-existent is subsistent. If 'non-existent' is more general, then it cannot be pure negation, else there would be no distinction between the general and specific; rather it must be something positive (subsistent). Since 'non-existent' applies to the negated (as a more general term), it entails that the negated is subsistent, which is absurd because being negated and being subsistent are contradictory. The Imam then constructs a syllogism: if non-existence is not pure negation but subsistent, then every negation is non-existence and every non-existence is subsistent, so every negation is subsistent, which is impossible. The answer denies the inference: being non-pure-negation does not mean being purely positive (thubuti mahd). The term 'non-existence' may have some instances that are subsistent (like possible non-existents) and some that are merely negated (like impossibles). Thus the distinction holds without committing to all non-existents being subsistent.

Key Terms
المعدوم
المنفي
ثابت
نفي
إثبات
العموم والخصوص
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If 'non-existent' is not pure negation, it must be a positive reality (thubuti mahd).
Correction: The answer shows that 'non-existent' can be a mixed concept: some instances are subsistent (possibles), some are merely negated (impossibles). It is not purely positive.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the answer to the fifth argument?
A) That non-existence is always pure negation.
B) That non-existence cannot be more general than negation.
C) That non-existence may have some subsistent and some merely negated instances.
D) That the Imam's syllogism is valid.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument fails because non-existence is a concept that covers both subsistent non-existents (possibles) and purely negated ones (impossibles), so it is neither purely positive nor purely negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a sharp logical argument can fail when the categories are not precisely defined. In your own life, where have you assumed a simple binary (e.g., something is either true or false, good or bad) when a more nuanced reality exists? Will you commit to re-examining one such binary this week?
?
The refutation teaches that non-existence is not a single kind—some non-existents are merely lacking (impossible) while others are real possibilities waiting to be created. How does your trust in Allah's power and wisdom change when you realize that what has not yet happened is not just 'nothing' but a potential reality in His knowledge?
?
If you were to apply the logic of this argument to your own soul, do you treat your sins (which are non-existences of obedience) as simply negated (removed from reality) or as subsistent (they leave a real mark)? The answer might determine whether you feel the weight of repentance or see it as merely erasing a blank slate. Which view dominates your heart, and what will you do to align it with the truth?

The Ilzami Objection and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes a dialectical objection to the fifth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical objection raised by Sahib al-Mawaqif and how al-Taftazani refutes it by exposing its flawed logic and irrelevance.

Core Idea
Dialectical objections must be assessed for logical soundness, not merely accepted as valid.

In this passage, al-Taftazani responds to a methodological challenge from the author of al-Mawaqif, who claims that the fifth argument for the subsistence of non-existents is merely dialectical. The response clarifies the original argument's intent and critiques the logic of the dialectical reformulation. This exchange illuminates the difference between direct proof and polemical reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the claim of Sahib al-Mawaqif that the argument is dialectical. Notice his reformulation: if non-existents are subsistent, then 'non-existent' is more general than 'negated' and distinct, so it must be subsistent.
The objection uses the opponent's own premises against them.
2
Understand the dialectical structure
Identify why Sahib al-Mawaqif considers this dialectical: it forces the opponent to accept an unwelcome conclusion (that the negated is subsistent) by using their own claims. Recognize the shift from direct proof to polemical force.
The original argument was presented as a direct proof, not as a dialectical one.
3
Follow the rebuttal
Read al-Taftazani's response carefully. He first points out that the original answer was based on a different exposition (that of the Imam). Then he argues that even if the objection were dialectical, many premises would be superfluous. Finally, he shows the objection has no real connection to subsistence.
Pay attention to the critique of specificity and generality.
4
Identify the logical flaw
Notice al-Taftazani's key criticism: the objection wrongly moves from 'non-existent is more general than negated' to 'non-existent is subsistent' while ignoring that distinctness exists in both cases. The logic is inconsistent.
The objection fails because it does not properly handle generality and specificity.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this exchange matters: it shows the importance of distinguishing between direct proof and dialectical argumentation. A good response must address the actual structure of the argument, not just its surface.
This lesson illustrates a common methodological debate in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم صاحب المواقف أن الاستدلال إلزامي تقريره : أنه لو كان المعدوم (٤) ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم من المنفي، وكان متميزاً عنه، فكان ثابتاً، لأن كل متميز ثابت عندكم، وقد صدق المعدوم على المنفي فيكون ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الوصف الثبوتي فهو ثابت ولا خفاء في أن الجواب المذكور لا يتأتى على هذا التقدير، فمن أورده لم يتفطن بمراد المستدل، وكون كلامه إلزامياً .
فنقول : الجواب المذكور إنما أورد على تقرير الإمام، ولا أثر فيه لحديث الإلزام، على أنه لو قصد ذلك لكانت أكثر المقدمات لغواً. إذ يكفي أن يقال : لو لم يكن المعدوم والمنفي واحداً لكان المنفي متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً، على أن الحق أنه لا تعلق لهذا الإلزام بكون المعدوم ثابتاً. إذ يقال: لو كان المنفي مبايناً للموجود كان متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً. وليت شعري كيف جعل خصوص المعدوم مستلزماً لكونه منفياً، وعمومه مستلزماً لكونه ثابتاً مع قيام التميز في الحالين فإن قيل على التقريرين (٦) : لما كان زعم الخصم ثبوت
And the author of al-Mawaqif claimed that the argument is dialectical. His exposition: That if the non-existent were subsistent, then the non-existent would be more general than the negated, and distinct from it, so it would be subsistent, because everything distinct is subsistent according to you, and the non-existent is true of the negated, so it is subsistent necessarily because that of which a positive attribute is true is subsistent. And it is not hidden that the mentioned answer is not viable upon this estimation, so whoever presented it did not comprehend the intention of the arguer and that his speech is dialectical. So we say: The mentioned answer was only given upon the exposition of the Imam, and there is no trace in it of the notion of dialectical force. Moreover, if he intended that, most of the premises would be superfluous, for it would suffice to say: If the non-existent and the negated were not identical, then the negated would be distinct from it, and would be subsistent. Except that the truth is that this dialectical force has no connection to the subsistence of the non-existent. For it could be said: If the negated were different from the existent, it would be distinct from it and subsistent. And would that I knew how he made the specificity of the non-existent entail its being negated, and its generality entail its being subsistent, while distinctness obtains in both cases. For if it is said upon the two estimations: Because the opponent claims subsistence...
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a further exchange. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) objects that the fifth argument is not a direct proof but a dialectical (ilzami) argument, i.e., one that forces the opponent to accept an unwelcome conclusion by using the opponent's own premises. He reformulates the argument: if the opponent claims non-existents are subsistent, then 'non-existent' is more general than 'negated' and distinct, so it is subsistent (since every distinct entity is subsistent according to the opponent). The non-existent applies to the negated, making the negated subsistent, which the opponent denies. The original answer failed to address this dialectical structure. The author responds: the original answer was given based on the Imam's exposition, not the dialectical version. Moreover, if the argument were truly dialectical, many of its premises would be unnecessary; a simpler formulation suffices. More importantly, the dialectical objection actually has no bearing on whether non-existents are subsistent; it employs flawed reasoning about specificity and generality in the context of distinctness. The author criticizes the illogical move from generality to subsistence and specificity to negation while ignoring that distinctness is present in both cases. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing the refutation.

Key Terms
إلزامي
تقرير
متميز
وصف ثبوتي
صاحب المواقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an argument uses the opponent's premises, it is necessarily dialectical and therefore invalid.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that even if the argument were dialectical, the objection's logic is flawed and does not refute the original proof. Dialectical arguments can still be logically sound.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Taftazani's response to Sahib al-Mawaqif?
A) The original answer was dialectical and thus invalid.
B) The dialectical objection is irrelevant and uses flawed logic.
C) The argument from al-Mawaqif proves non-existents are subsistent.
D) The author concedes the original answer was insufficient.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes the dialectical objection by exposing its flawed logic and showing its irrelevance to the original proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a clever argument that seems to force you into a corner, do you instinctively retreat or do you patiently dissect its logic, as al-Taftazani does? What would it take for you to develop that habit of critical scrutiny in matters of faith?
?
Al-Taftazani exposes that the dialectical objection relies on a flawed inference about specificity and generality. Are there beliefs you hold that rest on similar logical leaps you have never examined? Will you commit to revisiting one such belief this week with the same precision?
?
This lesson shows that even respected scholars can make logical errors. How does that challenge your trust in intellectual authorities? At the same time, how does al-Taftazani's careful refutation demonstrate the proper use of reason in defending truth? How can you balance critical thinking with respect for scholars in your own journey?

The Imam's Argument: Non-Existence Must Be Pure Negation
Imam's syllogism linking non-existence, negation, and positivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical syllogism the Imam uses to argue that non-existence (al-'adam) cannot be a positive attribute, because that would make negation positive as well.

Core Idea
If non-existence were positive, negation would become positive — a contradiction.

The Imam begins by presenting a dense argument that non-existence is not a positive attribute but pure negation. He constructs a syllogism to show that if non-existence were positive, then negation would also be positive, which is impossible. This lesson unpacks his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Imam's syllogism
Read the Arabic passage that contains the Imam's core argument. Then follow the translation. Focus on the structure: non-existence as predicate, its relation to negation.
The Imam uses a conditional: if non-existence is not pure negation, then it is positive.
2
Identify the three propositions
The Imam states a syllogism: every negation is non-existence, every non-existence is positive, therefore every negation is positive. Identify each proposition in the text.
The conclusion is said to be impossible (muhal).
3
Understand why the conclusion is impossible
The Imam asserts that the conclusion is absurd because it would mean that a negative concept (negation) is positive, which violates the law of non-contradiction.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفياً صرفاً، وإلا لما بقى فرق بين العام والخاص بل ثابتاً. وقد صدق على المنفي فيلزم كونه ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الأمر الثابت ثابت، وهو باطل ضرورة استحالة صدق أحد النقيضين على الآخر، هذا تقرير الإمام على اختلاف عباراته، وقد اعتبر في بعضها النسب بين العدم والنفي، ثم قال: وإذا لم يكن العدم نفياً صرفاً بل ثابتاً وهو صادق على النفي انتظم قياس هكذا كل نفي عدم وكل عدم ثابت فكل نفي ثابت. وهو محال . وأجيب عنه : بعبارات محصلها أنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، كان ثبوتياً محضاً. لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً بكون بعض أفراده ثابتاً كالمعدومات الممكنة، وبعضها منفياً كالممتنعات، وهذا القدر كاف في الفرق، وحينئذ لا يصدق أن كل معدوم ثابت ليلزم كون المنفي ثابتاً وزعم صاحب المواقف أن الاستدلال إلزامي تقريره : أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم من المنفي، وكان متميزاً عنه، فكان ثابتاً، لأن كل متميز ثابت عندكم، وقد صدق المعدوم على المنفي فيكون ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الوصف الثبوتي فهو ثابت ولا خفاء في أن الجواب المذكور لا يتأتى على هذا التقدير، فمن أورده لم يتفطن بمراد المستدل، وكون كلامه إلزامياً . فنقول : الجواب المذكور إنما أورد على تقرير الإمام، ولا أثر فيه لحديث الإلزام، على أنه لو قصد ذلك لكانت أكثر المقدمات لغواً. إذ يكفي أن يقال : لو لم يكن المعدوم والمنفي واحداً لكان المنفي متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً، على أن الحق أنه لا تعلق لهذا الإلزام بكون المعدوم ثابتاً. إذ يقال: لو كان المنفي مبايناً للموجود كان متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً. وليت شعري كيف جعل خصوص المعدوم مستلزماً لكونه منفياً، وعمومه مستلزماً لكونه ثابتاً مع قيام التميز في الحالين فإن قيل على التقريرين : لما كان زعم الخصم ثبوت المعدوم : فأي حاجة للمستدل إلى إثبات ذلك بالتكلف، ليفرع عليه ثبوت المنفي ؟ . وهلا قال : من أول الأمر - لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً وهو صادق على المنفي لزم ثبوته . قلنا : زعمه أن الذوات المعدومة الممكنة ثابتة ومقصود المستدل إثبات : أن الوصف الذي هو المعدوم المطلق ثابت منه ثبوت موصوفه . وإلى هذا يشير قول المواقف : لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم بإعادة لفظ المعدوم دون ضميره . ألا بری أن ما آل إليه كلامه، أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً. لكـان المعدوم ثابتاً فلو لم يرد الموصوف وبالثاني الوصف لكان لغواً. ومما يجب التنبه له : أن المراد بالأعم في تقرير الإمام ، ما يشمل العموم المطلق، والعموم من وجه ليتم الحصر، وفي تقرير المواقف يجوز أن يحمل على المطلق ويبين الملازمة بأنه صادق على كل منفي
Pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific, but rather it would be positive. And it has been predicated of the negated thing, so it follows that it is positive, necessarily because whatever a positive thing is predicated of is positive, and that is false due to the impossibility of one of the two contradictories being true of the other. This is the Imam's exposition according to his various expressions, and in some of them he considered the relation between non-existence and negation. Then he said: 'If non-existence is not pure negation but rather positive and is true of negation, then a syllogism follows: every negation is non-existence, every non-existence is positive, so every negation is positive. That is impossible.' And it was answered with expressions whose gist is: we do not grant that if it is not pure negation it is purely positive. Because it is possible that the concept [of non-existence] includes some individuals that are positive, like possible non-existent things, and some that are negated, like impossible things, and this is sufficient for the distinction. Then it does not follow that every non-existent is positive, so that the negated would be positive. And the author of al-Mawaqif claimed that the inference is illzami (obligatory), its exposition: if the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be more general than negation, and would be distinct from it, so it would be positive, because everything distinct is positive according to you. And non-existence has been predicated of negation, so it is positive, necessarily because whatever a positive attribute is predicated of is positive. There is no concealment that the mentioned answer does not hold on this supposition, so whoever presented it did not understand the intention of the arguer, and that his speech is illzami. We say: the mentioned answer was only presented on the exposition of the Imam, and it has no effect regarding the binding argument; even if he intended that, most of the premises would be superfluous. It would suffice to say: if the non-existent and the negated were not one, then the negated would be distinct from it and would be positive. However, the truth is that this binding argument has no connection to the non-existent being positive. For it is said: if the negated were different from the existent, it would be distinct from it and would be positive. Woe to me! How did he make the particularity of non-existence necessitate its being negated, and its generality necessitate its being positive, while distinctness exists in both cases? If it is said on the two expositions: since the opponent claims the positivity of the non-existent, what need does the arguer have to prove that with effort in order to branch off from it the positivity of the negated? And why did he not say from the beginning: if the non-existent were positive and it is true of negation, its positivity follows? We say: his claim is that the possible non-existent essences are positive, and the intention of the arguer is to prove that the attribute which is absolute non-existence is positive, from which follows the positivity of its subject. And to this points the statement of al-Mawaqif: 'If the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be more general by repeating the word non-existence without its pronoun.' Do you not see that his speech amounts to: if the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be positive. So if he did not intend the subject by the first and the attribute by the second, it would be meaningless. And it is necessary to note: what is meant by 'more general' in the Imam's exposition includes absolute generality and generality from one aspect to complete the counting, and in the exposition of al-Mawaqif it can be taken as absolute and the entailment explained by its being true of every negated thing.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by the Imam (likely a significant Ash'ari theologian) regarding the nature of non-existence (`adam). The Imam claims that if non-existence were not pure negation (`adam mahḍ) but a positive concept (thābit), then since it applies to negation (nafy), negation would also be positive, leading to a contradiction. The opponent responds by denying that non-existence must be purely positive if not pure negation; it could be a concept with some positive individuals (possible non-existents) and some negative (impossibilities). The author of al-Mawaqif then criticizes this reply, arguing that the Imam's reasoning is illzāmī (a binding argument that the opponent must accept on his own premises). The author defends the original reply, explaining that the binding argument does not cohere with the Imam's exposition and that the intended point is about the attribute of absolute non-existence being positive, not the subject. The passage ends with a note on the interpretation of 'more general' in both expositions.

Key Terms
عدم
نفي
ثابت
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think non-existence is a simple label without logical consequences.
Correction: The Imam shows that if non-existence were positive, it would entail that negation itself is positive, which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the Imam's syllogism?
A) Non-existence is pure negation.
B) Every negation is positive.
C) Non-existence and negation are identical.
D) The general and specific are the same.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's syllogism demonstrates that non-existence must be pure negation, otherwise negation would become positive, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

In your daily life, do you ever speak of non-existent things as if they have some reality (e.g., 'the absence of rain is a problem')? How does this lesson challenge you to think more precisely about what is real and what is not? Will you commit to using 'non-existence' only as pure negation from now on?

This lesson shows that even logical reasoning can protect us from absurd conclusions. When you rely on reason to understand faith, are you willing to accept conclusions that contradict clear logic? Identify one area where you have resisted logical clarity and decide today to embrace it.

The Response: Non-Existence as a Complex Concept
The opponent's refutation of the Imam's syllogism by denying its second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's response, which denies that non-existence is either pure negation or purely positive, claiming it is a concept with both positive and negative instances.

Core Idea
Non-existence is not purely positive; it includes both possible (positive) and impossible (negative) instances.

The opponent responds to the Imam's dilemma by rejecting the forced choice. While non-existence is not pure negation, it need not be purely positive either. Instead, it is a concept that encompasses both types of instances: possible non-existents (which are 'positive' in some sense) and impossible non-existents (which are pure negation). This distinction suffices to block the Imam's syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's answer
Carefully read the Arabic passage presenting the response. The key is the denial that non-existence is purely positive if not pure negation.
The opponent says 'we do not grant' (la nusallimu).
2
Analyze the concept of non-existence
The opponent explains that non-existence is a concept with two kinds of individuals: possible non-existents (like non-existent contingent things) are 'positive' in the sense of being conceivable, while impossible non-existents (like square circles) are pure negations.
This shows that non-existence is not a single positive attribute.
3
See how it undermines the syllogism
If non-existence is not uniformly positive, then the premise 'every non-existence is positive' is false. Therefore the conclusion that every negation is positive does not follow.
The opponent's answer preserves the distinction between the general and the specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It would suffice to say: if the non-existent and the negated were not one, then the negated would be distinct from it and would be positive. However, the truth is that this binding argument has no connection to the non-existent being positive. For it is said: if the negated were different from the existent, it would be distinct from it and would be positive. Woe to me! How did he make the particularity of non-existence necessitate its being negated, and its generality necessitate its being positive, while distinctness exists in both cases? If it is said on the two expositions: since the opponent claims the positivity of the non-existent, what need does the arguer have to prove that with effort in order to branch off from it the positivity of the negated? And why did he not say from the beginning: if the non-existent were positive and it is true of negation, its positivity follows? We say: his claim is that the possible non-existent essences are positive, and the intention of the arguer is to prove that the attribute which is absolute non-existence is positive, from which follows the positivity of its subject. And to this points the statement of al-Mawaqif: 'If the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be more general by repeating the word non-existence without its pronoun.' Do you not see that his speech amounts to: if the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be positive. So if he did not intend the subject by the first and the attribute by the second, it would be meaningless. And it is necessary to note: what is meant by 'more general' in the Imam's exposition includes absolute generality and generality from one aspect to complete the counting, and in the exposition of al-Mawaqif it can be taken as absolute and the entailment explained by its being true of every negated thing.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents an argument by the Imam (likely a significant Ash'ari theologian) regarding the nature of non-existence (`adam). The Imam claims that if non-existence were not pure negation (`adam mahḍ) but a positive concept (thābit), then since it applies to negation (nafy), negation would also be positive, leading to a contradiction. The opponent responds by denying that non-existence must be purely positive if not pure negation; it could be a concept with some positive individuals (possible non-existents) and some negative (impossibilities). The author of al-Mawaqif then criticizes this reply, arguing that the Imam's reasoning is illzāmī (a binding argument that the opponent must accept on his own premises). The author defends the original reply, explaining that the binding argument does not cohere with the Imam's exposition and that the intended point is about the attribute of absolute non-existence being positive, not the subject. The passage ends with a note on the interpretation of 'more general' in both expositions.

ثابت
ممكن
ممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if something is not pure negation, it must be a positive attribute.
Correction: The opponent shows that a concept can have both positive and negative instances, so it is not purely positive.

Checkpoint
What is the opponent's main point in response to the Imam?
A) Non-existence is purely positive.
B) Non-existence is a concept with both positive and negative instances.
C) Non-existence is identical to negation.
D) The Imam's syllogism is valid but the conclusion is true.

Key Takeaway
The opponent refutes the Imam's syllogism by denying that non-existence is uniformly positive, showing it is a concept with mixed instances.

?

Al-Mawaqif's Binding Argument and the Final Analysis
Al-Mawaqif's binding argument and the author's defense with final clarification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Mawaqif transforms the argument into a binding (ilzāmī) proof based on distinctness, and how the author defends the original response.

Core Idea
Al-Mawaqif's binding argument: if non-existence is distinct from negation, then it is positive, but the original answer still holds.

The author of al-Mawaqif criticizes the opponent's response, claiming that the Imam's argument is actually a binding argument (ilzām) that works on the opponent's own principles. He reconstructs it using the concept of distinctness (tamayyuz). The author then defends the original answer, showing that the binding argument misses the mark and that the true focus is on the attribute of absolute non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Mawaqif's criticism
Read the part where al-Mawaqif claims the inference is binding. Note that he uses the opponent's own principle: everything distinct is positive (thābit).
He says 'every distinct thing is positive according to you'.
2
Understand the author's defense
The author responds that the binding argument does not apply to the Imam's original exposition. He points out that if the intention were binding, most premises would be superfluous.
The author argues that the binding argument is about the difference between non-existent and existent, not non-existent and negation.
3
Grasp the key distinction: attribute vs. subject
The author clarifies that the Imam's aim was to prove that the attribute 'absolute non-existence' is positive, not the subject. This resolves the confusion.
Al-Mawaqif's wording hints at this: repeating 'non-existence' without pronoun indicates subject and attribute.

نفياً صرفاً، وإلا لما بقى فرق بين العام والخاص بل ثابتاً. وقد صدق على المنفي فيلزم كونه ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الأمر الثابت ثابت، وهو باطل ضرورة استحالة صدق أحد النقيضين على الآخر، هذا تقرير الإمام على اختلاف عباراته، وقد اعتبر في بعضها النسب بين العدم والنفي، ثم قال: وإذا لم يكن العدم نفياً صرفاً بل ثابتاً وهو صادق على النفي انتظم قياس هكذا كل نفي عدم وكل عدم ثابت فكل نفي ثابت. وهو محال . وأجيب عنه : بعبارات محصلها أنا لا نسلم أنه إذا لم يكن نفياً صرفاً، كان ثبوتياً محضاً. لجواز أن يكون مفهوماً بكون بعض أفراده ثابتاً كالمعدومات الممكنة، وبعضها منفياً كالممتنعات، وهذا القدر كاف في الفرق، وحينئذ لا يصدق أن كل معدوم ثابت ليلزم كون المنفي ثابتاً وزعم صاحب المواقف أن الاستدلال إلزامي تقريره : أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم من المنفي، وكان متميزاً عنه، فكان ثابتاً، لأن كل متميز ثابت عندكم، وقد صدق المعدوم على المنفي فيكون ثابتاً ضرورة أن ما صدق عليه الوصف الثبوتي فهو ثابت ولا خفاء في أن الجواب المذكور لا يتأتى على هذا التقدير، فمن أورده لم يتفطن بمراد المستدل، وكون كلامه إلزامياً . فنقول : الجواب المذكور إنما أورد على تقرير الإمام، ولا أثر فيه لحديث الإلزام، على أنه لو قصد ذلك لكانت أكثر المقدمات لغواً. إذ يكفي أن يقال : لو لم يكن المعدوم والمنفي واحداً لكان المنفي متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً، على أن الحق أنه لا تعلق لهذا الإلزام بكون المعدوم ثابتاً. إذ يقال: لو كان المنفي مبايناً للموجود كان متميزاً عنه، وكان ثابتاً. وليت شعري كيف جعل خصوص المعدوم مستلزماً لكونه منفياً، وعمومه مستلزماً لكونه ثابتاً مع قيام التميز في الحالين فإن قيل على التقريرين : لما كان زعم الخصم ثبوت المعدوم : فأي حاجة للمستدل إلى إثبات ذلك بالتكلف، ليفرع عليه ثبوت المنفي ؟ . وهلا قال : من أول الأمر - لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً وهو صادق على المنفي لزم ثبوته . قلنا : زعمه أن الذوات المعدومة الممكنة ثابتة ومقصود المستدل إثبات : أن الوصف الذي هو المعدوم المطلق ثابت منه ثبوت موصوفه . وإلى هذا يشير قول المواقف : لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم بإعادة لفظ المعدوم دون ضميره . ألا بری أن ما آل إليه كلامه، أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً. لكـان المعدوم ثابتاً فلو لم يرد الموصوف وبالثاني الوصف لكان لغواً. ومما يجب التنبه له : أن المراد بالأعم في تقرير الإمام ، ما يشمل العموم المطلق، والعموم من وجه ليتم الحصر، وفي تقرير المواقف يجوز أن يحمل على المطلق ويبين الملازمة بأنه صادق على كل منفي
Pure negation, otherwise there would be no difference between the general and the specific, but rather it would be positive. And it has been predicated of the negated thing, so it follows that it is positive, necessarily because whatever a positive thing is predicated of is positive, and that is false due to the impossibility of one of the two contradictories being true of the other. This is the Imam's exposition according to his various expressions, and in some of them he considered the relation between non-existence and negation. Then he said: 'If non-existence is not pure negation but rather positive and is true of negation, then a syllogism follows: every negation is non-existence, every non-existence is positive, so every negation is positive. That is impossible.' And it was answered with expressions whose gist is: we do not grant that if it is not pure negation it is purely positive. Because it is possible that the concept [of non-existence] includes some individuals that are positive, like possible non-existent things, and some that are negated, like impossible things, and this is sufficient for the distinction. Then it does not follow that every non-existent is positive, so that the negated would be positive. And the author of al-Mawaqif claimed that the inference is illzami (obligatory), its exposition: if the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be more general than negation, and would be distinct from it, so it would be positive, because everything distinct is positive according to you. And non-existence has been predicated of negation, so it is positive, necessarily because whatever a positive attribute is predicated of is positive. There is no concealment that the mentioned answer does not hold on this supposition, so whoever presented it did not understand the intention of the arguer, and that his speech is illzami. We say: the mentioned answer was only presented on the exposition of the Imam, and it has no effect regarding the binding argument; even if he intended that, most of the premises would be superfluous. It would suffice to say: if the non-existent and the negated were not one, then the negated would be distinct from it and would be positive. However, the truth is that this binding argument has no connection to the non-existent being positive. For it is said: if the negated were different from the existent, it would be distinct from it and would be positive. Woe to me! How did he make the particularity of non-existence necessitate its being negated, and its generality necessitate its being positive, while distinctness exists in both cases? If it is said on the two expositions: since the opponent claims the positivity of the non-existent, what need does the arguer have to prove that with effort in order to branch off from it the positivity of the negated? And why did he not say from the beginning: if the non-existent were positive and it is true of negation, its positivity follows? We say: his claim is that the possible non-existent essences are positive, and the intention of the arguer is to prove that the attribute which is absolute non-existence is positive, from which follows the positivity of its subject. And to this points the statement of al-Mawaqif: 'If the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be more general by repeating the word non-existence without its pronoun.' Do you not see that his speech amounts to: if the non-existent were positive, then non-existence would be positive. So if he did not intend the subject by the first and the attribute by the second, it would be meaningless. And it is necessary to note: what is meant by 'more general' in the Imam's exposition includes absolute generality and generality from one aspect to complete the counting, and in the exposition of al-Mawaqif it can be taken as absolute and the entailment explained by its being true of every negated thing.

Two Proofs for the Existence of Non-Existents and the Refutation
First two proofs for the existence of non-existents and the response distinguishing mental and real existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments for the existence of non-existents (shay'iyyat al-ma'dum) and how the author refutes them by distinguishing mental from real existence.

Core Idea
Being known or possible does not require real existence; mental existence is sufficient.

Some theologians argued that non-existent things have some kind of positive existence because they are known, distinct, and possibly existent. In this passage, the author presents two such arguments and then gives a unified refutation based on the distinction between mental existence and external reality. This is a key debate in Islamic theology about the nature of non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: two proofs introduced with 'الأول' and 'الثاني', followed by the refutation beginning with 'قلنا'.
The passage is compact; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Identify the first proof
Locate the first argument: the non-existent is distinct (mutamayyiz) because it is known, willed, and subject to power. The opponent claims that distinctness implies real existence (thubut 'ayni). Ask yourself: why does the opponent think distinction requires external existence?
They argue that mental indication to pure nothing is impossible, so the non-existent must have some reality.
3
Identify the second proof
Find the second argument: the non-existent is possible (mumkin), and possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute, therefore the possible thing must have real existence. Reflect on why possibility is considered a positive attribute.
4
Understand the refutation
Read the reply: distinction and possibility are mental attributes (umur 'aqliyya); they only require mental existence (thubut dhihni). If they required real existence, then impossibilities (mumtani'at) and imaginary composites would also have to exist externally, which is absurd. Grasp how the author uses a reductio ad absurdum.
The key is that impossibilities are also mentally distinct but cannot exist externally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة القائلين بشيئية المعدوم والرد عليهم (قال: تمسك المخالف بوجوه الأول : أن المعدوم متميز، لأنه معلوم ومراد ومقدور وكل متميز ثابت. لأن التميز إنما يكون بالإشارة العقلية والإشارة إلى النفي الصرف محال . الثاني: أنه ممكن وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان ثبوتي. قلنا كل من التميز والإمكان عقلي يكفيه ثبوت المتميز والممكن في الذهن، ولو اقتضيا الثبوت عيناً لزم ثبوت الممتنعات لتميزها، والمركبات الخياليات التميزها وإمكانها .
The proofs of those who affirm the existence of the non-existent and the refutation of them. (He said: the opponent clung to aspects: First: that the non-existent is distinct, because it is known, willed, and capable, and everything distinct is positive. For distinction is only by mental indication, and indication to pure negation is impossible. Second: that it is possible, and everything possible is positive because possibility is positive. We said: both distinction and possibility are mental; the existence of the distinct and the possible in the mind suffices for them. And if they necessitated real existence, then the existence of impossibilities would follow due to their distinction, and also of imaginary composites due to their distinction and possibility.)
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage lists two arguments (wujūh) used by those who claim that non-existent things have some sort of existence (shay'iyyat al-ma'dūm). The first argument: the non-existent is distinct (mutamayyiz) because it can be known, willed, and subject to divine power; everything distinct must be positive (thābit). They argue that distinction entails mental indication, and you cannot mentally indicate pure nothingness. The second argument: the non-existent is possible (mumkin), and possibility is a positive attribute, so the possible must be positive in reality. The author refutes both with a single reply: both distinction and possibility are mental (ʿaqlī). They only require the existence of the distinct or possible thing in the mind (dhihn), not in external reality. If they required external (real) existence, then impossible things (mumtaniʿāt) would also have real existence because they too are known and distinct, as well as imaginary composites (murakkabāt khayāliyya) which are also distinct and possible in the mind. Thus the argument fails.

إمكان
ثبوت ذهني
ممتنعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is known, distinct, or possible, it must have real existence outside the mind.
Correction: Distinction and possibility are mental attributes; they do not imply external existence. Mental existence is sufficient for these attributes, as shown by the fact that impossibilities and imaginary composites are also mentally distinct but do not exist externally.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that distinction (tamayyuz) entails real existence?
A) By denying that non-existent things are distinct.
B) By arguing that distinction is mental and does not require external existence.
C) By pointing out that impossible things are also distinct.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Distinction and possibility are mental attributes; they do not require external existence, otherwise impossibilities would also exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

How can this free you from unnecessary anxiety about imagined futures? What one imagined burden can you release today?

Critique of the Claim that 'Non-Existent' is More General
Al-Taftazani critiques an argument about the generality of 'non-existent'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical distinction between the description (wasf) and the described (mawsuf) in arguments about the non-existent, and why al-Taftazani critiques the claim that 'non-existent' is more general.

Core Idea
The key to the critique is distinguishing 'non-existent' as a description from 'non-existent' as a described entity.

This passage from al-Taftazani delves into a subtle logical point in the debate over the status of the non-existent. The arguer tries to prove the establishment of the non-existent by claiming the term 'non-existent' is more general. Al-Taftazani questions the need for a convoluted proof and clarifies that the arguer's real aim is different. He then explains the confusion between the description and the described, which is central to the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm and critique come through.
Notice the question-and-answer style.
2
Identify the arguer's claim and al-Taftazani's objection
Find where the arguer claims that 'non-existent' is more general and al-Taftazani questions why a complicated proof is needed.
The simpler argument proposed is: if the non-existent is established and applies to the negated, then the negated is established.
3
Analyze the distinction between wasf and mawsuf
Focus on the repetition of 'ma'dum' in al-Mawaqif's statement. Understand that the first occurrence refers to the described (mawsuf) and the second to the description (wasf).
Al-Taftazani says if it were the other way, it would be pointless.
4
Grasp the two interpretations of 'a'am'
Note that al-Imam (Razi) means both absolute generality and generality from one aspect, while al-Mawaqif means absolute generality, showing entailment by applying to every negated thing.
This completes the logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعدوم : فأي حاجة للمستدل إلى إثبات ذلك بالتكلف، ليفرع عليه ثبوت المنفي؟ وهلا قال : من أول الأمر - لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً وهو صادق على المنفي لزم ثبوته . قلنا : زعمه أن الذوات المعدومة الممكنة ثابتة ومقصود المستدل إثبات : أن الوصف الذي هو المعدوم المطلق ثابت منه ثبوت موصوفه . وإلى هذا يشير قول المواقف : لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً كان المعدوم أعم بإعادة لفظ المعدوم دون ضميره . ألا ترى أن ما آل إليه كلامه، أنه لو كان المعدوم ثابتاً. لكن المعدوم ثابتاً فلو لم يرد الموصوف وبالثاني الوصف لكان لغواً. ومما يجب التنبه له : أن المراد بالأعم في تقرير الإمام ، ما يشمل العموم المطلق، والعموم من وجه ليتم الحصر، وفي تقرير المواقف يجوز أن يحمل على المطلق ويبين الملازمة بأنه صادق على كل منفي
The non-existent: So what need does the arguer have to prove that with difficulty, in order to base upon it the establishment of the negated? And why did he not say from the beginning: 'If the non-existent were established, and it is true of the negated, then its establishment would be entailed'? We said: His claim is that possible non-existent essences are established, and the arguer's purpose is to prove that the description which is the absolute non-existent, from its establishment follows the establishment of its described. And to this points the statement of al-Mawaqif: 'If the non-existent were established, then the non-existent would be more general,' by repeating the word 'non-existent' without the pronoun. Do you not see that what his statement amounts to is that if the non-existent were established, but the non-existent is established; if he did not intend the described by the first [occurrence] and the description by the second, it would be pointless. And it must be noted that what is meant by 'more general' in the exposition of al-Imam is that it includes absolute generality and generality from one aspect, to complete the enumeration, while in the exposition of al-Mawaqif it can be interpreted as absolute, and he shows the entailment by saying that it is true of every negated [thing].
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of an argument that tries to prove the establishment of the non-existent by claiming that the term 'non-existent' is more general (a'am) and therefore includes the negated. The author questions why the arguer goes through a complicated proof when he could simply say: if the non-existent is established and it applies to the negated (manfī), then the negated is established. The author then explains that the arguer's claim is that possible non-existent essences are established, and his purpose is to show that from the establishment of the description (the absolute non-existent) follows the establishment of the described (the specific non-existent). He refers to al-Mawaqif's statement that if the non-existent were established, then the non-existent would be more general (by repeating the word without a pronoun). The author points out that if the first 'non-existent' meant the described and the second meant the description, it would be pointless. He then distinguishes between two interpretations: al-Imam (likely Razi) meant general in the sense of including absolute generality and generality from one aspect, while al-Mawaqif meant absolute generality, showing the entailment by saying it applies to every negated thing. This passage clarifies a subtle point in the logic of the argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'non-existent' in the argument always refers to the same thing.
Correction: Actually, the arguer uses 'non-existent' twice: first as the described (the specific non-existent entity), and second as the description (absolute non-existence). Al-Taftazani exposes this confusion.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what does the repetition of the word 'ma'dum' (non-existent) in al-Mawaqif's statement indicate?
A) That it refers to two different things.
B) That it refers to the description, not the described.
C) That it refers to the described, not the description.
D) That it is a redundant expression.

Key Takeaway
The critical point is that the arguer's claim conflates the description of non-existence with the specific non-existent entity, and al-Taftazani exposes this confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as a 'sinner', do you treat that description as an inescapable identity (a described entity) or as a temporary attribute (a description) that can be changed through repentance? How does conflating the two affect your hope in Allah's mercy?
?
In what areas of your belief or life do you assume that because a general concept is true (e.g., 'Everything is from Allah'), all specific instances must follow a certain rule, ignoring the nuances that the scholars like al-Taftazani highlight? How can you train yourself to be more precise in your thinking?
?
The arguer in this passage wanted to prove something about the non-existent through a complicated proof. When you try to defend your faith or understand a difficult issue, do you sometimes rely on convoluted reasoning instead of simply accepting clear truths? How might this lesson call you to simplify your approach to Allah's guidance?

6 min
Refutation of the First Two Arguments for the Thingness of the Non-Existent
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes two arguments for the thingness of the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's arguments from distinctness and possibility fail to prove the external existence of non-existent possibles.

Core Idea
Mental distinctness does not entail external existence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani engages with the Mu'tazilite claim that non-existent things have a kind of external reality. He presents two of their arguments—based on distinctness and possibility—and refutes them by showing that these properties are purely mental. This lesson walks through the arguments and their rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the structure: two arguments followed by a refutation.
2
Identify the first argument
The first argument says the non-existent is distinct (known, willed, etc.), and distinctness implies external existence because indication to pure negation is impossible.
The key term is 'tamyīz' (distinctness).
3
Identify the second argument
The second argument says the non-existent is possible, and possibility is a positive attribute, so the possible must be established externally.
The term 'imkān' (possibility) is central.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes both by stating that distinctness and possibility are mental. They only require mental existence. If they required external existence, then impossible things and imaginary compounds would also exist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: تمسك المخالف بوجوه الأول : أن المعدوم متميز، لأنه معلوم ومراد ومقدور وكل متميز ثابت. لأن التميز إنما يكون بالإشارة العقلية والإشارة إلى النفي الصرف محال . الثاني: أنه ممكن وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان ثبوتي. قلنا كل من التميز والإمكان عقلي يكفيه ثبوت المتميز والممكن في الذهن، ولو اقتضيا الثبوت عيناً لزم ثبوت الممتنعات لتميزها، والمركبات الخياليات التميزها وإمكانها.
He said: The opponent clung to [several] aspects: First: that the non-existent is distinct [from other things], because it is known, willed, and empowered [over], and every distinct thing is established. Because distinctness only occurs by mental indication, and indication to pure negation is impossible. Second: that it is possible, and every possible thing is established, because possibility is a positive attribute. We said: Both distinctness and possibility are mental; the existence of the distinct and possible in the mind suffices for them. If they necessitated external existence, then impossible things would be established due to their distinctness, and imaginary compounds due to their distinctness and possibility.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the first two arguments used by opponents (the Mu'tazila or philosophers) to prove that the non-existent possible thing (ma'dum mumkin) has some kind of thingness (shay'iyya) in external reality. The first argument is based on distinctness (tamyīz): the non-existent is known, willed, and empowered over, so it is distinct from other things; whatever is distinct must be established (thābit) because distinctness requires mental indication (ishāra 'aqliyya), and indication to pure negation is impossible. The second argument is based on possibility (imkān): the non-existent possible thing is possible, and every possible thing is established because possibility (imkān) is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī). The author (al-Taftazani) refutes both arguments by saying that both distinctness and possibility are purely mental (ʻaqli). They require only that the distinct or possible thing exist in the mind (fi al-dhihn), not in the external world. If they necessitated external existence (thubūt ʻaynan), then impossible things (mumtaniʻāt) like a partner of God would also have to exist externally because they are distinct in the mind, and likewise imaginary compounds (murakkabāt khayāliyāt) would have to exist because they are distinct and possible. This shows the fallacy of the opponent's inference.

Key Terms
متميز
معدوم
ثابت
التميز
الإمكان
إشارة عقلية
نفي صرف
ثبوتي
ذهن
ممتنعات
مركبات خياليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental distinctness or possibility entails existence in the external world.
Correction: They only entail existence in the mind, not externally.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation given to the first two arguments for the thingness of the non-existent?
A) The non-existent is not distinct.
B) Distinctness and possibility are mental, not external.
C) Possibility is not a positive attribute.
D) The non-existent is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's arguments from distinctness and possibility only prove mental existence, not external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about something simply because you could clearly imagine it? How can this lesson's distinction between mental and external existence help you catch yourself before mistaking a strong mental conviction for objective truth? What will you do the next time you catch yourself doing that?
?
Think of something you desperately want but do not have (a goal, a relationship, a possession). In what ways do you treat it as if it already exists? How does al-Taftazani's refutation challenge you to separate wishful thinking from reality? What concrete step can you take today to realign your expectations with the truth?

Refutation of the Essence Argument
Al-Taftazani refutes the third argument about non-existent essence's self-subsistent property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's logical refutation of the claim that a non-existent essence is self-subsisting and the distinction between mental abstraction and external reality.

Core Idea
Mental abstraction does not imply external reality.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange where an opponent argues that non-existent blackness must be blackness in itself. Al-Taftazani refutes this by showing that the cessation of blackness coincides with the cessation of existence, and that mentally isolating an essence does not prove its independent existence. Understanding this refutation clarifies a key nuance in the kalam debate on the thingness of the non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the flow of the argument register.
Notice the three rounds of objection and response.
2
Identify the dialectical structure
The passage has three exchanges: argument, refutation, counter-objection, second refutation, final objection, final reply. Mark each turn.
The opponent's view is that blackness is blackness in itself (sawād fī nafsihi).
3
Understand the key concepts
Focus on the terms 'salb' (negation) and 'ʻudūl' (deviation) and the final point: cutting off consideration (qaṭʻ al-naẓar) does not necessitate non-existence.
This distinction is crucial: mentally isolating a property does not make it externally real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن معنى ثبوت المعدوم، أن السواد المعدوم مثلاً سواد في نفسه؛ إذ لو كان ذلك بالغير لزم ارتفاعه بارتفاع الغير، فلا يبقى السواد الموجود سواداً حينئذ . قلنا : ممنوع إذ كما ترتفع سواديته يرتفع وجوده فإن قيل : فلا يكون السواد سواداً وهو محال . قلنا : بمعنى السلب فيمتنع الاستحالة، أو بمعنى العدول بـأن ماهية السواد مع كونها لا سواداً فتمتنع الملازمة . فإن قيل : السواد سواد، وإن لم يوجد الغير ضرورة أن لكل شيء ماهية هو بها مع قطع النظر عن كل ما عداه قلنا قطع النظر عن الشيء لا يوجب انتفاءه.
Third: That the meaning of the establishment of the non-existent is that the non-existent blackness, for example, is blackness in itself; for if it were so by another, it would cease by the cessation of that other, and then existing blackness would not remain blackness at that time. We said: It is denied, for just as its blackness ceases, its existence ceases. If it is said: Then blackness would not be blackness, which is impossible. We said: It means negation, so impossibility is impossible; or it means deviation, that the essence of blackness while being non-blackness, so the entailment is impossible. If it is said: Blackness is blackness even if the other does not exist, necessarily because everything has an essence by which it is what it is, regardless of everything else. We said: Cutting off consideration of a thing does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the thingness of the non-existent and its refutation. The argument claims that the meaning of the non-existent being established is that a non-existent blackness, for example, is blackness in itself (sawād fī nafsihi). If its blackness were derived from something else (bi al-ghayr), then when that other thing ceases, the blackness would cease to be blackness, so existing blackness would not remain blackness. Therefore, blackness must have an essence independent of existence. The author refutes this by saying that as blackness ceases, its existence also ceases – so the opponent's inference is invalid. Then there is an exchange: if it is said that then blackness would not be blackness, which is impossible, the author responds that this is either a negation (salb) or a deviation (ʻudūl); either way, the impossibility does not follow. Then the opponent insists that blackness is blackness regardless of other things because every thing has an essence by which it is itself, independent of everything else. The author replies that cutting off consideration (qaṭʻ al-naẓar) does not necessitate its non-existence, meaning that the essence is mentally separable but not externally independent. The argument does not prove external existence.

Key Terms
سواد
ماهية
بالغير
سلب
عدول
قطع النظر
استحالة
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that mentally isolating an essence (cutting off consideration) proves its external self-subsistence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that 'cutting off consideration' does not necessitate non-existence, but it also does not prove existence. The opponent confuses mental abstraction with external reality.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument's refutation, what does 'cutting off consideration' (قطع النظر) not entail?
A) The non-existence of the essence.
B) The existence of the essence.
C) The impossibility of blackness.
D) The distinctness of blackness.

Key Takeaway
Mental separation of an essence from existence does not prove its external self-subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you treat your own mental constructs—like your self-image, your judgments of others, or your assumptions about how life 'should' be—as if they are objective realities independent of your mind? What would it cost you to admit they are not, and what freedom might follow?
?
Al-Taftazani's opponent insisted that blackness must be blackness in itself. Similarly, we often cling to beliefs about our identity or worth as if they are eternal, unchanging essences. What is one belief you hold about yourself that you assume is self-evident and independent of circumstances? How might it actually be contingent on other factors, and what would it mean to release it?
?
The argument reveals the danger of confusing conceptual separation with actual separation. Where in your relationship with Allah have you mentally separated attributes like mercy and justice, or faith and action, only to act as if they are truly independent? What would it look like to reintegrate them in your heart and practice today?

Refutation of the Distinctness Argument
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the distinctness argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from distinctness (tamyīz) fails to prove the external existence of possible non-existents.

Core Idea
Distinctness only proves mental existence, not external existence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses those who claim that possible non-existents have some kind of external reality. Their first argument relies on the idea that if something is distinct (e.g., known, willed), it must exist. The refutation shows this distinctness is merely mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument for distinctness
Read the first two sentences where the argument is presented: the minor premise (possible non-existents are distinct) and the major premise (every distinct thing is established).
Notice the two premises: minor (al-ṣughrā) and major (al-kubrā).
2
Identify the refutation
Read the refutation starting with 'wa-al-jawāb'. It distinguishes between mental and external distinctness.
The key is that distinctness in the mind does not imply external existence.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on why this refutation is effective: if distinctness were external, the argument would work, but it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ،ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف . الثاني : أنه ممكن، وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان وصف ثبوتي على ما سيأتي ، فيكون الموصوف به ثابتاً بالضرورة. والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع ، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد . وعن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم كون الإمكان ثبوتياً بمعنى كونه ثابتاً في الخارج، بل هو اعتبار عقلي يكفي ثبوت الموصوف به في العقل، ثم لا خفاء في أن الممتنعات كشريك الباري ، واجتماع النقيضين وكون الجسم في آن
Those who claim that the possible non-existent is established in the external world clung to [several] aspects: First: that it is distinct, and every distinct thing is established. As for the minor premise, because it may be known, so it is distinct from the unknown, and willed, so distinct from the unwilled, and empowered over, so distinct from the unempowered over. As for the major premise, because distinctness in the intellect is only conceivable by mental indication of 'this' and 'that', and indication necessitates the existence of the indicated thing, due to the impossibility of indicating pure negation. Second: that it is possible, and every possible thing is established, because possibility is a positive attribute, as will come, so that which is described by it is necessarily established. The answer to the first: that if it is intended that distinctness necessitates existence in the external, it is denied; it would only follow if distinctness were according to the external. If it is intended in the mind, it is of no use. And concerning the second: we do not concede that possibility is positive in the sense of being established in the external; rather, it is a mental consideration (i'tibār 'aqli) which suffices that the thing described by it is established in the intellect. Moreover, it is not hidden that impossible things, such as the partner of the Creator, the conjunction of two opposites, and the body being at an instant [in two places]...
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents a second set of arguments from a group (likely the Ash'ari or others who affirm the external existence of possible non-existents before creation) who claim that the possible non-existent (ma'dum mumkin) is established (thābit) in the external world. They give two arguments. The first is based on distinctness (tamyīz): the possible non-existent is known, willed, and empowered over, so it is distinct from what is not these; every distinct thing is established because distinctness in the intellect requires mental indication (ishāra 'aqliyya) to 'this' and 'that', and indication necessitates an existing indicated object, because indicating pure negation is impossible. The second argument is based on possibility (imkān): the possible non-existent is possible, and every possible thing is established because possibility is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī), as will be explained later. The author gives two answers. For the first argument: if by 'distinctness' they mean it necessitates external existence, it is denied; it would only follow if the distinctness were external. If they mean mental distinctness, then it only gives mental existence, which is not what they want. For the second argument: the author does not concede that possibility is positive in the sense of being an external attribute; rather, it is a mental consideration (i'tibār 'aqli). It only requires that the thing described by possibility exists in the mind. Then he points out that impossible things (mumtani'āt) like a partner of God, conjunction of opposites, and a body being in two places at once would also have to be established if the argument were valid, because they are also distinct and possible in some sense? The text cuts off but the point is clear.

Key Terms
معدوم ممكن
تميز
إشارة عقلية
ثابت في الخارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is distinct in the mind, it must exist in the external world.
Correction: Distinctness in the mind only implies mental existence; external existence requires additional proof.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's refutation, what does distinctness (tamyīz) in the mind actually prove?
A) External existence.
B) Mental existence only.
C) Both mental and external existence.
D) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
The distinctness argument fails because distinctness is mental, not external, so it only proves mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
