Refutation of the Possibility Argument
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the possibility argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkān) does not imply external existence but is merely a mental consideration.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental attribute, not an external property.

The second argument claims that because possible non-existents are possible, and possibility is a positive attribute, they must exist. Al-Taftazani responds by denying that possibility is an external attribute; it is a mental consideration. He also points out that impossible things would then also have to exist, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the possibility argument
Read the sentence beginning with 'al-thānī' (the second) where the argument is presented: possibility is a positive attribute, so the described thing is established.
Notice the claim that possibility (imkān) is a positive attribute (waṣf thubūtī).
2
Study the refutation
Read the refutation starting with 'wa-ʿan al-thānī'. Note the denial that possibility is an external attribute.
Key phrase: 'bal huwa iʿtibār ʿaqli' (rather it is a mental consideration).
3
Consider the reductio ad absurdum
Read the final part about impossibles (mumtaniʿāt). If possibility were external, then impossibles would also be distinct and possible in a sense, leading to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is possible in your mind, it must be real? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what concrete step will you take today to test your assumptions against external reality rather than mental convenience?
?
This refutation uses reductio ad absurdum: if possibility implied external existence, even impossible concepts would exist. Are there beliefs you hold that, when pushed to their logical conclusion, lead to absurd consequences? Will you examine one such belief today and submit it to the same rigorous critique al-Taftazani applies?
?
The argument that possibility is a mental consideration forces you to reconsider the foundation of your beliefs about God. What is one belief you hold about God that you have never examined at this level of intellectual honesty? Commit to investigating it using the criteria of reason and revelation, and do not rest until your certainty is built on solid ground.

Two Objections on the Essence of Blackness and Existence
Two objections and answers on blackness and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between negation (salb) and privation (udul) in responding to objections about the dependency of essences on existence.

Core Idea
Essences are not negated by mental abstraction; salb and udul handle two different types of negation.

In this passage, the author addresses two objections concerning the relationship between blackness and its existence. The first objection argues that removing blackness would make black not black, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between two types of negation: pure negation and privation. The second objection asserts that black is black by essence irrespective of external existence. The answer clarifies that disregarding something does not negate it. These distinctions are crucial for understanding how essences relate to existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the compressed style of kalam argumentation.
2
Identify the two objections and answers
Locate the two objections (indicated by 'فإن قيل') and their corresponding answers ('قلنا'). Observe how each answer uses a specific logical distinction.
The first answer uses salb and udul; the second uses qat' al-nazar.
3
Understand key terms
Focus on the terms السلب (negation), العدول (privation), and قطع النظر (disregarding). Understand how they resolve the objections.
السلب is pure negation, العدول is privation (implying a positive quality lost).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : ممنوع إذ كما ترتفع سواديته يرتفع وجوده فإن قيل : فلا يكون السواد سواداً وهو محال . قلنا : بمعنى السلب فيمتنع الاستحالة، أو بمعنى العدول بأن ماهية السواد مع كونها لا سواداً فتمتنع الملازمة . فإن قيل : السواد سواد، وإن لم يوجد الغير ضرورة أن لكل شيء ماهية هو بها مع قطع النظر عن كل ما عداه قلنا قطع النظر عن الشيء لا يوجب انتفاءه
We said: It is prevented, because just as its blackness is removed, its existence is removed. If it is said: Then the black would not be black, which is impossible. We said: In the sense of negation, so transformation is impossible; or in the sense of privation, that the essence of black, while it is not black, so the implication is impossible. If it is said: Black is black, even if the other does not exist, necessarily because everything has an essence by which it is what it is, disregarding everything else. We said: disregarding a thing does not necessitate its non-existence.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents two objections and their answers concerning the nature of blackness and its relation to existence. The first objection argues that if blackness ceases to exist, it would not be black, which is absurd. The answer distinguishes between two types of negation: pure negation (salb) and privation (udul). In pure negation, the statement 'it is not black' is simply true, so no impossibility. In privation, the essence of blackness would be not-black, which is contradictory, but the implication is denied because the essence is not considered separate from existence. The second objection asserts that black is black by its essence regardless of existence. The answer explains that disregarding other things (qat' al-nazar) does not entail their non-existence; mental abstraction does not remove external reality.

Key Terms
السواد
السلب
العدول
الماهية
قطع النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing negation (salb) with privation (udul) when analyzing the continuity of essences.
Correction: Salb is pure negation without implying a positive quality, while udul implies the absence of a positive quality that should be present.

Checkpoint
What is the difference between سلب (salb) and عدول (udul) in the context of the first objection?
A) Salb means privation, udul means negation.
B) Salb means negation, udul means privation.
C) Both mean the same.
D) Salb refers to existence, udul to essence.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between pure negation (salb) and privation (udul) is essential to refute objections about essences losing their identity when existence is removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally disregard your own faults or failures, do you sometimes act as if they don't exist? How does the lesson's truth that 'disregarding does not necessitate non-existence' expose a self-deception you need to confront? What specific habit will you change today to stop ignoring reality?
?
The objection claims blackness cannot cease to be black—yet you know your own deeds cease at death. Are you living as if your essence is independent of your Creator? How does the distinction between salb and udul remind you that your existence is contingent on Allah every moment? Write a short prayer acknowledging your absolute dependence.
?
Have you ever felt that if you fail in a good deed, you lose part of your identity? The lesson shows that essence can be considered without existence. How can you separate your worth as a servant of Allah from your actions? Commit to one act of worship today purely to affirm your relationship with Him, not your performance.

The Proof from Distinctness
First proof that non-existent possibles are established, based on distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that non-existent possibles are established in the external world, based on the idea that they are distinct, and how the proof argues that distinctness implies existence.

Core Idea
Distinctness implies existence, so non-existent possibles must exist.

The proponents of the view that non-existent possibles have some form of external existence put forward several arguments. The first of these relies on the concept of distinctness (tamyīz): if something is distinct from other things, it must be established in reality. Let us examine how they try to prove this.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument.
Note the syllogistic structure with two premises.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the minor premise (the non-existent possible is distinct) and the major premise (every distinct thing is established).
3
Understand the argument for the major premise
The author argues that distinctness requires mental pointing, and pointing implies an object, so distinctness entails existence.
This is a key step in the proof.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof aims to show that non-existent possibles are established in the external world. Consider what that means.
Be prepared for the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف . والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع ، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد
Those who say that the non-existent possible is established in the external world hold to proofs: First: It is distinct, and every distinct thing is established. As for the minor premise, because it may be known, so it is distinct from what is not known; and willed, so distinct from what is not willed; and subject to power, so distinct from what is not subject to power. As for the major premise, because distinctness in the intellect is not conceivable except by mental indication of 'this' and 'that', and indication necessitates the existence of the indicated, necessarily due to the impossibility of indicating pure negation. The answer to the first: If it is meant that distinctness necessitates existence in the external world, it is denied; it would only follow if distinctness were according to the external, but if it is meant in the mind, it does not benefit.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage gives the first proof of the claim that non-existent possibles are established in the external world. The proof has two premises: (1) the non-existent possible is distinct (mutamayyiz) from other things, and (2) whatever is distinct is established (thabit). The minor premise is supported by examples: it is known, willed, or subject to power, so it is distinct from what is not known, etc. The major premise is argued by saying that distinctness in the intellect requires mental pointing to 'this' and 'that', and pointing implies an object of pointing, since pure negation cannot be pointed to. The answer refutes the major premise: if distinctness is mental, it only implies mental existence, not external. The proof fails because it conflates mental and external distinctness.

Key Terms
التميز
الثبوت
الإشارة العقلية
النفي الصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that mental distinctness automatically proves external existence.
Correction: The proof conflates mental and external distinctness; the answer will show that mental distinctness only proves mental existence.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise of the first proof for the establishment of non-existent possibles?
A) Every possible is established.
B) Every distinct thing is established.
C) Every known thing is established.
D) Every mental thing is established.

Key Takeaway
The proof from distinctness argues that non-existent possibles must exist because they are distinct, but the refutation will challenge the inference from mental distinctness to external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that your thoughts, intentions, and desires are distinct from each other—you can point to them mentally. But do you treat them as real as physical objects? If God knows your every intention, does that make your inner world more 'real' and accountable? Take a moment to consider: if a thought is distinct, God holds you accountable for it. How does that change the way you monitor your inner life starting today?
?
The proof says pure negation cannot be indicated. When you make dua for something that doesn't yet exist, you are indicating it in your mind—so it is not pure negation. Do you truly believe that what you ask for is 'real' to Allah before it happens? Does that trust impact the certainty (yaqīn) of your dua? If not, what would it take to make your supplication more real to you?
?
You are distinct from your actions—your self (nafs) can be known, willed, and subject to power separately from what you do. If distinctness implies existence, then your self exists even when you are not acting. Are you more than your deeds? What does that mean for your self-worth? If your self is established by its own distinctness, does that change how you rely on Allah for your identity?

Refutation of the Distinctness Proof
Refutation of the first proof based on distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refutation of the first proof for non-existent possibles exposes the confusion between mental and external distinctness.

Core Idea
Distinctness in the mind does not entail existence outside the mind.

Having presented the first proof that non-existent possibles are established externally by virtue of their distinctness, the author now provides a decisive refutation. The refutation targets the major premise: it argues that if the distinctness is merely mental, it only proves mental establishment, not external. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of possibility in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully.
Notice the conditional language (إن أريد) indicating a distinction.
2
Identify the conditional response
The author distinguishes between distinctness in the external world and distinctness in the mind.
3
Understand the denial
If external distinctness is meant, the major premise is denied; if mental distinctness, the conclusion only gives mental existence.
This invalidates the proof.
4
Reflect on the implication
The proof fails because it equivocates between two kinds of distinctness.
This is a classic refutation in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القائلون بأن المعدوم الممكن ثابت في الخارج تمسكوا بوجوه : الأول : أنه متميز وكل متميز ثابت أما الصغرى فلأنه قد يكون معلوماً فيتميز عن غير المعلوم، ومراداً فيتميز عن غير المراد، ومقدوراً فيتميز عن غير المقدور. وأما الكبرى فلأن التميز عند العقل لا يتصور إلا بالإشارة العقلية بهذا وذاك، والإشارة تقتضي ثبوت المشار إليه ضرورة امتناع الإشارة إلى النفي الصرف . والجواب عن الأول : أنه إن أريد أن التميز يقتضي الثبوت في الخارج فممنوع ، وإنما يلزم لو كان التميز بحسب الخارج وإن أريد في الذهن فلا يفيد
Those who say that the non-existent possible is established in the external world hold to proofs: First: It is distinct, and every distinct thing is established. As for the minor premise, because it may be known, so it is distinct from what is not known; and willed, so distinct from what is not willed; and subject to power, so distinct from what is not subject to power. As for the major premise, because distinctness in the intellect is not conceivable except by mental indication of 'this' and 'that', and indication necessitates the existence of the indicated, necessarily due to the impossibility of indicating pure negation. The answer to the first: If it is meant that distinctness necessitates existence in the external world, it is denied; it would only follow if distinctness were according to the external, but if it is meant in the mind, it does not benefit.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage gives the first proof of the claim that non-existent possibles are established in the external world. The proof has two premises: (1) the non-existent possible is distinct (mutamayyiz) from other things, and (2) whatever is distinct is established (thabit). The minor premise is supported by examples: it is known, willed, or subject to power, so it is distinct from what is not known, etc. The major premise is argued by saying that distinctness in the intellect requires mental pointing to 'this' and 'that', and pointing implies an object of pointing, since pure negation cannot be pointed to. The answer refutes the major premise: if distinctness is mental, it only implies mental existence, not external. The proof fails because it conflates mental and external distinctness.

الخارج
الذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the refutation denies that non-existent possibles are distinct in any way.
Correction: The refutation accepts mental distinctness but denies that it implies external existence.

Checkpoint
How does the refutation of the first proof address the claim that distinctness implies external existence?
A) It denies the minor premise.
B) It distinguishes mental from external distinctness.
C) It accepts the major premise but denies the minor.
D) It says the proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The first proof fails because it conflates mental distinctness, which is admitted, with external distinctness, which is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Proof from Possibility and Its Refutation
Second proof from possibility and its refutation by denying external positivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second proof from possibility fails and how the answer distinguishes between external and mental attributes.

Core Idea
Possibility is a mental label, not an external property.

The second proof attempts to show that non-existent possibles must exist because they are described as 'possible,' which is a positive attribute. But the response reveals a subtle distinction: possibility is not a real property in the outside world—it is a mental consideration. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping how kalam arguments treat possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the passage from 'الثاني' to 'بالضرورة' and note the claim that possibility is a positive attribute (وصف ثبوتي).
The proof assumes that if something is described by a positive attribute, it must exist.
2
Identify the answer
Read the answer from 'وعن الثاني' to the end. Notice that it denies the premise that possibility is an external attribute.
The answer offers an alternative characterization: possibility is a mental consideration.
3
Understand the distinction
Reflect on the difference between an external attribute and a mental consideration. The answer argues that mental existence suffices for the description 'possible'.
This distinction blocks the proof's conclusion that non-existent possibles must exist externally.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Consider why this argument matters: it defends the concept of non-existent possibles against the claim that they must actually exist.
This is part of a larger debate about the status of things before creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه ممكن، وكل ممكن ثابت لأن الإمكان وصف ثبوتي على ما سيأتي ، فيكون الموصوف به ثابتاً بالضرورة. وعن الثاني : أنا لا نسلم كون الإمكان ثبوتياً بمعنى كونه ثابتاً في الخارج، بل هو اعتبار عقلي يكفي ثبوت الموصوف به في العقل
Second: It is possible, and every possible is established, because possibility is a positive attribute, as will come, so that which is described by it is necessarily established. The answer to the second: We do not accept that possibility is positive in the sense of being established in the external world; rather it is a mental consideration, and the establishment of that which is described by it in the mind suffices.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof: possibility (imkan) is a positive attribute, so that which is described by it must be established. The answer denies that possibility is a positive attribute in the external sense; it is merely a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli). The establishment of the thing in the mind is sufficient to be described as possible. Thus, possibility does not require external existence.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage clarifies that possibility is merely a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي), not an attribute that exists in the external world.

Checkpoint

A) As a positive external attribute.
B) As a mental consideration.
C) As a necessary attribute.
D) As a relational attribute.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a mental consideration, not an external attribute, so it does not prove external existence.

Refutation by Impossible Things and the States Objection
Refutation and objection with impossible things and states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam refutes the claim that distinctness implies external establishment, using impossible beings and imaginary composites, and how the states objection is answered.

Core Idea
Distinctness does not guarantee external existence; even imaginary things are distinct but non-existent.

The proof we encountered earlier relied on a major premise: every distinct thing must be established in the external world. But is that always true? The author now challenges this premise with clear counterexamples: impossible things and imaginary composites are undeniably distinct, yet they have no external reality. Then he addresses a sophisticated objection based on the concept of states (ahwal)—attributes that are neither existent nor non-existent. This lesson unpacks the logic of the refutation and the response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counterexamples
Read the first part of the passage where the author lists impossible things (like a partner of God) and imaginary composites (like a mountain of ruby). Notice that they are distinct but not established in the external world.
These counterexamples directly attack the premise that distinctness equals external establishment.
2
Understand the states objection
Next, the author presents an objection using states (ahwal) such as existence. States are distinct but neither existent nor non-existent. The objector might claim they are not established in non-existence, so the rule fails.
3
Analyze the response
The author responds by refining the opponent's principle: every distinct thing is established in the external world according to its nature. Since states are attributes of existents, they have no 'state of non-existence', so they do not need to be established in non-existence. The objection is invalid.
This shows that the original premise is not as simple as it seems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لا خفاء في أن الممتنعات كشريك الباري ، واجتماع النقيضين وكون الجسم في آن واحد في حيزين بعضها متميز عن البعض وعن الأمور الموجودة مع أنها منتفية قطعاً، وإن مثل جبل من الياقوت وبحر من الزئبق من المركبات الخيالية، متميز مع أنها غير ثابتة ،وفاقاً، فيورد بالأول معارضة أو نقض على الوجه الأولى، وبالتالي على الوجهين وقد يورد النقض بالأحوال من الوجود ،وغيره فإنها مع تميزها ليست بثابتة في العدم، إذ لا عدم لها ولا وجود، سبق من أن الحال صفة للموجود لا موجودة ولا معدومة، وفيه نظر لأن قاعدة الخصم ليست سوى أن كل متميز ثابت في الخارج، فإن كان موجوداً، ففي الوجود، أو معدوماً ففي العدم، أو لا موجوداً ولا معدومـاً ففي تلك الحال، والوجود وغيره من الأحوال ليس لها حالة العدم أصلا فمن أين يلزم ثبوتها في العدم
Then it is not hidden that impossible things, such as the partner of the Creator, and the conjunction of contradictories, and a body being in two places at one moment, some of them are distinct from some and from existing things, while they are absolutely non-existent; and likewise a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury from imaginary composites, they are distinct while they are not established, by agreement. So it is objected as a contradiction to the first, or as a refutation in the first manner, and thus to both. And an objection may be brought using the states (al-ahwal), such as existence and others, for they, despite their distinctness, are not established in non-existence, since they have neither non-existence nor existence; it has preceded that the state is an attribute of the existent, neither existent nor non-existent. And in this there is consideration, because the opponent's principle is only that every distinct thing is established in the external world: if it is existent, then in existence; if non-existent, then in non-existence; or if neither existent nor non-existent, then in that state. But existence and other states have no state of non-existence at all, so how does the necessity of their establishment in non-existence follow?
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage refutes the first proof's major premise by providing counterexamples. Impossible things like the partner of God and the conjunction of contradictories are distinct but unequivocally non-existent. Imaginary composites like a mountain of ruby are also distinct but not established. Thus, distinctness does not entail external establishment. Then it addresses an objection using the notion of states (ahwal), which are attributes of existents that are themselves neither existent nor non-existent. The objection claims that states are distinct but not established in non-existence. The answer analyzes the opponent's principle: every distinct thing is established somewhere, depending on its nature. But states have no state of non-existence because they are not non-existent; they are only in the external world as qualities of existents. Therefore, they do not need to be established in non-existence, and the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
الممتنعات
الأحوال
المركبات الخيالية
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that states (ahwal) are a third category of beings that require a separate realm of existence (like a 'state of non-existence')
Correction: States are attributes of existents and are neither existent nor non-existent; they are established only in the external world as qualities, not in a separate realm.

Checkpoint
What counterexample is given to refute the claim that every distinct thing is established in the external world?
A) The non-existent possible.
B) Imaginary composites like a mountain of ruby.
C) The blackness of black.
D) The possibility of existence.

Key Takeaway
Distinctness alone does not entail external existence; impossible and imaginary things are distinct but non-existent, and the states objection fails because states have no state of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can imagine a mountain of ruby vividly, but it has no reality. What belief or assumption in your life might be just as vividly imagined yet completely false? Will you today test one such idea against the objective truth of revelation?
?
The opponent's principle sounds plausible: distinct things must be real. But the lesson shows it's wrong. What in your life are you assuming is true simply because you have a clear concept of it? Would you be willing to examine that assumption?
?
States like existence are neither existent nor non-existent; they are simply attributes of what exists. When you think of God's attributes, do you treat them as separate entities or as inherent to His essence? How can you train your mind to avoid reifying attributes?

Third Proof: Non-Existent Possibles Have Self-Subsistent Essences
Third proof that non-existent possibles are self-subsistent essences, with objection and partial answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that non-existent possibles have self-subsistent essences, and the beginning of a dialectical exchange about it.

Core Idea
A non-existent possible is itself, regardless of external existence.

This passage presents a critical proof in the debate about the reality of non-existent possibles. The author argues that a possible thing, even when not existing, has its own essence that does not depend on external factors. We'll examine the proof, an objection, and the start of a response that is unfortunately cut off.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice how the proof uses blackness as an example to argue that an essence is what it is independently of existence.
The proof hinges on the idea that if blackness were black by another, removing that other would make blackness cease to be black, which is absurd.
2
Understand the objection
The objector claims that if the cause of blackness is removed, blackness disappears, leading to a self-evident contradiction. Identify this objection in the text.
The objection relies on the intuition that a thing cannot be black if its blackness is removed.
3
Note the incomplete answer
The answer begins to reject the objection by distinguishing between negation of essence and negation of existence, but the text cuts off. Reflect on what the full answer might be.
The lesson is incomplete; the original source page ends here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أن معنى كون المعدوم الممكن ثابتاً في الخارج، أن السواد المعدوم مثلاً سواد في نفسه سواء وجد الغير أو لم يوجد، وبيانه ظاهر، لأنه لو كان كونه سواداً بالغير لزم ارتفاع كون السواد سواداً عند ارتفاع الغير واللازم باطل لأنه يستلزم أن لا يبقى السواد الموجود سواداً عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير، الذي هو الموجب لكونه سواداً وهو محال . والجواب : أنا لا نسلم استلزامه لذلك، وإنما يلزم لو كان وجود السواد عند ارتفاع موجب السوادية وهو ممنوع ، لم لا يجوز أن يكون ارتفاع الغير كما يوجب ارتفاع سواديته، يوجب ارتفاع وجوده لكونه العلة للوجود أو لازمها . فإن قيل : لو ارتفع عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير سوادية السواد لزم أن لا يكون الاستحالة السواد سوادا، وهو بديهي . قلنا : إن أريد به أنه يلزم السلب أي ليس السواد المعدوم سواداً، فلا
Third: That the meaning of the non-existent possible being established in the external world is that blackness, for example, is black in itself whether the other exists or not. And its explanation is clear, because if its being black were by the other, then the removal of blackness would follow upon the removal of the other, and the consequent is false because it would entail that existing black would not remain black upon the removal of that other, which is the cause of its being black, and that is impossible. The answer: We do not accept that it entails that; it would only follow if the existence of black were upon the removal of the cause of blackness, which is denied. Why is it not permissible that the removal of the other, just as it necessitates the removal of its blackness, necessitates the removal of its existence, because it is the cause of existence or its necessary concomitant? If it is said: If upon the removal of that other the blackness of black is removed, it would necessitate that the thing (al-istihala) be not black, and it is self-evident. We said: If it is meant by it that a negation follows, i.e., the non-existent black is not black, then [the answer is incomplete].
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof: the meaning of a non-existent possible being established externally is that it is itself, e.g., blackness is black in itself regardless of external existence. The argument is that if blackness were black by another, then when that other is removed, blackness would cease to be black, which is false because existing black would then not be black. The answer rejects the entailment: removal of the cause of blackness could also remove existence itself, so the black thing would cease to exist, and no contradiction follows. The objector retorts that if blackness is removed, then the thing is not black, which is self-evident. The answer begins by saying that if by 'it is not black' is meant a negation (salb), i.e., the non-existent black is not black, then [...] and the text cuts off. This incomplete argument requires further discussion.

The Universal with Particulars: Neither Existent nor Nonexistent
Argument on universal 'human' existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a universal like 'human' cannot be considered existent (otherwise it would be individuated) nor nonexistent (otherwise it could not be part of existing particulars).

Core Idea
A universal with actual particulars must exist in a third mode — mental existence.

Sanusi begins his second discussion by examining the universal that has actual particulars, such as the concept 'human' that applies to Zayd, 'Amr, etc. He argues that this universal cannot be fully existent in the external world nor fully nonexistent. The passage sets up a foundational problem in ontology: how do universals exist? Let's read the original Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: 'not existent because... not nonexistent because...'.
This is a classic kalam dilemma.
2
Identify the two main arguments
The passage presents two key points: why the universal cannot be existent (would be individuated) and why it cannot be nonexistent (cannot precede an existent).
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if a universal is neither existent nor nonexistent, what alternative mode of existence might it have? The commentary will hint at mental existence.
This connects to later Islamic ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : أن الكلي الذي له جزئيات متحققة مثل الإنسان ليس بموجود، وإلا لكان مشخصاً ، فلا يكون كلياً، فلا يكون كلياً، ولا معدوماً، وإلا لما كان جزءاً من جزئياته الموجودة، كزيد مثلاً، لامتناع تقدم الموجود بالمعدوم، وأيضاً الجنس كالحيوان ليس بموجود لكليته، ولا معدوم لكونه جزءاً من الماهية الحقيقية كالإنسان، وأيضاً جنس الماهيات الحقيقية من الأعراض، كلونية السواد ليس بمعدوم لما ذكر ولا موجود لاستلزامه قيام العرض بالعرض قيل : - أي اللون بالسواد لأنه المحمول طبعاً . وقيل بالعكس : لأن الجنس مقوم للنوع ، وقيل : أي اللون بالفصل الذي هو قابض البصر، مثلاً لكونه المحمول، وقيل بالعكس. لكون الفصل مقوماً للجنس، والكل فاسد لأن جزء المركب سيما المحمول عليه لا يكون عرضاً
And the second: that the universal which has actual particulars like 'human' is not existent, otherwise it would be individuated, so it would not be universal, and it is not nonexistent, otherwise it would not be a part of its existing particulars, like Zayd for example, due to the impossibility of the existent being preceded by the nonexistent. Also, the genus like 'animal' is not existent due to its universality, and not nonexistent because it is a part of the true quiddity like 'human'. Also, the genus of true quiddities among accidents, like 'color-ness' of blackness, is not nonexistent for what was mentioned, and not existent due to its necessitating the subsistence of an accident by an accident. It is said: that is, the color by blackness because it is the naturally predicated. And it is said the opposite: because the genus is constitutive of the species. And it is said: that is, the color by the differentia which is, for example, the light-grasper, because it is the predicated. And it is said the opposite: because the differentia is constitutive of the genus. And all are invalid because a part of the compound, especially that which is predicated upon, cannot be an accident.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the ontological status of universals (kullī). The author presents a second discussion (al-thānī) concerning the universal that has actual particulars, such as 'human'. The argument is that such a universal cannot be existent, because if it were, it would be individuated (mushakhkhaṣ) and thus not universal. It also cannot be nonexistent, because then it could not be a part of its existing particulars (e.g., Zayd), since an existent cannot be preceded by a nonexistent. The same reasoning applies to genera (jins) like 'animal': not existent due to universality, not nonexistent because it is part of the true quiddity. The author then turns to the genus of true quiddities among accidents, like 'color-ness' (lawniyya) of blackness. This is not nonexistent for the same reason, but not existent because it would entail the subsistence of an accident (the accident 'color-ness') by another accident (the color). This leads to a discussion of predication: is the accident predicated on the genus or vice versa? Various opinions are mentioned: some say color is predicated on blackness because it is naturally predicated; others say the opposite because the genus constitutes the species. Another view says color is predicated on the differentia (e.g., light-grasper) because it is the predicated; others say the opposite because the differentia constitutes the genus. The author concludes that all these views are invalid (fāsid) because a part of the compound, especially the subject of predication (al-maḥmūl ʿalayhi), cannot be an accident.

Key Terms
كلي
جزئي
موجود
معدوم
مشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think universals are either fully real in the external world or mere names.
Correction: The passage shows that universals like 'human' are neither fully existent in the external world (otherwise individuated) nor nonexistent (otherwise cannot be part of real particulars), implying a third mode: mental existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot a universal like 'human' be considered existent?
A) Because it would then be nonexistent
B) Because it would be individuated and thus not universal
C) Because it is part of a particular
D) Because it is an accident

Key Takeaway
A universal with actual particulars cannot be fully existent (would lose universality) nor nonexistent (cannot be part of existent), pointing to a mode of mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universal 'human' exists only in the mind, then what is the basis for your belief that every person shares a common essence? Does your treatment of others reflect a real connection or just a label?
?
This argument shows that something can be real without being physically existent. What does that mean for your attachment to material things? Are you investing your soul in realities that are less real than the concepts in your mind?
?
The universal 'human' is neither fully existent nor nonexistent. How does this unsettle your usual categories of 'real' and 'imaginary'? Will you now question whether other unseen realities (like the soul, or the afterlife) might have a mode of existence you have not considered?

Genera: Not Existent, Not Nonexistent
Dilemma for genera and accidental genera

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the same dilemma applies to genera like 'animal' and accidental genera like 'color-ness', and why accidental genera cannot subsist in an accident.

Core Idea
Genera share the same ontological ambiguity as universal kinds.

After examining the universal 'human', Sanusi extends the same reasoning to genera (jins) such as 'animal' and then to the genus of accidental quiddities, like 'color-ness' of blackness. The latter introduces a further problem: if the genus is an accident, it would require subsistence by another accident, leading to infinite regress. Let's read the Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the extended passage
Read the Arabic for the genus 'animal' and 'color-ness'. Notice the parallel structure: 'not existent due to universality, not nonexistent because part of quiddity'.
2
Identify the new problem with accidents
For 'color-ness', the author adds that it cannot be existent because it would require an accident (color) to subsist in another accident, which is impossible.
This is a key point about the hierarchy of accidents.
3
Connect to the previous lesson
Reflect on how the genus argument is similar but adds the accidental dimension.
This builds toward the critique of predication views.

Predication of Accidental Genera: The Invalid Views
Critique of predication views on accidental genera

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four opinions on predication of accidental genera and why the author deems them all invalid.

Core Idea
Predication of accidental genera leads to contradictions; all proposed solutions fail.

Having established the dilemma for accidental genera, Sanusi now examines various scholarly opinions on how an accidental genus like 'color' relates to its species like 'blackness' or to the differentia. He presents four views and declares them all invalid because the subject of predication cannot be an accident. Let's read the Arabic.

3
Understand the author's critique
The author says all are invalid because a part of the compound, especially the subject of predication, cannot be an accident.
This is the key point.

External Reality vs. Mental Composition: Refuting Accident Inherence
Refutation of accident-in-accident by mental distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that an accident can inhere in another accident by distinguishing external existence from mental composition.

Core Idea
External reality is one; distinctions are only in the mind.

The classical kalam debate about accidents often led to claims that one accident could inhere in another. The author here takes a concrete example—color, light-grasping, and blackness—to show that such a claim is based on a misunderstanding. He argues that in external existence there is only one thing; composition and distinction are mental constructs. This lesson explains the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the author's tone of astonishment at the opponents' view.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the example of color, light-grasping, and blackness. What claim about them does the author refute?
The opponent claims one inheres in another, leading to accident-in-accident.
3
Analyze the author's refutation
Notice the key move: 'في الوجود أمر واحد' (in existence there is one thing). Composition and distinction are only according to the intellect.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Understand the critique of non-existence
The author criticizes the opponent's distinction between al-lamawjud and al-ma'dum. Why is this problematic?
The opponent claims part of the existent comes from the non-existent (contradictory), but denies it comes from al-ma'dum (more specific).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس في الخارج شيء هو اللون، وآخر هو قابض البصر، وآخر مركب منهما هو السواد ليلزم من قيام واحد من الثلاثة بآخر منها على ما مر من التفصيل، قيام العرض بالعرض بل في الوجود أمر واحد وإنما التركب والتمايز بحسب العقل فقط، فلا يلزمنه إلا كون الكلي أو الجنس موجوداً في الذهن، ولا استحالة فيه وأنا أتعجب منهم كيف ادعوا أن جزء الموجود يجب أن يكون من أفراد اللاموجود، الذي هو نقيض الموجود، ويمتنع أن يكون من أفراد المعدوم ، الذي ليس عندهم نقيض الموجود بل أخص منه
There is nothing in external reality that is color, another that is light-grasping, and another compounded from them that is blackness, such that from the inherence of one of the three in another would follow, according to the detail given, the inherence of an accident in an accident. Rather, in existence there is one thing; composition and distinction are only according to the intellect. So it only entails that the universal or genus exists in the mind, and there is no impossibility in that. And I am astonished at how they claimed that part of the existent must be from individuals of the non-existent, which is the contradictory of the existent, and it is impossible that it be from individuals of the non-existent (al-ma'dum), which according to them is not the contradictory of the existent but more specific than it.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that an accident can inhere in another accident, and it also criticizes a related erroneous view. The author begins by giving an example: color, light-grasping (or light-capturing), and blackness—these three are not distinct external realities; rather, there is a single external object. The claim that they are distinct and that one inheres in another would lead to an accident inhering in an accident, which is impossible. In external existence, there is only one thing; composition and distinction (tamyiz) occur only in the mind. Therefore, the only necessary consequence is that the universal (kulli) or genus (jins) exists mentally, and there is nothing impossible in that. Finally, the author expresses astonishment at those who claim that part of the existent must come from the non-existent (al-lamawjud), which is the contradictory of the existent, but they deny that it could come from al-ma'dum, which they consider a narrower concept. The author is critiquing a position that distinguishes between al-lamawjud and al-ma'dum in an incoherent way.

كلي
جنس

معدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents are distinct external realities that can inhere in one another.
Correction: In external existence, accidents are not separate; composition and distinction are only mental. Accident inherence in another accident is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only necessary consequence of the mental distinction between color, light-grasping, and blackness?
A) The inherence of one accident in another
B) That the universal or genus exists in the mind
C) That these three are distinct external realities
D) That there is an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
External reality is a single entity; multiplicity arises only in the mind, so accident-in-accident is impossible and only universal/genus exists mentally.

?
The author is astonished at the opponent's inconsistency. Where in your own life do you hold contradictory beliefs—perhaps in how you view divine decree versus human responsibility—without seeking resolution? Will you confront that inconsistency today, even if it unsettles you?
?
If external reality is one single thing and mental distinctions are just tools, how does that change the way you approach your own sins? Do you mentally separate 'the sin' from 'the sinner' to excuse yourself? Will you instead see them as one reality and repent wholly?

Refutation of Proofs for the State via Similarity
Sanusi refutes proofs for the state using similarity and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of similarity among states leads to an infinite regress, and how Sanusi refutes the proofs for the state using this argument.

Core Idea
If states were real, their shared subsistence would cause an infinite regress.

Sanusi now turns to refute the proofs for the state (hal). The refutation relies on showing that if states were real, they would be similar in subsistence, leading to an infinite regress. He also addresses the predication problem and the accident-in-accident issue. An objection is raised and answered. This lesson focuses on the first refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the three points of refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three refutation points
Look for: (1) similarity leading to regress, (2) predication of states on particulars, (3) accident inhering in accident.
Each point is introduced by 'و' (and).
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection claims states don't admit similarity/difference, so no regress. The response says these concepts are necessary and the impossibility of regress will be explained later.
Note the phrase 'سيجيء' (will come later).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ونوقض الوجهان بالحال فإن الأحوال متماثلة في الثبوت في الخصوصيات فيزيد ثبوتها ويتسلسل وإنها تحمل على جزئياتها، فإن كانت ثابتة تشخصت وإلا انتفت وعلى أحوال هي أعراض فيقوم العرض بالعرض. فإن قيل : الأحوال لا تقبل التماثل والاختلاف، فلا يزيد ثبوتها ليتسلسل، ولا يكون شيء منها كلياً وحالاً، والآخر جزئياً ومحلاً على أن التسلسل إنما يمتنع في الموجود [؟] دون الثابت. قلنا: قبول المفهومين التماثل والاختلاف ضروري وامتناع التسلسل سيجيء. تقرير الأول : أن الأحوال لو كانت ثابتة لكانت متشاركة في الثبوت التسلسل : في اللغة بمعنى اتصال الماء وجريانه في الحلق . وعند المحدثين : عبارة عن توارد رجال إسناد الحديث واحداً فواحداً على حالة وصفة واحدة عند رواية ذلك الحديث. وعند الحكماء : عبارة عن ترتيب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الوجود والترتيب سواء كان الترتب وضعياً أو عقلياً. وأما التسلسل مطلقاً : فهو ترتب أمور غير متناهية عند الحكماء وكذا عند المتكلمين . وأما التسلسل المستحيل عندهم فترتب أمور غير متناهية مجتمعة في الوجود.
He said: And the two proofs are refuted by the state (hal), for the states are similar (mutamathila) in subsistence (thubut) in the particulars, so their subsistence increases and yields an infinite regress (tasalsul); and they are predicated of their particulars—if they are existing (thabita), they become particularized, otherwise they are negated; and they inhere in states that are accidents, so an accident would inhere in an accident. If it is said: 'States do not admit similarity (tamathul) and difference (ikhtilaf), so their subsistence does not increase to yield an infinite regress; and none of them is a universal (kulli) while being a state, nor another a particular (juz'i) while being a locus (mahall), given that infinite regress is only impossible in the existent [؟] not in the subsistent (thabit).' We say: 'The concepts of similarity and difference are necessarily accepted (daruri), and the impossibility of infinite regress will come later.' The exposition of the first [refutation]: If states were existing (thabita), they would share in subsistence. [Definition of tasalsul:] Tasalsul in language means the connection of water and its flowing in the throat. According to the hadith scholars: it is the successive occurrence of narrators of a hadith one by one upon a single state and description when narrating that hadith. According to the philosophers (hukama'): it is the arrangement of infinite things coexisting in existence, whether the arrangement is positional or intellectual. As for tasalsul absolutely: it is the arrangement of infinite things according to the philosophers and likewise according to the theologians. As for the impossible tasalsul according to them: it is the arrangement of infinite things coexisting in existence.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the proofs for the state (hal) and includes a definition of tasalsul (infinite regress). The author begins by stating that the two proofs for the hal are refuted by the concept of hal itself. The refutation proceeds in three points: (1) states are similar (mutamathila) in subsistence (thubut) in their particulars, so their subsistence would increase and lead to an infinite regress; (2) states are predicated of their particulars—if states are existing (thabita), the particulars become particularized (tashakhkhus), otherwise the particulars are negated; (3) states inhere in other states that are accidents, leading to accident inhering in accident. An objection is raised: states do not admit similarity and difference, so their subsistence does not increase to cause regress; also, no state can be a universal while being a state, nor another a particular while being a locus; moreover, infinite regress is only impossible in the existent (mawjud), not in the subsistent (thabit). The author responds: the concepts of similarity and difference are necessarily accepted, and the impossibility of infinite regress will be discussed later. Then the first refutation is presented: if states were existing (thabita), they would share (mushtaraka) in subsistence. The passage then provides a multi-disciplinary definition of tasalsul: from language, hadith terminology, philosophy, and theology, distinguishing between absolute regress and the impossible regress (which involves infinite things coexisting in existence).

ثبوت
تماثل
تسلسل
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think states are completely unique and incomparable.
Correction: The author shows that states share in subsistence, making them comparable and leading to regress.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the first problem with states?
A) They are similar in subsistence leading to infinite regress
B) They cannot be predicated of particulars
C) They cause accidents to inhere in substances
D) They are not real

Key Takeaway
The refutation of the state relies on showing that if states were real, they would share in subsistence, leading to an infinite regress that is considered impossible.

Definition of Tasalsul (Infinite Regress) by Discipline
Sanusi defines tasalsul across multiple disciplines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various definitions of tasalsul across language, hadith, philosophy, and theology, and distinguish between absolute and impossible regress.

Core Idea
Tasalsul is the arrangement of infinite things; only the existential infinite regress is impossible.

After refuting the proofs for the state using infinite regress, the author provides a comprehensive definition of tasalsul. This is important because the term is used in multiple disciplines with different nuances. Understanding these definitions helps clarify which type of regress is considered impossible in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic definitions slowly. Notice the different fields: language, hadith, philosophy, theology.
The author distinguishes between absolute regress and impossible regress.
2
Compare the definitions
Note the common thread: arrangement of infinite things. But the conditions vary: in philosophy, they must be coexisting in existence; in theology, the impossible regress is specifically the existential one.
The key difference is the 'coexistence in existence' condition.
3
Understand why only the existential regress is impossible
The author states that the impossible tasalsul is the arrangement of infinite things coexisting in existence. This implies that other forms of regress (like logical or mental) may be possible.
This connects back to the refutation: the regress of states would be existential, hence impossible.

Refutation of the Two Proofs for al-Ḥāl
Author refutes two proofs for al-ḥāl and answers the objection with two expositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the proofs for the theory of al-ḥāl by showing that it leads to infinite regress and the problem of accident subsisting in accident.

Core Idea
The state cannot be both similar in existence and different in particularities without causing regress.

This passage from al-Sanūsī's refutation targets the theory of al-ḥāl (the state), a concept in kalām theology that attempts to explain attributes. The author argues that if states are real, they lead to logical problems such as infinite regress and the subsistence of accidents in other accidents. An objection is raised, but the author defends his refutation with two detailed expositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation statement
The author presents his initial objection: states are similar in existence but differ in particularities, leading to regress and problems of predication.
2
Understand the objection
The opponent argues that states do not admit similarity or difference, denying the regress.
The opponent also claims regress only applies to existents, not non-existents.
3
Follow the author's reply
The author insists that similarity and difference are necessary, and regress will be discussed later.
4
Examine the first exposition
The author elaborates: if states are established, they share existence but differ, so existence is additional and leads to regress.
5
Examine the second exposition
The author shows that a universal state predicated on particulars leads to the same problems, including accident-subsisting-in-accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ونوقض الوجهان بالحال فإن الأحوال متماثلة في الثبوت في الخصوصيات فيزيد ثبوتها ويتسلسل وإنها تحمل على جزئياتها، فإن كانت ثابتة تشخصت وإلا انتفت وعلى أحوال هي أعراض فيقوم العرض بالعرض.
فإن قيل : الأحوال لا تقبل التماثل والاختلاف، فلا يزيد ثبوتها ليتسلسل، ولا يكون شيء منها كلياً وحالاً، والآخر جزئياً ومحلاً على أن التسلسل إنما يمتنع في الموجود دون الثابت.
قلنا: قبول المفهومين التماثل والاختلاف ضروري وامتناع التسلسل سيجيء.
تقرير الأول : أن الأحوال لو كانت ثابتة لكانت متشاركة في الثبوت متخالفة بالخصوصيات، فكان ثبوتها زائداً عليها ضرورة أن ما به الاشتراك مخالف لما به الامتياز، وثبوتها ليس بمنفي فيكون ثابتاً، وبتسلسل لما ذكرتم في الوجود .
وتقرير الثاني : أن الحال قد يكون كلياً محمولاً على جزئيات ثابتة، فإن كان ثابتاً كان متشخصاً، وإن كان منفياً امتنع كونه جزءاً من الثابت، وكذا إذا كان جنساً لأنواع، وإذا كان من أجناس الأعراض، لزم قيام العرض بالعرض على ما ذكرتم فما هو جوابكم ؟ فهو جوابنا
He said: And the two aspects are refuted by the state (al-ḥāl): for states (al-aḥwāl) are similar in existence (thubūt) but different in particularities, so their existence would increase and lead to infinite regress (tasalsul), and they are predicated on their particulars: if they are established they become particularized, otherwise they are negated, and they are states that are accidents, so an accident would subsist in an accident. If it is said: States do not admit similarity and difference, so their existence does not increase to cause regress, and none of them is universal and a state, and the other particular and a locus, given that regress is only impossible in existents, not in non-existents. We say: The two concepts admitting similarity and difference are necessary, and the impossibility of regress will come later. Exposition of the first: That if states were established, they would share in existence but differ in particularities, so their existence would be additional to them, necessarily because that by which they share is different from that by which they are distinguished, and their existence is not negated, so it is established, and would lead to regress as you mentioned in the case of existence. Exposition of the second: That the state may be universal predicated on established particulars: if it is established, it becomes particularized; if it is negated, it is impossible for it to be part of the established. Likewise if it is a genus for species, and if it is from the genera of accidents, then an accident would subsist in an accident as you mentioned. So what is your answer? That is our answer.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the two proofs for the theory of al-ḥāl (the state). The author begins by stating that the two proofs are refuted by invoking the concept of the state itself. He argues that states are similar in their existence (thubūt) but differ in their particularities (khuṣūṣiyyāt), so their existence would be additional and lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul). Also, states are predicated on their particulars; if they are real (thābita), they become particularized, otherwise they are negated. Moreover, states are considered accidents, so an accident would subsist in an accident, which is problematic. Then an objection is raised: states do not admit similarity and difference, so no regress, and regress only applies to existent things, not to non-existents. The author replies that the concepts of similarity and difference are necessary attributes of states, and the impossibility of regress will be addressed later. He then provides two expositions to elaborate the refutation: first, if states are established, they share in existence but differ in particularities, so existence is additional, leading to regress. Second, a state may be universal and predicated on particulars; if it is established, it becomes particularized, and if not, it cannot be a part of the established. This leads to the same problem. The author concludes by challenging the opponent to provide a different answer.

Key Terms
ḥāl
thubūt
tamāthul
ikhtilāf
tasalsul

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: States are considered non-existent entities that can be predicated without causing regress.
Correction: The author shows that if states are real, they must share existence and differ, leading to infinite regress; if they are not real, they cannot be part of the established.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used by the author to refute the proofs for al-ḥāl?
A) States are similar in existence but different in particularities, leading to regress.
B) States are not real.
C) States cannot be predicated.
D) States are identical to their objects.

Key Takeaway
The refutation of al-ḥāl hinges on the impossibility of similarity and difference in existence without leading to infinite regress.

Are you willing to follow the logic to its end, even if it forces you to change?

Second Objection and Defense of al-Ḥāl Theory
Second objection and reply on al-ḥāl theory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to the theory of states (al-ḥāl) and the author's defense based on the intelligibility of similarity and difference.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference depend on what is understood from a thing, not on its existence.

The debate over states (al-aḥwāl) now faces a second objection: if states are not existent, can they admit similarity and difference? The opponent argues they cannot, because similarity and difference are properties of existents. The author replies by grounding similarity and difference in the intelligible content (al-maʿqūl) of a thing, regardless of its existential status. This passage also addresses the scope of the impossibility of regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opponent's argument that states cannot admit similarity or difference because these properties belong only to existents. Notice the claim that this avoids regress.
The opponent also questions whether the impossibility of regress applies to non-existent entities.
2
Analyze the author's first reply
The author asserts that similarity and difference are necessary and based on the intelligible content (al-maʿqūl) of a thing: if two things share the same meaning, they are similar; otherwise different.
This response is independent of whether the thing is existent or non-existent.
3
Examine the second and third replies
The author addresses the claim that similarity/difference require an existent substrate, stating that attributes can subsist in non-existents (thābit). Then he argues that regress can indeed be impossible in non-existents as well.
This refutes the opponent's claim that regress only applies to existents.
4
Understand the dismissal of the Imām's concern
The author dismisses the concern that allowing regress in non-existents would undermine proofs of the eternal Creator, because those proofs only prohibit regress in existents.
Thus the defense of states remains intact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الحال لا تقبل التماثل والاختلاف لأن ذلك من الموجود فلا يتحقق فيها ما به الاشتراك وما به الاختلاف ليلزم زيادة ثبوتها ، ويتسلسل، ولا يتعين حال للكلية، وآخر للجزئية، أو حال للحالية. وآخر للمحلية ليلزم ما ذكرتم بخلاف الموجودات فإنها قابلة لذلك باعترافكم . وأيضاً لا نسلم : استحالة التسلسل في الأمور الثابتة، وإنما قام الدليل على استحالته في الموجودات .
قلنا : قبولها التماثل والاختلاف ضروري لأن المعقول من الشيء. إن كان هو المعقول من الآخر فهما متماثلان، وإلا فمختلفان.
وما قيل : إنهم جعلوا التماثل والاختلاف إما حالاً أو صفة، وعلى التقديرين فلا يقوم إلا بالموجود ليس بشيء، لأن الصفة قد تقوم بالثابت، وإن لم يكن موجوداً وإن أريد أنه حال أو صفة موجودة فممنوع ، واستحالة التسلسل في الأمور الثابتة، مما قام عليه بعض أدلة امتناع التسلسل على ما سيجيء، وأما ما ذكره الإمام. من أنا لو جوزناه أفسد إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وإثبات الصانع القديم فضعيف لأنا لا نجوزه في الموجودات، وبه يتم إثبات الصانع، وتقرير القوم في النقض بالحال . أن الأحوال متخالفة
If it is said: The state does not admit similarity and difference because that is [a property] of the existent, so there is no realization in them of that by which they share and that by which they differ, to entail increase of their existence and regress, nor is a state determined for universality and another for particularity, or a state for state-ness and another for locus-ness, to entail what you mentioned, contrary to existents which are capable of that by your admission. Also, we do not concede the impossibility of regress in non-existent things; rather the proof only demonstrates its impossibility in existents. We say: Their admission of similarity and difference is necessary, because what is understood (al-maʿqūl) from a thing—if it is the same as what is understood from another, they are similar; otherwise they are different. And what is said—that they made similarity and difference either a state or an attribute, and in both cases it only subsists in an existent—is nothing, because the attribute may subsist in the non-existent (al-thābit) even if it is not existent. And if it is meant that it is a state or an attribute that is existent, it is denied. And the impossibility of regress in non-existent things is something that some proofs of the impossibility of regress have established, as will come. As for what the Imām mentioned, that if we allow it, it would corrupt the refutation of events without beginning and the proof of the Eternal Maker, it is weak, because we do not allow it in existents, and by that the proof of the Maker is completed. And the exposition of the people concerning the refutation by the state: that states are different...
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the refutation of al-ḥāl and the author's reply. The opponent argues that states (al-aḥwāl) cannot admit similarity or difference because these are properties of existent things (mawjūdāt). Therefore, there is no basis for claiming an increase in existence or regress, and no need to assign a state for universality or particularity. The opponent also denies that regress is impossible in non-existent (thābita) things; they claim the proof only applies to existents. The author replies that similarity and difference are necessary concepts because they depend on the intelligible content (al-maʿqūl) of the thing: if two things have the same intelligible content, they are similar; otherwise, they are different. Regarding the claim that similarity/difference must subsist in an existent, the author responds that an attribute can subsist in a non-existent (thābit) even if it is not an existent entity. The author further asserts that some proofs for the impossibility of regress apply to non-existents as well, and he dismisses the Imām's concern that allowing regress in non-existents would undermine proofs of the eternal Creator, because the argument only prohibits regress in existents. The passage ends with a reference to the exposition of the refutation by the state, cut off at the page break.

Correction: The author argues that some proofs of the impossibility of regress apply to non-existent things (thābita) as well, and that the opponent's restriction to existents is not universally supported.

C) Because states are mental constructs.
D) Because similarity and difference are not applicable to universals.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the theory of states by arguing that similarity and difference are determined by intelligible content, not by existential status, and that regress can apply to non-existents as well.

?

?

Objection and Response: Does the State Admit Similarity and Difference?
Objection and response regarding the state's capacity for similarity/difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the state does not admit similarity/difference and the author's response, including the arguments about infinite regress and the nature of attributes.

Core Idea
The state admits similarity and difference because intelligible content determines them, not existence.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection to the theory of states (aḥwāl). The objector argues that states cannot be similar or different because only existent things admit those categories. The author responds by clarifying the basis of similarity and difference in the intelligible concept itself, and rebuts the infinite regress objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection part of the passage. Note the objector's main points: similarity/difference only for existents, potential infinite regress, and denial of regress impossibility in subsistent things.
The objector uses the term 'al-mawjūd' (existent) and 'al-umūr al-thābita' (subsisting things).
2
Understand the author's response on similarity/difference
The author asserts that similarity and difference are based on the intelligible (maʿqūl) of a thing, not on existence. If two things share the same intelligible, they are similar; otherwise different.
This shifts the basis from ontology to conceptual content.
3
Examine the rebuttal on attribute subsistence
The author argues that an attribute can subsist in a subsistent (thābit) even if it is not existent, so similarity/difference can be states without being existent.
This counters the claim that attributes only subsist in existent things.
4
Consider the regress argument and the Imām's objection
The author states that impossibility of infinite regress applies to subsistent things as well, and dismisses the Imām's worry about breaking the proof of the Maker by limiting the denial of regress to existent things.
The author maintains that only denying regress in existent things suffices for the proof.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this debate matters: it defends the theory of states, which is crucial for understanding attributes in kalām without positing multiple eternal entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الحال لا تقبل التماثل والاختلاف لأن ذلك من الموجود فلا يتحقق فيها ما به الاشتراك وما به الاختلاف ليلزم زيادة ثبوتها ، ويتسلسل، ولا يتعين حال للكلية، وآخر للجزئية، أو حال للحالية. وآخر للمحلية ليلزم ما ذكرتم بخلاف الموجودات فإنها قابلة لذلك باعترافكم . وأيضاً لا نسلم : استحالة التسلسل في الأمور الثابتة، وإنما قام الدليل على استحالته في الموجودات .

قلنا : قبولها التماثل والاختلاف ضروري لأن المعقول من الشيء. إن كان هو المعقول من الآخر فهما متماثلان، وإلا فمختلفان.

وما قيل : إنهم جعلوا التماثل والاختلاف إما حالاً أو صفة، وعلى التقديرين فلا يقوم إلا بالموجود ليس بشيء، لأن الصفة قد تقوم بالثابت، وإن لم يكن موجوداً وإن أريد أنه حال أو صفة موجودة فممنوع ، واستحالة التسلسل في الأمور الثابتة، مما قام عليه بعض أدلة امتناع التسلسل على ما سيجيء، وأما ما ذكره الإمام من أنا لو جوزناه أفسد إبطال حوادث لا أول لها، وإثبات الصانع القديم فضعيف لأنا لا نجوزه في الموجودات، وبه يتم إثبات الصانع،
If it is said: The state (al-ḥāl) does not admit similarity and difference because that is (only) of the existent (al-mawjūd). So there does not obtain in it that by which sharing (al-ishtirāk) and that by which differentiation (al-ikhtilāf) occur, so that an increase of its subsistence (thubūt) would be necessary, and an infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. And neither a state for universality nor another for particularity, nor a state for statehood nor another for being-in-a-subject, is determined, so that what you mentioned would be necessary, unlike existing things (al-mawjūdāt), for they are capable of that by your admission. And also we do not grant the impossibility of infinite regress in subsisting things (al-umūr al-thābita); rather the proof has been established for its impossibility in existent things (al-mawjūdāt). We say: Their admission of similarity and difference is necessary, because the intelligible (al-maʿqūl) of a thing – if it is the same intelligible as that of another, they are similar (mutamāthilān), otherwise they are different (mukhtalifān). And what is said: They made similarity and difference either a state or an attribute, and on both estimations it subsists only in the existent – this is nothing, because the attribute may subsist in the subsistent (al-thābit) even if it is not existent. And if it is intended that it is a state or an existent attribute, it is denied. And the impossibility of infinite regress in subsisting things is something upon which some proofs of the impossibility of infinite regress have been established, as will come. As for what the Imām mentioned, that if we were to allow it, it would corrupt the refutation of beginningless events and the proof of the eternal Maker – this is weak, because we do not allow it in existent things, and by this the proof of the Maker is completed.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the theory of states (aḥwāl) and the author’s response. The objector argues that the state does not admit similarity or difference, because only existing things admit those. If the state admitted similarity/difference, it would require an additional state for the commonality and another for the difference, leading to an infinite regress. Moreover, the objector denies the impossibility of regress in subsisting (non-existent) things. The author answers: The state does admit similarity and difference, because the intelligible content of a thing determines whether it is similar or different. The claim that similarity/difference only subsist in the existent is false; an attribute can subsist in a subsistent something that is not existent. The impossibility of regress applies to subsisting things as well, as some proofs establish. As for the Imām’s argument that allowing regress would break the refutation of beginningless events and the proof of the eternal Maker, it is weak because we only deny regress in existent things, and through that the proof of the Maker is established.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The state must be an existent thing to admit similarity and difference.
Correction: The state admits similarity and difference because similarity/difference are determined by the intelligible content, not by existence. An attribute can subsist in a subsistent (non-existent) thing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the state admit similarity and difference?
A) Because it is an existent thing and all existents admit similarity/difference.
B) Because the intelligible of a thing determines similarity or difference.
C) Because the state is an attribute and all attributes admit similarity/difference.
D) Because the objector's claim is false by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The state (ḥāl) can be similar or different based on its intelligible content, and it does not require existence for that attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's attributes (like His knowledge or will), do you ever imagine them as separate existent things? How does the concept of a 'state' (ḥāl) challenge you to affirm their reality without compromising tawhid? Will you strive to refine your understanding of Allah's attributes based on this distinction?
?
In your daily judgments of people, do you tend to assess them by their external status (existence) or by the inner intelligible content of their character? How can you, like the author's reasoning, prioritize the maʿqūl over the mawjūd in your relationships?
?
The objector feared that allowing similarity in states would lead to infinite regress. Do you ever avoid deep theological reflection because you fear endless questions? How does the author's reassurance that regress is impossible in subsisting things challenge you to trust that there is a foundation for your faith? Will you commit to deeper study even when it feels uncomfortable?
