The Opponents' Infinite Regress Argument and the Imam's Response
Presents the opponents' infinite regress argument and the Imam's refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the opponents argue that the theory of states leads to an infinite regress and how the Imam refutes this by classifying statehood as a negative attribute.

Core Idea
Statehood is not a positive attribute, so it does not require another state.

The opponents press a second objection: if states share in being states, that commonality itself would be a state, leading to an endless chain. The Imam's reply is subtle but decisive — he reclassifies 'statehood' as a negative attribute, not a positive one. This breaks the regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the passage is dense; take your time to understand the logical flow.
The passage has two clear parts: the opponents' objection and the Imam's response.
2
Identify the opponents' argument
Find the statements about sharing and differentiating. Understand why they claim a regress follows from the commonality of being a state.
Look for the phrases 'بخصوصياتها' and 'في عموم كونها حالاً'.
3
Examine the Imam's response
Notice how the Imam denies that 'statehood' is a positive attribute. Understand what a negative attribute means here and why it stops the regress.
The key phrase is 'الحالية ليست صفة ثبوتية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقرير القوم في النقض بالحال . أن الأحوال متخالفة بخصوصياتها، ومتشاركة في عموم كونها حالاً ، وما بـه المشاركة غير ما به الممايزة فيلزم أن يكون للحال حال آخر إلى غير النهاية. ودفعه الإمام بأن الحالية ليست صفة ثبوتية، حتى يلزم أن يكون للحال حال آخر وذلك لأنــه لا معنى للحال إلا ما يكون موجوداً ، ولا معدوماً وهو صفة سلبية فلا يكون الاشتراك فيها اشتراكاً في حال ليلزم تسلسل الأحوال.
And the people's formulation in the refutation by the state: That the states (al-aḥwāl) differ in their particularities (khuṣūṣiyyāt) and share in the generality of being a state (ḥālan). And that by which sharing occurs is other than that by which differentiation occurs, so it would be necessary that the state have another state ad infinitum. And the Imam's repelling of it: That statehood (al-ḥāliyya) is not a positive (thubūtiyya) attribute, such that it would be necessary that the state have another state. That is because there is no meaning of 'state' except what is neither existent nor non-existent, and it is a negative (salbiyya) attribute. So the sharing in it is not a sharing in a state, so that a regress of states would be necessary.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument from the opponents against the theory of states, and the Imam’s response. The opponents argue: states differ in their particular properties but share in the general characteristic of being a state. Since what makes them share is different from what makes them differ, the state itself would require another state for that commonality, leading to an infinite regress. The Imam replies by denying that 'statehood' is a positive attribute; rather it is a negative attribute, meaning it is neither existent nor non-existent. Therefore, the sharing in being a state is not a sharing in a positive state that would require another state. The regress is thus avoided.

Misconception: Statehood is a positive attribute that would require another state.
Correction: Statehood is a negative attribute; it does not cause a regress.

Checkpoint
How does the Imam prevent the infinite regress of states according to this passage?
A) By denying that states have particularities.
B) By asserting that statehood is a positive attribute that stops the regress.
C) By stating that statehood is a negative attribute, not a positive one.
D) By claiming that the opponents' argument is invalid because states do not share in anything.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's classification of statehood as a negative attribute prevents the infinite regress of states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own thinking about your status before Allah, do you treat your 'state' (like being a believer) as a positive attribute that you must keep proving, or as a negative attribute that simply negates disbelief? How might that change your anxiety about validation?
?
The opponents assumed that sharing a commonality (statehood) requires a separate reality. Where in your life do you create unnecessary complexity by assuming a shared trait must be a 'thing' rather than a mere description?
?
If statehood is a negative attribute, then what you are not (non-existent, non-disbeliever) defines you more than what you are. What is one 'negative' truth about yourself that, if embraced, would stop a cycle of overthinking or pride?

The Hakim's Refutation: The State as a Fixed Description
The hakim argues that the state is not pure negation but a fixed description, neither existent nor non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verifying sage argues that the state (al-ḥāl) is not a pure negation but a fixed description with positive content, and how this refutes the Imam's view.

Core Idea
The state is not mere negation; it is a fixed description that exists only in connection with a subject.

In this passage, the verifying sage challenges the Imam's claim that statehood is purely negative. He argues that the state carries a positive meaning beyond absence, using the example of the impossible to show that not every neither-existent-nor-existent thing qualifies as a state. Let's examine his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the key terms like 'salb mahḍ' (pure negation) and 'waṣf thābit' (fixed description).
The passage begins with 'ذاتان' (two selves) as a heading.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where the hakim defines the state: 'ليس سلباً محضاً، بل هو وصف ثابت للموجود ليس بموجود ولا معدوم'. Contrast this with the impossible (mustaḥīl) which is also neither existent nor non-existent but is not a state.
This shows that state includes a positive meaning beyond mere negation.
3
Understand the argument about similarity and difference
The hakim explains that states share a common meaning, but they are not described by similarity (tamāthul) or difference (ikhtilāf) because those apply to selves (dhātāt) which are perceived independently, whereas states are not.
This refutes the Imam's reduction of state to pure negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاتان ورده الحكيم المحقق، بأن الحال عندهم ليس سلباً محضاً، بل هو وصف ثابت للموجود ليس بموجود ولا معدوم، ولهذا لم يجعلوا المستحيل حالاً مع أنه ليس بموجود ولا معدوم ، فإذن الحال يشتمل عندهم على معنى غير سلب الوجود والعدم يختص بتلك الأمور التي يسمونها حالاً، وتشترك الأحوال فيه وهي لا توصف بالتماثل والاختلاف، لأن المثلين عندهم يفهم منهما معنى واحد والمختلفان ذاتان لا يفهم منهما معنى واحد، والحال ليس بذات لأنها التي تدرك بالانفراد والحال لا تدرك بالانفراد والمشترك ليس بمدرك بالانفراد حتى يحكم بأن المدرك من أحدهما هو المدرك من الآخر أو ليس .
Two selves (dhātān). And the verifying sage (al-ḥakīm al-muḥaqqiq) refuted it: That the state (al-ḥāl) according to them is not a pure negation (salb mahḍ), but rather it is a fixed description (waṣf thābit) of the existent, neither existent nor non-existent. For this reason they did not make the impossible (al-mustaḥīl) a state, even though it is neither existent nor non-existent. So then the state according to them includes a meaning other than the negation of existence and non-existence, specific to those things which they call states. And the states share in it, and they are not described by similarity and difference, because the two similars (al-mithlān) according to them have one meaning understood from them, and the two differents (al-mukhtalifān) are two selves (dhātān) from which one meaning is not understood. And the state is not a self (dhāt), because the self is that which is perceived (tudrak) by itself (bi-al-infirād), and the state is not perceived by itself. And the shared (al-mushtarak) is not perceived by itself, so that it could be judged that the perceived from one of them is the perceived from the other or not.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the verifying sage (the hakim) of the Imam's view that statehood is a negative attribute. The hakim argues that the state is not a pure negation; rather it is a fixed description of the existent that is neither existent nor non-existent. This is why the impossible (like a square circle) is not considered a state, even though it is also neither existent nor non-existent. The state therefore includes a positive meaning beyond mere negation. States share in this meaning, but they are not described by similarity and difference. The reason is that similarity and difference apply to selves (dhātāt) which are perceived by themselves. A state is not a self because it is not perceived by itself; it is only perceived in connection with the subject. Therefore, the shared meaning among states is not a self, and thus similarity and difference do not apply to states in the same way. This refutes the Imam's claim that statehood is merely negative.

Key Terms
ذات
حال
سلب محض
وصف ثابت
تماثل
اختلاف
إدراك بالانفراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the state is a pure negation (salb mahḍ), i.e., just the absence of existence and non-existence.
Correction: The hakim shows that the state includes a positive meaning because the impossible is also neither existent nor non-existent, yet it is not a state.

Checkpoint
Why does the hakim say that the state is not a pure negation?
A) Because the state is an existent thing.
B) Because the impossible is not a state, even though it is neither existent nor non-existent.
C) Because the state is a self (dhāt) that is perceived by itself.
D) Because statehood is a negative attribute.

Key Takeaway
The state is a fixed description with positive content, not a mere negation, and because it is not independently perceived, similarity and difference do not apply to it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your imān fluctuates—today strong, tomorrow weak. Do you treat these fluctuations as mere absences of something, or as fixed descriptions of your current condition? How would your self-accountability change if you saw each state as a positive description that you are responsible for transforming?
?
You often label things as 'impossible' in your spiritual life: defeating a bad habit, attaining sincerity, or loving a difficult person. But according to this lesson, the impossible is not a state—it is pure non-existence. Is your 'impossible' actually a state that you have not yet changed? What fixed description have you settled for that Allah calls you to replace?
?
When you describe yourself by your anger, your laziness, or your generosity, do you treat those descriptions as if they are your very self—unchanging and independent? The hakim says a state is not a self. Can you, right now, separate your identity from your states and begin to see them as descriptions that can be rewritten through action and reliance on Allah?

The Third Proof: Bringing into Existence as a State
Proof that ījād is a state and response using predication example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'bringing into existence' (ījād) must be a state (ḥāl) and how the response using predication defends this view.

Core Idea
Ījād is neither existent nor non-existent; it is a state bridging action and attribute.

The third proof for states argues that the act of bringing into existence cannot be classified as existent or non-existent. This passage presents the dilemma and a response that uses the example of blindness. Although the text is cut off, the core argument and its defense are clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof dilemma
Read the passage carefully. Note the two alternatives: if ījād were existent, it would require another ījād leading to regress; if it were non-existent, the agent wouldn't be a bringer into existence.
This is a classic kalam argument.
2
Understand the response
The response argues that absence of the predicate's subject does not invalidate the predication itself, as shown by the example 'Zayd is blind'.
3
Connect to states theory
Recognize that ījād, like blindness, is a state: something attributed but not an independent existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أدلة إثبات الحال (قال: الثالث : الإيجاد ليس بموجود، وإلا احتاج إلى إيجاد آخر وتسلسل ولا معدوم وإلا لما كان الفاعل موجداً .

قلنا : عدم مبدأ المحمول لا يستلزم عدم الحمل، فزيد أعمى، مع أن
From the proofs of establishing the state: He said: The third: Bringing into existence (al-ījād) is not existent (mawjūd), otherwise it would need another bringing into existence and there would be a regress, nor is it non-existent (maʿdūm), otherwise the agent (al-fāʿil) would not be a bringer into existence. We said: The absence of the subject (mabdaʾ) of the predicate (al-maḥmūl) does not necessitate the absence of the predication (al-ḥaml); for Zayd is blind, even though
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of the state (ḥāl). The proof argues that the act of bringing into existence (ījād) cannot be existent, because if it were existent, it would need another bringing into existence, leading to an infinite regress. Nor can it be non-existent, because if it were non-existent, the agent would not be a bringer into existence. Thus, ījād must be a state, i.e., something that is neither existent nor non-existent. The author then gives the response: The absence of the subject of the predicate does not entail the absence of the predication itself. The example given is 'Zayd is blind': the predicate 'blindness' is attributed to Zayd even though blindness is not an existent thing in Zayd (it is a privation). Similarly, ījād can be predicated of the agent without being an existent attribute. The text is cut off but the argument is clear.

Correction: The states theory posits that ījād is a state (ḥāl), which is neither existent nor non-existent but a real attribute that does not subsist in a subject.

C) Because the agent is not a bringer into existence if ījād is non-existent.
D) Because it is a pure negation.

Key Takeaway
The act of bringing into existence is best understood as a state (ḥāl), not an existent or non-existent attribute.

?

Think of a negative attribute you often apply to yourself (e.g., 'I am weak,' 'I am forgetful'). Is that attribute a 'thing' or a 'state'? How does viewing it as a state (neither existent nor non-existent) affect your responsibility and hope for change? What does this teach you about how God relates His attributes to His essence?
?

When you contemplate creation, do you imagine God's power as a 'thing' or as a 'state' always present? The Absurd Consequences of Affirming Thingness of the Non-Existent and the State
Al-Taftazani enumerates four logical derivations accepted by proponents of shayʾiyyat al-maʿdūm and al-ḥāl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proponents of the thingness of the non-existent and the state are led to several logical consequences that ultimately undermine their position.

Core Idea
Affirming a middle state between existence and non-existence leads to absurdities.

The author al-Taftazani now turns to the logical consequences (tafrīʿāt) that follow from the principles of shayʾiyyat al-maʿdūm and al-ḥāl. By listing these consequences, he aims to demonstrate the incoherence of the position. The passage enumerates four claims that these proponents are forced to accept, each of which contradicts sound rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Note the four items listed.
The passage uses 'such as' (مثل) to introduce the examples.
2
Identify each derivation
Find the four claims: (1) essences in non-existence are infinite, (2) the effective cause has no influence on them, (3) no distinction among them, (4) certainty of the Creator while doubting His existence.
Each derivation is separated by 'و' (and).
3
Consider why each is problematic
Reflect on why each claim contradicts accepted theological principles. The commentary will help, but try to think of the issues yourself.
For example, how can there be infinite actual things in non-existence? How can the Creator have no influence?
4
Understand the author's purpose
Recognize that al-Taftazani is not endorsing these derivations; he is presenting them as absurd consequences to refute the underlying principles.
The phrase 'they have derivations' (لهم تفريعات) shows these are the opponents' views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر تفريعات المثبتين لشيئية المعدوم والحال. قال: ولهم على الأصلين تفريعات مثل اتفاقهم على أن الذوات المتحققة في العدم غير متناهية، ولا تأثير للمؤثر فيها، ولا تباين بينها، وأنه يجوز القطع، بأن للعالم صانعاً متصفاً بالحياة والقدرة والعلم مع الشك في وجوده.
Mention of the derivations (tafrīʿāt) of those who affirm the thingness (shayʾiyya) of the non-existent and the state. He said: They have derivations based on the two principles, such as their agreement that the essences (dhawāt) realized in non-existence are infinite, that the effective cause has no influence on them, that there is no distinction among them, and that it is permissible to be certain that the world has a Creator characterized by life, power, and knowledge while doubting His existence.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage records the logical consequences (tafrīʿāt) that proponents of shayʾiyyat al-maʿdūm (thingness of the non-existent) and al-ḥāl (the state) accept based on those two principles. The author (al-Taftāzānī) is likely listing these as objections, showing that their position leads to absurdities. The consequences are: (1) The essences realized in non-existence are infinite—implying that there are infinite actual things in non-existence. (2) The effective cause (al-muʾaththir) has no influence on these non-existent essences—which undermines the role of the creator. (3) There is no distinction (tabāyun) among these essences—making them indiscernible. (4) It is possible to be certain that the world has a Creator with attributes like life, power, and knowledge, while simultaneously doubting His existence—a contradiction, as certainty and doubt about the same thing cannot coexist. By presenting these derivations, the author intends to show the incoherence of affirming a middle state between existence and non-existence.

Key Terms

الحال
التفريعات
الذوات المتحققة في العدم
المؤثر
التباين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is endorsing these derivations as valid conclusions.
Correction: Al-Taftazani presents these derivations as absurd consequences that follow from the principles of shayʾiyyat al-maʿdūm and al-ḥāl, in order to refute those principles. The passage is part of a polemic, not an affirmation.

Checkpoint
What is one of the derivations listed for proponents of shayʾiyyat al-maʿdūm and al-ḥāl?
A) Non-existent essences are finite.
B) The effective cause has unlimited influence on non-existent essences.
C) There is no distinction among non-existent essences.
D) One cannot be certain of the Creator's existence while also doubting it.

Key Takeaway
The four derivations show that affirming thingness of the non-existent leads to conclusions that contradict sound rational theology, thereby refuting the position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The lesson shows that a middle state between existence and non-existence leads to absurdities. Where in your heart have you been trying to maintain a middle ground between full submission to God and partial indifference? What step can you take today to fully commit to His reality?
?
The fourth derivation reveals that certainty and doubt cannot coexist. Examine your own conviction: Do you truly believe in the Creator's attributes as described, or do you sometimes act as if He is unaware? What practical change would align your conduct with your creed?

The State of Bringing-into-Existence: Objection and Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes an argument for the existence of the state (hal).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the argument that the state (hal) of 'bringing-into-existence' must be either existent or non-existent, and why he chooses the latter without undermining predication.

Core Idea
A predicate can be true even if the attribute it names is non-existent in the external world.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses a key objection to the existence of states (ahwal). The objector tries to force a dilemma: the state of 'bringing-into-existence' (ijad) must be either existent or non-existent, both with problematic consequences. Al-Taftazani responds by choosing the second horn and showing it does not lead to absurdity, using analogies like 'Zayd is blind' and 'impossible conjunction.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the objector's argument is presented, followed by 'wa'l-jawab' indicating the refutation.
Pay attention to the key term 'ijad' (bringing-into-existence) as a state.
2
Identify the dilemma and its horns
The objector argues that ijad cannot be existent (due to infinite regress) and cannot be non-existent (otherwise the agent wouldn't be called a bringer-into-existence). Find these two horns in the text.
The dilemma is a classic logical argument to force a conclusion.
3
Understand the refutation and the analogy
Al-Taftazani chooses non-existence and denies the entailment. He uses 'Zayd is blind' as an example where blindness is non-existent yet the predication holds. See how this parallels the case of ijad.
The key principle: affirmative predication does not require the attribute to exist in the external world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي من وجوه إثبات الحال : إن الإيجاد ليس بموجود، وإلا احتاج إلى إيجاد له محتاج إلى آخر ، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له، ولا معدوم، وإلا لما كان الفاعل موجدا، لأنه بعد صدور المعلول عنه، لم يحصل له صفة وكما لم یکن قبل الصدور موجداً ، فكذا ،بعده لا يقال إيجاد الإيجاد عينه، لأنا نقول : مثل هذا لا يصح في الأمور الموجودة، لأن ذلك إيجاد للمعلول، وهذا إيجاد للوصف الذي هو إيجاد. والجواب إنا نختار أنه معدوم ، ولا نسلم لزوم أن لا يكون الفاعل موجداً، فإن صحة الحمل الإيجاب لا تنافي كون الوصف، الذي أخذ منه المحمول معدوماً وما في الخارج كما في قولنا زيد أعمى في الخارج، واجتماع الضدين ممتنع في الخارج، مع أن كلاً من العمى والامتناع معدوم في الخارج.
That is, from the proofs of establishing the state (al-hal): that bringing-into-existence (al-ijad) is not existent, otherwise it would need a bringing-into-existence for it, which would need another, and so on ad infinitum; and it is not non-existent, otherwise the agent would not be a bringer-into-existence, because after the effect issues from him, no attribute has accrued to him, and just as before the issuing he was not a bringer-into-existence, so after it, it cannot be said that the bringing-into-existence of the bringing-into-existence is identical to it. For we say: such [reasoning] is not valid for existent things, because that is bringing the effect into existence, and this is bringing the description which is bringing-into-existence into existence. And the answer: we choose that it is non-existent, and we do not grant the entailment that the agent is not a bringer-into-existence, for the validity of affirmative predication does not conflict with the description from which the predicate is taken being non-existent and [not] in the external world, as in our saying 'Zayd is blind in the external world' and 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible in the external world', while both blindness and impossibility are non-existent in the external world.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the ontological status of the 'state' (hal), specifically the state of 'bringing-into-existence' (ijad). The objector argues that ijad cannot be existent (otherwise infinite regress) nor non-existent (otherwise the agent would not be called a 'bringer-into-existence'). The answerer (al-Taftazani) chooses the second horn, asserting that ijad is non-existent, but this does not negate the agent's description as a bringer-into-existence. The analogy of 'Zayd is blind' shows that a true affirmative predication can have a non-existent attribute as predicate. Similarly, 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' predicates impossibility, which is non-existent. Thus the objection fails. The key principle is that the truth of a predication does not require the independent existence of the attribute in the external world.

Key Terms
الحال
الإيجاد
المعدوم
الصفة
الحمل الإيجاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that for a predicate like 'bringer-into-existence' to be true, the state of bringing-into-existence must exist as a real entity in the external world.
Correction: The passage shows that a true affirmative predication can be made even when the attribute (like 'blindness' or 'impossibility') is non-existent in the external world.

Checkpoint

A) It is existent.
B) It is non-existent.
C) It is neither existent nor non-existent.
D) It is a real attribute in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The truth of an affirmative predication does not require the attribute to exist in the external world, as shown by analogies like 'Zayd is blind' and 'impossibility of opposites.'

Are there claims you make about yourself that you rely on no present, tangible evidence to justify? Will you examine those claims today and ask whether they are true despite the absence of external reality?

Why Affirm Subsistence of the Non-Existent?
Al-Taftazani explains the first root of confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars claimed that non-existent things have a fixed essence or subsistence in external reality, based on the distinction between causes of essence and causes of existence.

Core Idea
Non-existents can have a fixed essence even when not existing.

Al-Taftazani begins with an objection: if the falsehood of subsistence of the non-existent and the intermediate state is so clear, why did many intelligent people accept it? He responds by isolating two distinct bases of error. This lesson focuses on the first: the intuition that a non-existent like blackness is still blackness in reality, because its essence does not depend on the causes of existence. This led some to speak of 'subsistence in external reality' for possible non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first basis
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the objection sets up two bases but here we focus on the first.
The passage is dense; let the author's own words guide you.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the text says 'the causes of essence are other than the causes of existence.' This is the core insight that leads to the error.
3
Understand the reasoning
The mind judges that blackness is blackness in reality even if it does not exist. This judgment is about the essence, not about its existence. Scholars then called this 'subsistence in external reality' to highlight a kind of determination.
This is similar to the philosophers' claim that essences are not created by the Maker.
4
Distinguish impossible and possible non-existents
The passage notes that only possible non-existents have essences that can be described by existence sometimes. Impossible things have no such essence.
5
Reflect on the error
The error was to treat this conceptual subsistence as a real property in the external world. Understand why later theologians rejected this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قلت بطلان ثبوت المعدوم والواسطة في غاية الجلاء، فكيف ذهب على الكثير من العقلاء؟ قلت: كان مبنى الأول: على أن السواد المعدوم مثلا سواد في الخارج لا يتعلق سواديته بأسباب الوجود. والثاني: على أن من الصفات ما قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة ولا سبيل إلى نفيها لاتصاف الموجود بها وجد فرض العقل أو لم يوجد، كالوجود والإيجاد والعالمية واللونية فجزموا بأنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة. لما كان بطلان القول بثبوت المعدوم في الخارج وتحقق الواسطة بينه وبين الموجود جلياً بل ضرورياً، وقد ذهب إليهما سيما إلى تحقق الواسطة العلماء المحققون، حاول التنبيه على ما يصلح مظنة للاشتباه في المقامين. أما الأول فهو أن العقل جازم بأن السواد سواد في الواقع وإن لم توجد أسباب الوجود من الفاعل والقابل، فإن أسباب الماهية غير أسباب الوجود على ما سيجيء. فعبروا عن هذا المعنى بالثبوت في الخارج، لما رأوا فيه من شائبة التقرر والتحقق مع نفيهم الوجود الذهني، وهو قريب من قول الفلاسفة: أن الماهيات ليست بجعل الجاعل. وحاصله: أنهم وجدوا تفرقة بين الممتنعات والمعدومات الممكنة، فإن لها ماهيات تتصف بالوجود تارة وتتعرى عنه أخرى بحسب حصول أسباب الوجود ولا حصولها، فعبروا عن ذلك بالشيئية والثبوت في الخارج. وأما الثاني: فهو أنهم وجدوا بعض ما يتصف به الموجود كوجود الإنسان وإيجاد الله تعالى إياه وعالمية زيد ولونية السواد قد قام الدليل على أنه ليس بموجود، ولم يكن لهم سبيل إلى الحكم بأنه لا تحقق له أصلاً لما رأوا الموجودات تتصف به سواء وجد اعتبار العقل أو لم يوجد، على أنه لو وجد اعتبار العقل وفرضه فهو عندهم ليس بموجود في العقل، فجزموا بأن لهذا النوع من المعاني تحققاً ما في الخارج، وليست بموجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة، وسموه بالحال. توضيحه: أنه إذا صدر المعلول عن العلة، فنحن نجد في كل منهما صفة كانت معدومة قبل الصدور، أعني الموجدية والوجود، فلا تكون حينئذ معدومة، ضرورة التفرقة بين الحالين وقد قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة فتكون واسطة.
He said: If you say the falsity of the subsistence of the non-existent and the intermediate state is extremely clear, so how did it escape many of the intelligent? I say: The basis of the first is that blackness when non-existent, for example, is blackness in external reality, its blackness is not connected to the causes of existence. And the second is that among the attributes there are those for which proof has established that they are not existent, and there is no way to deny them because the existent is described by them whether the supposition of the mind exists or not, such as existence, bringing-into-existence, omniscience, and blackness; so they concluded that they are neither existent nor non-existent. Since the falsity of the statement of the subsistence of the non-existent in external reality and the realization of an intermediate between it and the existent is evident, nay necessary, and yet the investigating scholars have adopted them, especially the realization of the intermediate, he attempted to draw attention to what might be a source of confusion in the two positions. As for the first, it is that the mind judges decisively that blackness is blackness in reality even if the causes of existence from agent and recipient do not exist, for the causes of essence are other than the causes of existence as will come. So they expressed this meaning by subsistence in external reality, because they saw in it a tinge of determination and realization together with their denial of mental existence, and it is close to the statement of the philosophers that essences are not by the making of the maker. Its gist is that they found a distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents: they have essences which are sometimes described by existence and sometimes divested of it according to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the causes of existence, so they expressed that by thingness and subsistence in external reality. As for the second, it is that they found some of what the existent is described by, such as the existence of man, God's bringing him into existence, Zayd's omniscience, and the blackness of blackness, regarding which proof has established that it is not existent, and they had no way to judge that it has no reality at all because they saw that existents are described by it whether the consideration of the mind exists or not, and even if the consideration and supposition of the mind exist, it is in their view not existent in the mind; so they concluded that this type of meanings has a certain realization in external reality, and they are neither existent nor non-existent but intermediate, and they named it the state. Its clarification: when the effect issues from the cause, we find in each of them an attribute that was non-existent before the issuing, namely causal-efficacy and existence; then they are not at that time non-existent, due to the necessity of distinguishing between the two states, and proof has established that they are not existent, so they are intermediate.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector notes that the falsehood of the subsistence of non-existents and of an intermediate state is obvious, so why did many intelligent people believe in them? The answer distinguishes two bases of error. The first basis concerns non-existents: some thought that a non-existent like blackness has a fixed essence (sawād) in reality independent of existence, because the essence's definition does not depend on existence—thus they affirmed a 'subsistence in the external world' (thubūt fī al-khārij). This is akin to the philosophers' claim that essences are not created. The second basis concerns an intermediate state: attributes like existence, God's act of causing existence (ījād), and universal quiddities (e.g., 'human-ness') are not existent in themselves but are not nothing, because existents are described by them even without mental consideration. So they posited a category called 'state' (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The commentator then clarifies each: for the first, the mind still affirms blackness as blackness even without external existence, leading to talk of 'subsistence'; for the second, attributes like causal-efficacy (mūjidiyya) and existence itself in a cause-effect relation are not existent but also not non-existent, hence intermediate. The argument proceeds step by step, showing how both errors arise from subtle philosophical distinctions.

Key Takeaway
The belief in subsistence of the non-existent arose from the valid insight that the essence of a possible thing is not dependent on its existence.

?

The Intermediate State (Ḥāl) Explained
Al-Taftazani explains the second root of confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some scholars posited an intermediate state (ḥāl) for attributes like existence and causation, which are neither existent nor non-existent.

Core Idea
Some attributes are real but not existent—they are intermediate states.

Al-Taftazani now turns to the second basis of error: the claim of an intermediate state between existence and non-existence for certain attributes. Scholars noticed that attributes such as existence ('existence of Zayd'), God's act of causing existence (ījād), and universal quiddities like 'human-ness' are predicated of existents, yet proofs show they are not themselves existent. Since these cannot be denied (they are real descriptions) but are not existent, some concluded they belong to a third category: the 'state' (ḥāl). This lesson unpacks that reasoning and the clarification using the example of a cause and its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the second basis passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second basis. Notice the examples given: existence, bringing-into-existence, omniscience, blackness.
2
Identify why these attributes cannot be existent
The passage says 'proof has established that they are not existent.' For example, the existence of Zayd is not a separate entity; it is the fact that Zayd exists.
3
Understand why they cannot be non-existent
These attributes are truly predicated of existents regardless of mental consideration. If they were non-existent, they could not be predicated.
4
See the solution: the intermediate state (ḥāl)
Scholars concluded that these are neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediate, termed 'state' (ḥāl). This is a technical term in kalām.
5
Reflect on the clarification example
When a cause produces an effect, each has an attribute (causal-efficacy and existence) that was not before. These attributes are not non-existent (since they are now) but also not existent (as separate entities). Thus they are intermediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قلت بطلان ثبوت المعدوم والواسطة في غاية الجلاء، فكيف ذهب على الكثير من العقلاء؟ قلت: كان مبنى الأول: على أن السواد المعدوم مثلا سواد في الخارج لا يتعلق سواديته بأسباب الوجود. والثاني: على أن من الصفات ما قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة ولا سبيل إلى نفيها لاتصاف الموجود بها وجد فرض العقل أو لم يوجد، كالوجود والإيجاد والعالمية واللونية فجزموا بأنها لا موجودة ولا معدومة. لما كان بطلان القول بثبوت المعدوم في الخارج وتحقق الواسطة بينه وبين الموجود جلياً بل ضرورياً، وقد ذهب إليهما سيما إلى تحقق الواسطة العلماء المحققون، حاول التنبيه على ما يصلح مظنة للاشتباه في المقامين. أما الأول فهو أن العقل جازم بأن السواد سواد في الواقع وإن لم توجد أسباب الوجود من الفاعل والقابل، فإن أسباب الماهية غير أسباب الوجود على ما سيجيء. فعبروا عن هذا المعنى بالثبوت في الخارج، لما رأوا فيه من شائبة التقرر والتحقق مع نفيهم الوجود الذهني، وهو قريب من قول الفلاسفة: أن الماهيات ليست بجعل الجاعل. وحاصله: أنهم وجدوا تفرقة بين الممتنعات والمعدومات الممكنة، فإن لها ماهيات تتصف بالوجود تارة وتتعرى عنه أخرى بحسب حصول أسباب الوجود ولا حصولها، فعبروا عن ذلك بالشيئية والثبوت في الخارج. وأما الثاني: فهو أنهم وجدوا بعض ما يتصف به الموجود كوجود الإنسان وإيجاد الله تعالى إياه وعالمية زيد ولونية السواد قد قام الدليل على أنه ليس بموجود، ولم يكن لهم سبيل إلى الحكم بأنه لا تحقق له أصلاً لما رأوا الموجودات تتصف به سواء وجد اعتبار العقل أو لم يوجد، على أنه لو وجد اعتبار العقل وفرضه فهو عندهم ليس بموجود في العقل، فجزموا بأن لهذا النوع من المعاني تحققاً ما في الخارج، وليست بموجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة، وسموه بالحال. توضيحه: أنه إذا صدر المعلول عن العلة، فنحن نجد في كل منهما صفة كانت معدومة قبل الصدور، أعني الموجدية والوجود، فلا تكون حينئذ معدومة، ضرورة التفرقة بين الحالين وقد قام الدليل على أنها ليست بموجودة فتكون واسطة.
He said: If you say the falsity of the subsistence of the non-existent and the intermediate state is extremely clear, so how did it escape many of the intelligent? I say: The basis of the first is that blackness when non-existent, for example, is blackness in external reality, its blackness is not connected to the causes of existence. And the second is that among the attributes there are those for which proof has established that they are not existent, and there is no way to deny them because the existent is described by them whether the supposition of the mind exists or not, such as existence, bringing-into-existence, omniscience, and blackness; so they concluded that they are neither existent nor non-existent. Since the falsity of the statement of the subsistence of the non-existent in external reality and the realization of an intermediate between it and the existent is evident, nay necessary, and yet the investigating scholars have adopted them, especially the realization of the intermediate, he attempted to draw attention to what might be a source of confusion in the two positions. As for the first, it is that the mind judges decisively that blackness is blackness in reality even if the causes of existence from agent and recipient do not exist, for the causes of essence are other than the causes of existence as will come. So they expressed this meaning by subsistence in external reality, because they saw in it a tinge of determination and realization together with their denial of mental existence, and it is close to the statement of the philosophers that essences are not by the making of the maker. Its gist is that they found a distinction between impossible things and possible non-existents: they have essences which are sometimes described by existence and sometimes divested of it according to the occurrence or non-occurrence of the causes of existence, so they expressed that by thingness and subsistence in external reality. As for the second, it is that they found some of what the existent is described by, such as the existence of man, God's bringing him into existence, Zayd's omniscience, and the blackness of blackness, regarding which proof has established that it is not existent, and they had no way to judge that it has no reality at all because they saw that existents are described by it whether the consideration of the mind exists or not, and even if the consideration and supposition of the mind exist, it is in their view not existent in the mind; so they concluded that this type of meanings has a certain realization in external reality, and they are neither existent nor non-existent but intermediate, and they named it the state. Its clarification: when the effect issues from the cause, we find in each of them an attribute that was non-existent before the issuing, namely causal-efficacy and existence; then they are not at that time non-existent, due to the necessity of distinguishing between the two states, and proof has established that they are not existent, so they are intermediate.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector notes that the falsehood of the subsistence of non-existents and of an intermediate state is obvious, so why did many intelligent people believe in them? The answer distinguishes two bases of error. The first basis concerns non-existents: some thought that a non-existent like blackness has a fixed essence (sawād) in reality independent of existence, because the essence's definition does not depend on existence—thus they affirmed a 'subsistence in the external world' (thubūt fī al-khārij). This is akin to the philosophers' claim that essences are not created. The second basis concerns an intermediate state: attributes like existence, God's act of causing existence (ījād), and universal quiddities (e.g., 'human-ness') are not existent in themselves but are not nothing, because existents are described by them even without mental consideration. So they posited a category called 'state' (ḥāl) that is neither existent nor non-existent. The commentator then clarifies each: for the first, the mind still affirms blackness as blackness even without external existence, leading to talk of 'subsistence'; for the second, attributes like causal-efficacy (mūjidiyya) and existence itself in a cause-effect relation are not existent but also not non-existent, hence intermediate. The argument proceeds step by step, showing how both errors arise from subtle philosophical distinctions.

Key Terms

الحال
الوجود
الإيجاد
الموجدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ḥāl is a third type of being (existent) alongside the existent and non-existent.
Correction: The ḥāl is not a being; it is a state of description that has a kind of reality but is not an existent entity. It is a technical term meaning an attribute that is neither existent nor non-existent.

Checkpoint

B) Because they are predicated of existents but are not separate entities
C) Because they are impossible
D) Because they are identical to the existent

Key Takeaway
The intermediate state (ḥāl) was proposed to account for attributes that are real descriptions but not independently existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage speaks of Zayd's omniscience and the blackness of blackness as states. Is your knowledge of yourself a state or a thing? If it is a state, how does that affect your sense of identity and responsibility?
?
The scholars posited an intermediate category to avoid reducing reality to only things or nothing. Can you think of a relationship or quality in your life (like love, intention, or faith) that is real but not a thing? How will you honor that reality without making it into an object?

The Logical Foundation of the Intermediate State: Privation vs. Contradiction
Al-Taftazani explains the logical basis for the intermediate state using the opposition of privation and possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intermediate state between existence and non-existence requires a specific type of opposition—privation and possession—and how this differs from simple contradiction.

Core Idea
The intermediate state exists only if non-existence means privation, not mere absence.

This passage from al-Taftazani unpacks the logical basis for a category of things that are neither existent nor non-existent. The key is how we define the relationship between existence and non-existence. If we treat them as contradictories, then everything must be one or the other. But if we treat them as privation and possession, a middle ground opens up for conceptual entities that cannot have existence. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the three types of opposition mentioned at the start.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify what the proponents avoided
See that they did not use the opposition of non-existence and existence (contradiction) nor negation and affirmation. Instead they used privation and possession.
3
Understand the definition of privation
Privation is defined as the removal of something from a subject that is apt to have it. For example, blindness is the privation of sight in an animal that normally sees.
This definition is crucial for the intermediate category.
4
Connect to conceptual notions
The passage says conceptual notions (like universals, relations) cannot receive existence at all, so under the privation framework they are neither existent nor non-existent.
They lack existence but are not simply non-existent because they are not apt for existence.
5
Note the conditional clause
Al-Taftazani adds that this argument is only valid if the impossible is not considered non-existent. If the impossible were counted as non-existent, the intermediate category would collapse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: وأما ابتناء ذلك على أنهم لم يجعلوا تقابل العدم والوجود، وتقابل السلب والإيجاب، بل العدم والملكة. إذ العدم ارتفاع ما من شأنه الوجود، فجعلوا المفهومات الاعتبارية التي لا يتصور عروض الوجود لها لا موجودة ولا معدومة. فإنما يصح ذلك إذا لم يجعل الممتنع معدوماً.
And he said: As for the basis of that [the intermediate state] being that they did not make the opposition between non-existence and existence and the opposition between negation and affirmation, but rather [they made it] the opposition between privation and possession. For non-existence is the raising of that which is apt for existence. Thus they made the conceptual notions that cannot be conceived to be accidentally characterized by existence neither existent nor non-existent. That is only valid if they do not make the impossible non-existent.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument about the logical foundation of the intermediate state (al-wāsita). The author states that proponents of an intermediate state base it on a specific type of opposition between non-existence and existence. Instead of treating it as the opposition of contradiction (between existence and non-existence) or the opposition of negation and affirmation (salb wa ījāb), they treat it as the opposition of privation and possession ('adam wa malaka). In privation, non-existence is the removal (irtifā') of something from a subject that is apt (sha'nuhu) to possess it. For example, blindness is the privation of sight in a being that normally sees. Under this framework, conceptual notions (mafhūmāt i'tibāriyya) like relations and secondary intelligibles, which cannot possibly be characterized by external existence, are considered neither existent nor non-existent. The author notes that this reasoning is only valid if one does not consider the impossible (mumtani') as simply non-existent. In other words, the intermediate category only makes sense if one restricts non-existence to the privation of something that could exist; impossible things have no share in either category.

Key Terms
تقابل العدم والوجود
تقابل السلب والإيجاب
العدم والملكة
المفهومات الاعتبارية
الممتنع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the intermediate state (wāsita) applies to all non-existent things, including impossible ones like a square circle.
Correction: The passage shows that the intermediate state is only for things that are not apt for existence (like conceptual notions). Impossible things are not considered non-existent in this framework, so they do not fall into the intermediate category. The argument requires that the impossible not be counted as non-existent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of opposition did the proponents of the intermediate state assume between existence and non-existence?
A) Opposition of contradiction
B) Opposition of negation and affirmation
C) Opposition of privation and possession
D) Opposition of identity

Key Takeaway
The intermediate state between existence and non-existence is logically possible only when non-existence is understood as privation—the removal of something from a subject apt to have it—and not as mere contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that your own thoughts and concepts—like your understanding of justice or truth—are neither existent in the external world nor simply non-existent? How does this change the way you value abstract ideas in your daily life?
?
When you struggle with a sin or a shortcoming, do you view it as a mere absence of good (like a privation) or as a positive reality? How might seeing it as a privation—something that ought to be filled with virtue—motivate you to actively seek improvement rather than just feel empty?
?
The passage warns against treating the impossible as non-existent. In your spiritual life, do you ever treat God's commands as 'impossible' to fulfill, thereby excusing your negligence? What would it look like to instead see your failures as a privation of obedience that God empowers you to remedy?

The Distinction of Non-Existents in the Mind
The author discusses the distinction of non-existents in the mind and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existents can be distinguished in the intellect based on their different logical implications.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct in the mind because each has unique logical relationships.

This passage introduces a key debate in Islamic theology: can non-existents be distinguished? The author argues that while they have no external reality, they are clearly distinct in the mind based on their different consequences. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping theological arguments about causation, conditionality, and contraries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author asserts non-existents have distinction in the intellect. This is the central claim.
Pay attention to the phrase 'للاعدام تمايز في العقل'.
2
Study the three examples
The author gives three examples: non-existence of the effect, non-existence of the condition, and non-existence of a contrary. Understand each relationship described.
These examples illustrate how each non-existence has a specific logical role.
3
Grasp the controversy and its resolution
The author distinguishes two meanings of the debate: one trivial (external distinction) and one substantive (mental distinction). He affirms the latter with a proof.
The key is that non-existences have distinct judgments that are true in themselves.
4
Reflect on the reliance on mental existence
The passage ends by linking the distinction to the concept of mental existence. Consider why this connection matters for the broader metaphysical framework.
This sets up the next investigation on mental existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس : تمايز الإعدام في العقل وما يتعلق بذلك (قال) : المبحث الخامس : للاعدام تمايز في العقل كاختصاص عدم المعلول بالاستناد إلى عدم العلة ، وعدم الشرط بمنافاة وجود المشروط، وعدم الضد بتصحيح وجود الآخر). قد اشتهر خلاف في تمايز الإعدام فإن أريد أن ليس التمايز أمراً محققاً في الخارج أو ليست للمعدمات أو المعدومات عينية متمايزة فضروري لا يتصور فيه نزاع، وإن أريد أن ليس لمفهوم العدم أفراد متمايزة عند العقل يختص كل منها بأحكام مخصوصة صادقة في نفس الأمر فباطل ، لأن عدم موجب لعدم المعلول من غير عكس وعدم الشرط مناف لوجود المشروط، وعدم المشروط لا ينافي وجود الشرط وعدم الضد عن المحل يصحح طريان الضد الآخر، بخلاف عدم غير الضد ولما لم يكن التمايز إلا بحسب التعقل، الذي وقع الخلاف في أنه هل هو وجود ذهني أو لا . . . ؟
The fifth investigation: The distinction of non-existents in the intellect and what pertains to that. He said: The fifth investigation: Non-existents have distinction in the intellect, like the exclusivity of the non-existence of the effect to being attributed to the non-existence of the cause, and the non-existence of the condition to being incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the contrary to enabling the occurrence of the other contrary. A controversy has become widespread concerning the distinction of non-existents. For if what is meant is that the distinction is not an actualized matter in the external world, or that non-existents have no distinct identities, then it is necessary and no dispute can be conceived about it. But if what is meant is that the concept of non-existence does not have distinct individuals in the intellect, each of which is specified with particular judgments that are true in themselves, then it is false. Because non-existence of the cause necessitates non-existence of the effect without the reverse, and non-existence of the condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and non-existence of the conditioned does not negate the existence of the condition, and non-existence of a contrary in the substrate enables the occurrence of the other contrary, unlike the non-existence of a non-contrary. And since the distinction is only according to intellection, concerning which controversy occurred whether it is mental existence or not...
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth investigation (al-mabhas al-khamis) on the distinction of non-existents in the mind. The author states that non-existents have distinction in the intellect, giving examples: the non-existence of the effect is exclusively attributed to the non-existence of its cause; the non-existence of the condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned; the non-existence of a contrary enables the occurrence of the other contrary. Then he clarifies the nature of the controversy. If one means that the distinction is not an external reality or that non-existents have no distinct identities in the external world, this is obvious and uncontested. However, if one means that the concept of non-existence does not have distinct individuals in the mind, each with specific true judgments, then this is false. The author provides proof: each non-existence has unique implications—e.g., non-existence of the cause entails non-existence of the effect, but not vice versa; non-existence of the condition contradicts the existence of the conditioned, but non-existence of the conditioned does not contradict the condition; non-existence of a contrary in a substrate enables the occurrence of the other contrary, unlike non-existence of a non-contrary. Thus, non-existents are distinct in the mind based on these differing judgments. The passage ends by noting that the dispute about this distinction relates to whether mental existence is admitted.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all non-existents are completely indistinguishable because they are nothing.
Correction: The passage shows that non-existents are distinct in the mind based on their different logical implications (e.g., lack of cause vs. lack of condition have different consequences).

A) Non-existents are distinct in the external world.
B) Non-existents have no distinction at all.

D) The non-existence of the condition necessitates the non-existence of the effect.

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are distinct in the mind because they have different logical implications, even though they lack external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage shows that even non-existences carry different judgments in the mind. Think of a specific sin you avoided today—its non-existence is distinct from the non-existence of a good deed you failed to do. Are you treating these two non-existences with the same indifference? How will you turn the non-existence of sin into a stepping stone to a good deed?
?
If every non-existence in the mind has a unique meaning and implication, then nothing—not even a past failure—is meaningless in Allah's knowledge. What failure have you been dismissing as 'just a nothing' that you now need to reconsider as a distinct part of your story? What will you do differently tomorrow because of this?

Distinction of Non-Existents in the Intellect: Definition and Examples
Defines and exemplifies distinction of non-existents in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existents can be distinguished in the intellect based on their logical relations, and why this distinction is not about external reality.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct in the mind through their logical roles, not externally.

This lesson introduces a key concept in Islamic theology and logic: the distinction of non-existents in the intellect. While non-existents have no external reality, they can be clearly distinguished in the mind based on their causal and conditional relationships. The passage provides three examples and clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the examples given.
The passage uses technical terms like 'عدم المعلول' and 'عدم الشرط'.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three types of non-existents: non-existence of the effect, non-existence of the condition, and non-existence of the contrary.
Each has a specific logical relationship.
3
Understand the distinction between external and conceptual
The passage distinguishes between external distinction (impossible for non-existents) and conceptual distinction in the intellect ( possible).
This resolves an apparent disagreement.
4
Connect to mental existence debate
The passage ends by linking this distinction to the broader debate on whether mental existence is real.
This is a key issue in Islamic philosophy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
تمايز الإعدام في العقل وما يتعلق بذلك
(قال): المبحث الخامس : للاعدام تمايز في العقل كاختصاص عدم المعلول بالاستناد إلى عدم العلة، وعدم الشرط بمنافاة وجود المشروط، وعدم الضد بتصحيح وجود الآخر.
قد اشتهر خلاف في تمايز الإعدام فإن أريد أن ليس التمايز أمراً محققاً في الخارج أو ليست للمعدمات أو المعدومات عينية متمايزة فضروري لا يتصور فيه نزاع، وإن أريد أن ليس لمفهوم العدم أفراد متمايزة عند العقل يختص كل منها بأحكام مخصوصة صادقة في نفس الأمر فباطل، لأن عدم موجب لعدم المعلول من غير عكس وعدم الشرط مناف لوجود المشروط، وعدم المشروط لا ينافي وجود الشرط وعدم الضد عن المحل يصحح طريان الضد الآخر، بخلاف عدم غير الضد ولما لم يكن التمايز إلا بحسب التعقل، الذي وقع الخلاف في أنه هل هو وجود ذهني أو لا . . ؟
Fifth Section. Distinction of Non-Existents in the Intellect and What Pertains to That. He said: Fifth Section: Non-existents have distinction in the intellect, such as the specific attribution of the non-existence of the effect to the non-existence of the cause, and the non-existence of the condition being incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the contrary enabling the existence of the other contrary. There has been a well-known disagreement concerning the distinction of non-existents. If it is intended that distinction is not a realized matter in the external world or that non-existents or non-existent things do not have distinct essences, then it is necessary and no dispute can be conceived about it. But if it is intended that the concept of non-existence has no distinct individuals in the intellect each characterized by specific judgments true in themselves, then it is false. Because the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect without the reverse, and the non-existence of the condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the conditioned does not negate the existence of the condition, and the non-existence of the contrary in the subject makes possible the occurrence of the other contrary, unlike the non-existence of what is not a contrary. And since distinction is only according to intellection, about which disagreement has occurred as to whether it is mental existence or not.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth section by defining the doctrine that non-existents are distinct in the intellect. It first quotes the matn, which gives three examples: (1) the non-existence of the effect is specifically linked to the non-existence of its cause, (2) the non-existence of a condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and (3) the non-existence of a contrary allows the other contrary to occur. Then the commentary explains the disagreement: if one means external distinction, it is obvious that non-existents have no external reality, so no dispute; but if one means conceptual distinction in the intellect, it is true because each non-existence has unique logical relations (e.g., the non-existence of the cause implies the non-existence of the effect, but not vice versa). The passage concludes by linking this distinction to the broader debate on mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The argument flows from accepting that distinction exists in the mind to asking whether that constitutes a kind of existence.

Key Terms
تمايز الإعدام
الوجود الذهني
عدم المعلول
عدم الشرط
عدم الضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existents are completely indistinguishable and have no differences whatsoever.
Correction: While non-existents have no external reality, they are conceptually distinct in the intellect based on their different logical relations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the non-existence of the effect is specifically linked to what?
A) Non-existence of the condition
B) Non-existence of the cause
C) Non-existence of the contrary
D) Non-existence of the subject

Key Takeaway
Non-existents are distinct in the intellect based on their unique logical relations, and this distinction is conceptual, not external.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you reflect on your own life, the 'non-existence of good deeds' in certain areas is not just a void—it carries specific consequences. Does your mind treat that absence as a mere concept, or do you recognize its logical necessity leading to punishment? What one omission will you actively work to eliminate this week?
?
The lesson shows that the non-existence of a condition is incompatible with the existence of its conditioned. In your relationship with Allah, what condition—like sincere repentance—is missing in your life, preventing a desired blessing? What concrete step will you take to remove that absence?
?
Non-existents are distinct in the intellect, meaning you are accountable not only for your actions but also for your omissions. Name a specific 'non-existence' in your obedience that you have been ignoring. How does this lesson change your sense of responsibility toward filling that void?

The Correct Relation Between Mental Existence and Distinction of Non-Existents
Al-Taftazani corrects al-Isfahani on the subordination of the two debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct relationship between the debate on mental existence and the distinction of non-existents, and how al-Taftazani corrects al-Isfahani's view.

Core Idea
Affirming mental existence enables distinction of non-existents; denying it prevents distinction.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a subtle connection between two famous kalam debates: the distinction of non-existents and mental existence. Al-Isfahani claimed that affirming mental existence leads to denying the distinction of non-existents. But al-Taftazani shows that the truth is the opposite. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a reported view, then a correction, then the reasoning.
2
Understand al-Isfahani's claim
Identify what the author of al-Mawaqif held: that the disagreement on distinction of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement on mental existence. Note his claimed correlation: affirmers of mental existence deny distinction, deniers affirm it.
This is the view that al-Taftazani will correct.
3
See how al-Taftazani corrects it
The text then says 'the matter is the opposite'. Al-Taftazani points out that the philosophers who affirm mental existence actually affirm the distinction, while the mutakallimun who deny mental existence deny distinction.
This is the key correction.
4
Grasp the reasoning
The reason given: distinction is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the described. Mental existence provides that positive existence for non-existents as concepts, enabling distinction. Without any existence, no distinction.
This shows why the correct subordination is the opposite of what al-Isfahani claimed.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters: it clarifies the positions of philosophers and mutakallimun on related issues and demonstrates how subtle distinctions in metaphysics have concrete effects on theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب صاحب المواقف إلى أن الخلاف في تمايز الإعدام فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني، فمن أثبته نفاه لأن التمايز لا يكون إلا في العقل، أي بحسب التعقل والتصور فإن كان ذلك بوجود في الذهن، على ما هو رأي المثبتين لم يتصور معدوم مطلقاً. أي معدوم ليس له شائبة الوجود، لأن كل وجود ذهني، فلا يكون التمايز إلا للموجودات، ومن نفاه أثبته . لأن الإعدام ليست لها شائبة الوجود متمايزة في التصور، وأنت خبير بأن الأمر بالعكس. لأن الفلاسفة المثبتين للوجود الذهني يقولون : بتمايز الإعدام وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له هم القائلون بعدم تمايزها، فالأولى أن يقال في بيان التفرع أنه لما كان التميز عندهم وصفاً ثبوتياً، يستدعي ثبوت الموصوف به فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الإعدام عند تصورها لما لها من الثبوت الذهني، وإن كانت هي إعداماً في أنفسها، ومن نفاه حكم بعدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً.
The author of al-Mawaqif held that the disagreement on the distinction of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement on mental existence. Whoever affirms mental existence denies the distinction, because distinction can only be in the intellect, i.e., according to intellection and conception. If that is by an existence in the mind, according to the view of the affirmers, no non-existent can be conceived absolutely, i.e., a non-existent that has no trace of existence, because every [conception] is a mental existence. So distinction applies only to existents. And whoever denies mental existence affirms the distinction, because non-existents have no trace of existence yet are distinct in conception. And you are aware that the matter is the opposite. For the philosophers who affirm mental existence say: non-existents are distinct; and the majority of the mutakallimun who deny it are the ones who say they are not distinct. So it is more appropriate to say in explaining the branching: Since distinction in their view is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the described, so whoever affirms mental existence judges that non-existents are distinct when they are conceived, because they have mental existence, even though they are non-existents in themselves. And whoever denies mental existence judges that they are not distinct due to the lack of any existence at all.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage examines the connection between the debate on the distinction of non-existents and the controversy over mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). It first reports the view of al-Isfahani (sahib al-Mawaqif) that the former is subordinate to the latter: mental existence affirmers deny distinction, and deniers affirm it. But the author (al-Taftazani) corrects this, stating that the truth is the opposite: the philosophers (falasifa) who affirm mental existence also affirm the distinction of non-existents, while the majority of mutakallimun who deny mental existence deny distinction. The reason is that distinction is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the thing described; mental existence provides that positive existence for non-existents as concepts, enabling their distinction. Without any existence, there is no basis for distinction. Thus the correct subordination is: affirmation of mental existence leads to affirmation of distinction of non-existents, and denial of mental existence leads to denial of their distinction.

Key Terms
صاحب المواقف
الوجود الذهني
تمايز الإعدام
الفلاسفة
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming mental existence necessarily leads to denying the distinction of non-existents (as claimed by al-Isfahani).
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the opposite is true: affirming mental existence actually leads to affirming the distinction, because mental existence provides the positive existence needed for distinction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who actually affirms the distinction of non-existents?
A) Philosophers affirming mental existence
B) Mutakallimun denying mental existence
C) The author of al-Mawaqif
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The distinction of non-existents is based on their mental existence; those who affirm mental existence affirm their distinction, and those who deny mental existence deny their distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in the Last Day, Paradise, and Hell, though you have never seen them. Are they real to you only as mental concepts, or do they have a reality that you are certain of? If they are merely mental, how does that affect your urgency to prepare for them? If they are real, what kind of reality is that, and does it move you to action today?
?
When you think about the attributes of Allah, do you treat them as real descriptions that are distinct from each other, or do you blur them together because they are 'just' mental? This lesson shows that mental existence allows for real distinction. If you deny mental existence or treat it as merely imaginary, how can you confidently distinguish between Allah's mercy and His justice? How can you worship a God whose attributes are not truly distinct?
?
Consider the greatest fear you have about something that does not exist yet—like a future failure or sin. That thing is non-existent now, but you distinguish it from other non-existent possibilities. On what basis do you distinguish them? If you say 'in my mind', then you are affirming mental existence. Does that change how seriously you take your thoughts? What if your mental distinctions are not just games but have a kind of reality that affects your soul?

Twofold Consideration of Non-Existence and the Objection on Judgment
Addresses an objection about judging non-existence and the twofold consideration of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam scholar resolves the apparent contradiction of judging non-existence by distinguishing two aspects of consideration.

Core Idea
Judging non-existence is not a contradiction—it depends on which aspect you consider.

This passage tackles a subtle logical puzzle: if non-existence is nothing, how can we say anything about it? The author first explains that non-existence can be considered in two ways—as a conceived concept and as absent reality. Then he addresses the objection that any judgment about non-existence would be self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two considerations
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to grasp the twofold nature of non-existence. Look for the key terms that signal the two aspects.
Notice the phrase 'باعتبار' which indicates each consideration.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that it is impossible to judge non-existence because it is not established, yet the very act of judging seems to affirm its existence as a subject.
This is a classic logical paradox.
3
Study the answer: two aspects prevent contradiction
The answer says that from the aspect where non-existence is established as a mental concept, judgment is valid; from the aspect where it is absent, judgment is impossible. The two considerations differ, so there is no contradiction.
Contradiction occurs only when the same aspect is used.
4
Apply the reasoning to similar cases
The same reasoning applies to the absolute unknown and the unimaginable—they can be judged as concepts even though they are not real objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال: والعدم قد يعرض لنفسه، بأن يعقل فيغفل عنه فيكون نوعاً من العدم باعتبار ومقابلا له باعتبار كما أن المعدوم المطلق ثابت باعتبار فيصح الحكم عليه وقسيم له باعتبار فيمتنع.
فإن قيل : فمن حيث إنه ليس بثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه وهذا حكم .
قلنا : نعم. لكن من حيث أنه ثابت ولا يناقض لاختلاف الاعتبارين، وكذا الحكم بامتناع الحكم على المجهول المطلق واللاممكن التصور .
He said: Non-existence may occur to itself, such that it is conceived and then overlooked; so it is a type of non-existence from one consideration and its opposite from another consideration. Similarly, absolute non-existence is established from one consideration so judgment about it is valid, and it is its opposite from another consideration so it is impossible. For it is said: Insofar as it is not established, judgment about it is impossible, yet this is a judgment. We say: Yes. But insofar as it is established, and there is no contradiction due to the difference of the two considerations. Similarly, the judgment that it is impossible to judge about the absolute unknown and the unimaginable.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents a matn and an objection-answer pair. The matn states that non-existence can be considered in two ways: as something conceived (hence a type of non-existence) and as something overlooked (hence opposite). Likewise, absolute non-existence is established from one aspect (as a concept) so judgment is valid, but from another aspect it is non-existent so judgment is impossible. The objection notes a paradox: if it is not established, judgment is impossible, yet we just made a judgment about it. The answer resolves this by distinguishing two considerations: from the aspect where it is established (as a mental concept), judgment is valid; from the aspect where it is not established, judgment is impossible. There is no contradiction because the considerations differ. The same applies to judgments about the absolute unknown and the unimaginable: from the conceptual aspect, we can judge that they cannot be judged, but from the aspect of their own reality, no judgment applies.

Key Terms
العدم
المعدوم المطلق
المجهول المطلق
اللاممكن التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any judgment about non-existence is contradictory.
Correction: Judgment is possible from the aspect of its conceptual establishment, while impossible from the aspect of its real absence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is judgment about non-existence possible?
A) By ignoring the contradiction
B) By considering it from the aspect of its establishment as a concept
C) By denying mental existence
D) By affirming mental existence

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can be judged in kalam by considering it as a mental concept, not as an absent reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation you deem hopeless or 'non-existent' in your life (e.g., a broken relationship or a lost opportunity), do you consider only its absence, or do you also reflect on it as a concept you can still mentally engage with? How might the twofold consideration change your perspective and actions today?
?
The objection in the passage mirrors an inner voice that says, 'If this is impossible, why am I even thinking about it?' Think of a time when you judged something as impossible yet kept judging it—what would it mean for you to acknowledge that you are judging it from one aspect while it remains absent from another? How can you act on the aspect where judgment is valid?
?
The passage says non-existence can be 'conceived and then overlooked.' What 'non-existence' in your faith—such as a sin you committed and then ignored—have you overlooked? How can you bring it into the light of judgment by considering it as a mental reality (i.e., acknowledging it) without getting stuck in the aspect of its absence (i.e., despair)?
